The Red Page
Anzallan
Maud Renner Liston reprints from South
Australian journals a number of what she
calls poems—and some of them do almost
justify that label. 8. George for England,
and U.P. (Heath, Cranton, London, 3s. (id.),
is a readable structure of neat verse. She
writes of the Empire—a couplet for each
Dominion, and the Allies—a verse for each,
not forgetting Montenegro; and her touch
in “Geography” is light but attractive :
Long years ago, as memory tells,
We learned about the Dardanelles ;
And wrote the name, like rules and dates,
With squeaking pencils on our slates.
How could we know it ever should
Be writ for us in signs of blood ?. . . .
We heard about the plains of Troy,
And to each listening girl or boy
It seemed a stirring tale to be
But wrapped in dim antiquity.
How could we see this classic place
Wet with the life-blood of our race?
And now >vc learn, in pride and rue,
Names on a map we never knew ;
And Gaba Tepe, to all years,
Tells of our glory and'our tears,
For that far hill where fell our best
Means more to us than all the rest.
There is. too, a fine marching lilt in “Sing-
ing through old Boulogne.” One or two
interesting sonnets, including an excellent
one on Blake ; a number of devout religious
verses; a few vivid glimpses of the woman’s
part in war—the whole technically skilled
but often prosaic in thought and expression,
make up a volume that calls for encourage-
ment but hardly criticism. “The Last
Night” sounds pitifully real: —
I said: “Dear lads, to-night you'll stay,
Before the dawn I’ll send you away.”
I tucked them in and kissed good night;
They nnist be gone before the light.
I piled the fire with mallee roots
To dry their raiicsoaked clothes and boots ;
And lingered over the things with care,
So glad to know that they were there.
And then to bed —but not to sleep,
Later these aching eyes may weep.
Ever inseparable these two,
What one did must the other do.
It was always so in every game,
And now, in war, it is just the same.
■One could not go without the other,
Each would be lost without his brother.
Five says the clock by the flickering lamp,
Time for reveille at the camp.
They slept as sweet as babies then
Who must go forth to fight like men.
I bent above each curb head,
And “Time to wake” I somehow said.
The taxi purred at the gate, the rain
Beat heavily on the window-pane.
“Good-bye* keep smiling to the last,
Good-bye, good-bye,” and that is past.
And what is left for me but sit
In the quiet house, and knit and knit?
d*
In After Many Days (Sydney: John
Andrew and Co.) Outhbert Fetlierstonliaugh
has written the story of his life, and part of
the story of Australia, up to 1872. If it
meets with a welcome, he promises to bring
it up to date. Well, he had better get to
work, for the book is sure of warm apprecia-
tion on the part of every good Australian
who reads it. Old boys and young boys will
revel in these racy reminiscences of a genu-
ine “sport,” a pioneer and a brave and good
man, who has ridden straight, lived straight
and written straight. He rode with Adam
Lindsay Gordon, helped in the opening up of
North Queensland and the development of
Southern Riverina, and fought blacks anc
bushrangers, drought and financial disastei
with equal courage and good humor. He is
the same Fetherstonhaugh of whom poor
Barcroft Boake wrote just before he died
by his own hand. The verses were found
on his body, with a note directing that they
should be handed to J. F. Archibald, of
The Bulletin, and they were first pub-
lished in this paper in 1892. There is a
vivid description of Mr. Fetherstonhaugh's
dealings with “Blue Cap’s” gang—the
theme of Boake’s ballad—in the present
volume.
The book is full of good things, told with
the Celtic wit which is part of the author’s
heritage from ancestors whose vitality was
extraordinary. His father was tlm 27tli
child o f be mother of 28 children,
w b“ ’.vtu married at 16 and lived to
he 75. He was 90 when he died, and his
son, the author, is in his 81st year
and still hale and well —though he has, in
his time, sustained 18 fractures and five dis-
locations, as a result of his consistent hard
riding! But apart from its qualities of
entertainment, After Many Days has real
value to the student of Australian history—
a value of the sort that is possessed by such
books as Joseph Holt’s Memoirs, the late
T. A. Browne’s (Rolfe Boldrewood) Old
Melbourne Memories, J. T. Ryan’s Reminis-
cenes, and the recent volume by R. D.
Barton. It is from such books as these
that living portraits of the pioneers and their
times may be drawn with fidelity. They
are invaluable, and the present happy chron-
icle adds worthily to their number. Should
its sequel come to publication, Mr. Fether-
stonliaugh will have added very materially
and usefully to the good__work he has accom-
plished in this pleasant volume, and it will
be sure of a wide circle of readers. After
Many Days is altogether a charming book,
characteristic of the man who has written
it, whose name is a household word in
Bushland from Portland Bay to Rockhamp-
ton.
The M.L. public having eaten up the
whole of the 500 copies of Wellington horses
within two months, Boyce Bowden hands
out a second helping, Roads and Fairies
(Whitcombe and Tombs. M.L. per Angus
and Robertson, Sydney). Like his first
plateful these verses are reprinted from
The Bulletin. They are just as filling,
but hardly so appetising. Boyce Bowden
has a fatal facility in verse-writing; and this
collection shows, alack, more facility and
less fertility of imagination arid sentiment.
And the “place” poem that gave his first
volume a niche by itself on the shelf marked
“Wellington,” is missing in the second.
These are pleasant, and agreeable verses
about roads and fairies and cities and the
sea ; but they largely lack the local touch,
and thereby lose their individuality. Yet
there are observation and freshness in this:
He best can sing the song of Love for whom Love
holds no' part ;
It is an honest song which swells from out a yearning
heart;
And he can hymn the breadth of Life to whom Life
is a name,
And panting fans a waning spark within a wasting
frame.
O baby toes and rosy heels which rest, within the
palm!
He, childless, sires his babe of dreams and shrines it
in a psalm,
And from the depth of Heart’s desire the cry rings
true and strong,
For each man tames liis darling Hope and weaves it
into song.
But this fondness for loosely-knit long
lines is soul-laziness. Boyce Bowden should
practise his scales —say, a sonnet or lyric
every morning before breakfast. His fairies
are more companionable than his rambling
roads. “The Fern Men” begins charmingly,
but is too long for complete quotation:—
An’ you set the new moon in the crotch of a tree
An’ you bide when the day’s at the turn
On the twist o’ the little grey track you will see
The little brown men in the fern;
But your eyes must be kind in the front o’ your head
And your heart must be soft for to ken ;
And ’tis hush you must keep and ’tis soft you must
tread
If you’d meet with the little brown men.
You must pluck from your ears all o’ bad they have
heard,
You must brush up your coat or your gown,
For the little brown men will take fright at a word,
And they hate the drab dust of the town.
You must spread out your sins on the lap o’ the
Night
If the brown, nimble men you would see,
For your soul must be clean and your heart must be
white
As the moon in the crotch o’ the tree.
If you do all these things and you tarry a 'mace
And you never give cause for alarm,
You will find a big blossom flung full at yr. r face
And feel a wee fist on your arm ;
While over the twigs like the steps of , the Dreeze
From the fronds of the fern and the roors,
The tiny brown men will swarm up on your knees
Or squat on the ground by your b00t5....
Yes, pretty and pleasant, but this page
misses the little brown hat. There are no
little brown hats in Roads and Fairies. A
sonnet and a lyric to be written every morn-
ing before breakfast, Boyce Bowden!
At last, a troopship magazine that bars
humor. The editors of The Billjim, pub-
lished on a returning troopship, announce
that “nothing approaching a joke can appear
in this paper.” This decision is perhaps
due to the susceptibilities of troopship skip-
pers, who believe that the old jokes dished
up voyage after voyage bring them ill-luck
and attract submarines. And the serious-
ness of this paper “for Billjims, edited by
some of them, read by all of them, damned
by a few of them, ignored by none of them,”
is plainly a relief. The editors proffer a
warning against the shirkers, “who by their
ignoble cuteness” have had no part in the
making of Billjim’s good name. “Suspect
those men with grievances. You, who have
been through the futile hell of a Pozieres, a
Bullecourt, a Passchendale —to your eternal
credit be it said you parade not your griev-
ance. Then suspect the history of those
men who agitate so glibly.” There is an
open letter to parents whose sons are return-
ing without a decoration. “This is a war
in which the individual is lost, and is found
again only by blind chance. That blind
chance has not yet found out and particu-
larised your son is no reproach to him. lor
himself, he may casually tell you that he has
been in many a tight corner where he was
content to get out ‘with a whole skin and a
darned big fright.’ But we have seen him
in such corners and know he came out per-
haps without a decoration, but with the
clear consciousness of having done his job.
An open letter to the Australian public
warns us that “some Billjims would come
back apparently less amiable in character,
certainly less ingenuous in mind. . . .All that
is immoral in civilisation... .was before
their eyes. They have come through _ nerve-
racking and unbalancing experiences. Our
duty is to nurse those Billjims back to men-
tal and moral health, to "protect the good
name of the returned soldiers from the hys-
terical and unintelligent criticism of cant-
ing wowsers,” and to protect these sick boys
from the evils of our own cities. Evidently
attempts were made by contributors to evade
the notice, “No jokes accepted” ; but luckily
the editors possessed something more drastic
than a w.p.b. Most of these efforts, we
learn in “Answers to Correspondents,
found their way to the incinerator. Worth
quoting is a typical reply :
Well, yours is no joke, but something long on
issue as an army ration. Was first heard in
Broadmcadows Camp at beginning of the war, printed
in a troopship paper published the day the Lmdeli
was sunk, afterwards much enjoyed by some 2nd Div.
reinforcements, told at a regimental dinner in the 4th
Div. (that poor, fellow lost his commission over it),
chalked on u dug-out in sth Div. lines at, Fleurbaix
(fortunately matter hushed up and never reached
Corps), finally brought over by 3rd Div. and
put on the market as a new-laid egg. Giving it
again only that you may bring it home to the Yarra
and drown it: “Kaiser: ‘Who are these Australians.
Crown Prince: ‘Dunno! But 11 of them beat Eng-
land, and there are 40,000 of them coming.’ ’
je
The Balmoral Boomerang is a reptile
contemporary of The Billjim, both being
published on the same Castle liner. One o *-
tlie poems is signed with the familiar Aus-
tralian initials W.H.0.; but even if Ogilvie
isn’t on board it is worth quotation:—
If a crowd collects at Charing Cross
And a horse is down in the Strand,
The Turned-up Hat will act as boss,
While his comrades lend a hand.
If a taxi-driver be impolite,
And a lady be left in tears,
The Turned-up Hat will set things right,
With a rousing box on the ears.
If a country child, with heart half-broke,
Is lost in the London streets,
The Turned-up Hat will find its folk
And smother its sobs with sweets.
In short, if there’s a bully to beat,
Or a wrong to be ridden at,
0/ anything fallen to put on its feet,
You may count on the Turned-up Hat.
The Cacolet, the journal of the Aus-
tralian Camel Field Ambulance, printed in
Cairo, is quite a lordly magazine, well illus-
trated with photographs and full of informa-
tion. “Cacolet,” we learn, is the “camel” for
“stretcher.” One effect of Billjim’s presence
in Egypt is to give a little local color to
ancient history: a guide at the Pyramids
remarked to an A.C.F.A. man: “The
Sphinkus was built 5000 years ago, by
Napoleon the Great, Australian Prime Mini-
ster.” The Cacolet has its bards, its humor-
ists and its answers to correspondents ; but
this small chunk of sincerity pleases most of
all: “They’re game, every one of them, the
wounded Australians —and there is never a
grumble when they come in all shot to pieces
....some of them swear, some say nothing
at all, and most of them seem to think that
they are being far too much trouble to
anyone who tries to help them. In reality,
too much cannot be done for them. They
have been fighting or lying wounded in the
sun, have probably 20 miles to travel in a
bumpy sand-cart or a murderously-swinging
camel cacolet, and 100 miles by train over a
none too even railway, before they get any
kind of decent comfort In the field make
sure that they are comfortable, but don’t
fuss over them. Let them help themselves
as much as they can.... Above all, keep
cheerful- —even if the place is crowded with
wounded and they are still coming—and you
are dead-beat, there is no transport avail-
able, and some fool says that we are losing
the war.”

11. Y. Edwards, a regular “Abo.” con-
tributor, tells, in Wild-Life Photography, the
best way to obtain snapshots of our fauna.
His booklet (Gordon and Gotch, 25.) is
illustrated by his own photographs; and his
yarns and hints are both interesting, especi-
ally his account of the exciting sport of
photographing snakes.
The Gum Tree, a quarterly devoted to
the conservation of Australian trees, the
official organ of the Forest League (Melb.,
Is.), celebrates its first anniversary. It is
making vigorous growth.

Fiction
Twinkletoes, a Tale of Chinatown
(Richards, per Dymock’s Library), will be
read. For it is by Thomas Burke, the author
of Limehouse Nights, that remarkable
achievement in the three realms of horror,
sordid realism and poetry. But Burke’s
genius is at home in the short-story, where
he can fuse his melodrama into beauty by
the white heat of his style. This novel is,
however, merely a long short-story. It does
not exceed 40,000 words; and Burke's effort
to stay that distance is occasionally obvious.
But the story of this charming girl of Lou-
don's Chinatown, a delightful child dancer
at a music-hall in the dockyard slums, is
engrossing and entrancing—if only Thomas
Burke wouldn’t remind us of his earlier
masterpiece. At 13 years of age—better to
call it 13 years of happiness—Twinkletoes
was the leader of the kiddie dancers at the
Quayside music-hall. And Chuck Lightfoot,
20 and married, loved her—loved her
fiercely hut reverently. And Burke makes us
see her as worth being loved: “Wailing
towards Chinatown was a child as lovely and
as insolently happy as a lyric. Torrents of
bright ctuds foamed about her shoulders, and
the black silk frock clung to her young
beauty as though it loved her. The mirror-
like candor of her face, undimmed by any
breath of the world’s abominations, reflected
nothing hut the serene joy of the moment.
Remote seemed her glory from that mephitic
chaos. Timid as a wraith that may melt at
a touch, she seemed too fragile even for child-
hood ; and the mind shrank from the thought
that the deflowering hand of man should rest
upon this phantom of a dream.”
The cautious reader need not fear that
Burke is going to inflict upon him a slum
replica of the nauseating angel-child of
American fiction. “She had a low flash-
point of temper; was explosively good-
natured, explosively combative; and cruelty
made her sick. It seemed to her so silly that
people should disturb a beautiful world.”
Twinkletoes led the troupe of “The Quay-
side Kids,” who sang and danced at The
Quayside. “And a brave picture they made,
those groups of glorious girls, touched with
all the charms of their little years, with
Twinkletoes in the centre, her young throat
quivering like a singing bird’s....At 15 she
knew all those terrible things that philoso-
phers achieve when they come home by
Weeping Cross. . . . She taught her audience,
without teaching, to look tenderly upon
everything.. . . She made sweet and desir-
able* all things that finer people called Vul-
gar.... She taught them to live boldly and
gladly, making beauty where no beauty w T as,
and her shining creed of ‘Ain’t people
and things lovely?’ lit the monotonous
hours wherever she carried it.” The
gallery boys loved her “since the night
of the alarm of fire, when she had
averted a panic by dancing on before a
front cloth and distributing her garters and
ribbons to the stalls, while chi-iking the gal-
lery with ribald comment and richly im-
proper gesture.”
It is in London’s yellow streak—Lime-
house Causeway and Penny fields —that the
tragedy of Twinkletoes is played out. And
the story moves against the sort of back-
ground that Burke knows so well how to
suggest: “Color and movement arrive; the
adventurous uncertainty of shadow ; mists;
and unapproachable windows of many tones
gleaming inscrutable messages. The sini-
ster clashes with the silver-pensive. Always
there are -following feet: the firm feet of the
constable; the bullying boots of the tough ;
the thick steps that trickle from the bars;
and, through all, the whisper of slippers and
the rustling vocabularies of the Pacific.”
But Twinkletoes’ belief in a lovely world
filled with lovely people is founded on illu-
sion. One night at the music-hall she learns
the news that her father is a forger; and
with her fervent belief in his goodness so
sharply shattered, the kid breaks loose to
drown'her grief. She joins the crowd at the
Blue Lantern, gets drunk on cheap wine, then
smokes a nipe of opium. The sight of opium-
smokers was no novelty to this too-wise
child ; but this was her first use of the drug.
She is taken home drunk by the manager of
the music-hall, also drunk ; but it is to his
home that he takes her. ...
Her world crashes about her. A murder and
the arrests of all the persons of the drama
leave the disillusioned child desperate. A
pathetic love-scene between Chuck and the
girl leads to the tragic end. “All her beauty,
her high character, her gladly-nourished
ideals, had gone to dust; and in the going
carried disaster to those she most loved.”
The inevitable end to the brief romance is
her suicide.
A story sordid and strangely beautiful, a
piece of nauseous melodrama made poetry, a
tale of realism and pathos, Twinkletoes will
be eagerly read.
Impatient correspondents might note that the
Photo. Play Competition has not been judged yet,
and that the award will be first announced on this
page. Also that Tamworth has not yet chosen its
inscription for its honor-roll; and the result will also
be announced here.
Correspondents state that Yanpoo, the title of a
troopship paper, is aboriginal for Messenger. The
Yaxdoo was first issued en route to England, and it
is the organ of the 7th Field Artillery Brigade.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The New Graves.
Here among the new graves none may see
Marigolds, or moss-buds modest on their
stalks;
Marjoram and lavender and rosemary
Spill no ancient flavor upon old pavfed
walks.
Here no breezes harping in high pines,
Here no bird sings *in a purple liac bower,
Here no painted butterfly in sunlight shines,
Here no bee takes homeward plunder of a
flower.
Here no rose-bush reddens, no lily blooms;
Only brown earth glistens and carved
crosses grey ;
Yet within this garden, green among the
tombs,
Flourishes eternally Fame’s immortal bay.
SCOTLAND.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
Nights of Long Ago.
We may travel fast and far,
We may follow gleam and glow—•
Glow of worm and gleam of star —
But at last we’ll come to know.
When the gathering glooms appal,
That the best guide of all
Was the window-lamp that cheered us in
the nights of long ago.
We may seek some passion-fire,
Warm of heart, and veins aglow
With the flame of red desire.
But at last we’ll come to know,
When the snows of winter fall,
That the best warmth of all
Was the hearthfire that we knelt by in the
nights of long ago.
We may love a score of times,
Finding love not fashioned so
As it seems in poets’ rhymes,
But at last we’ll come to know —
Yea, to know beyond recall —
That the best, Jove of all
Was the golden' girl we danced with in the
nights of long ago.
We may make a god of wealth,
We may cherish it as though
It were life and strength and health,
But at last we’ll come to know,
While the golden fetters gall,
That the best prize of all
Was the happy, careless mood of the nights
of long ago.
Though we travel fast and far,
Though we follow glqam or glow—
Glow of worm or gleam of star —
We shall come at last to know,
Be it great or be it small,
That the best goal of all
Was the old, old home in the nights of long
ago.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Builder.
You built an edifice with steady hand.
With mind and brain perfecting all you
planned.
So sweet the World’s loud voices to your ear,
That Love’s soft whisper you may never hear.
Yet once you loved me for a little day.
So I, within myself, for comfort say:
“This be your recompense for all the smart —
The corner stone he built on was your heart.”
QUEENSLAND.
EDITH SHIRLEY.
The Gift.
’Tis little that I can give to you who have
brought so much to me.
The chant of the stars, the moon’s still
laugh and the song of the wind-blown
sea
You have brought to me and taught to me,
and what can I give at all?
A little hour of dusky quiet that you may
hear the silence call.
You take my hand and lead me away through
the gates of Night and Morn,
And I hear the panting moan of Space when
a new white star is born.
You have drawn it near and shown it clear,
and here's for your soul’s delight
A little hour of healing quiet that you may
read your word aright.
I walk by your side heart-deep in bloom
through the fragrant fields of dream
And pluck white blossoms of tranquil
thought like stars in the dusk agleam.
You have reaped for me, you have heaped
for me, the harvest of all the past;
And here’s an hour of loving quiet that you
may sing your song at last.
N.S.W.
MARGARET FANE.
In A.D. 1918.
Blue bells, blue bells.
And the green hill under them,
Blue bells, blue bells
Dancing in the wind ;
Brown bees, brown bees.
All agog to plunder them....
What’s the use o’ bluebells when the world’s
gone blind?
Lights, lights, lights, lights,
And the warm city under them,
Home-lights, hearth-lights
Singing to the sky;
Blood-greed, man-greed,
Hell aleap to sunder them....
What’s the use o’ taper lights when God’s
lamps die?
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.

THURSDAY, APRIL 18, 1918.
GOING SLOW.
“O Cromwell, Cromwell, I charge you to put
away ambition. By it fell the angels. They
declared no trumps on an insufficient hand, and the
flood came and carried them all away.” —From
Solomon Malaprop’s revised and corrected version
of the- Bard of Stratford.
A feature of Australian trades unionism is
that it has to a great extent put away ambi-
tion—the profitable business-like form of
ambition. If there has been, at any time in
the last 10 years, a strenuous resolution
carried at a Labor conference, or a good
hard plank put in a Labor platform, about
getting a lot more work done even if there
was a lot more money attached to it, the
fact has escaped attention. If there has been
anything violent said about making the Com-
monwealth less abjectly dependent on the
skilled foreign artisan, colored or otherwise,
unionist or otherwise, and on the foreign
capitalist, hostile or otherwise, it hasn’t been
noticed. There has been a great deal of
Going Slow, and not much of anything else
that matters, though there have been plati-
tudes, and speeches, and resolutions passed
by a greater or less majority, and meetings
at which some people sang the .National
Anthem and some refused to sing it. The
trouble arises, to a great extent, out of the
idea that there is only so much work, and
that it must be spread out thin else it won’t
go round, while at the same time there is a
constant battle for more wages (even if
there isn’t more work) by reason of the in-
creased cost of living. And at the bottom of
all this, strange as it may seem, is old Tory
tradition.
* * * *
The old Tory “knew not what he did,” but
he certainly did it. The great basic myth,
still flourishing though not in quite such
good repair as aforetime, is that i commerce”
—the shifting or dragging about of sub-
stances —is a wonderful thing and 'the best
test of prosperity. The blessedness of shift-
ing or dragging holds good even w’hen it con-
sists of buying nigger or “scab” or slave-
labor products that we can t honestly pay for,
and bringing them here in cheap-labor ships
that we can’t pay for either. Inside a period
of four years just ended £13,3/4,000 of gold
disappeared out of Australia’s bank reserves ;
ditto all the gold (about £30.231,000) dug
out of the ground in four years; ditto, appar-
ently, about £7,000,000 of the gold that
people used to carry about with them or
keep in the cash-box or on the mantelpiece
(this estimate is based on the increase.of
paper money in actual circulation, and the
increase arises because people carry notes
now in place of sovereigns). In addition, the
States borrowed over £30,000,000 in London,
principal and interest payable in gold. This
was quite apart from ,war loans and renewal
of old debts. All these matters were evi-
dence of an amazing drain on the only kind
of currency which can keep this country sol-
vent in the outside world where Federal
notes aren’t accepted. And in return for
this profligacy and much previous profligacy
on a smaller scale *
(1) We get a vast amount of lack of work, for
while we import the goods that so much borrowed
and other gold is wasted to pay for we can’t have
the job of making the same goods.
(2) We get the Go-Slow policy.
(3) We get a mass of industrial ignorance and
incapacity, for we can’t employ outsiders to do all
our finer and more intricate anil scientific work and
yet have a fair chance to learn how to do that sort
of work ourselves.
(4) AA'e get a grumpy habit of mind; a feeling
that too many cats are after one mouse; a suspicion
that the fast worker is an enemy who wants to drink
too much of the scanty job and leave his mates on
the industrial raft to die of thirst; a conviction that
piece-work, however well paid, is a device for the
creation of an industrial aristocracy; and we get an
incoherent grudge against the “capitalist.” He often
deserves it, but it is a pity that so much grudge is
directed against the local manufacturer who employs
Australian labor at Australian wages, and that so
little grudge is left over for the outside manufacturer
who doesn’t, and for his minion, the importer, who
helps him not to do it.
(5) We get a hypocritical habit of mind the result
of making it a theoretical rule to employ white non-
union labor only, while paying as much as
£76,000,000 in a year for the products of labor
which is partly nigger and partly “scab” and partly
any old thing.
(6) AVe get another hypocritical frame of mind
through shouting about “freedom” and “Australia
for the Australians” while selling the children as
slaves to any money-lender who will buy them, and
persistently praqtising the doctrine of “Australia for
any Extraneous Money-Shark who will Lend Us a
Few More Millions.” AVage-slavery, about which
there is so much bloated fuss, only directly affects
the people now living. Cut interest-slavery, when
the debt isn’t incurred because war has made it
unavoidable and the country has to be saved from
a foreign tyrant, is the wanton trading into bondage
of our own flesh and blood to the tenth generation
and beyond. AVill the slave-sellers at the Trades
Hall ponder this on next Eight Per Cent. Day—or
Eight Hours Day?
(7) AVe get a confused aspect of mind through
trying to believe, with one and the same belief, that
this is the richest country on earth and that it can’t
afford to sole its own boots without raising a loan
at s') per cent, nominally repayable in 1950.
(3) AA ? e get a high cost of living because our
multitudinous imported goods are at war prices and
subject to war freights. Even the products of our
comparatively simple local manufactures have gone
up, largely because so much of the so-called “raw
material” is imported. This state of things might
lead to the creation of new industries, only the fool
tariff provides that the foreign competitor shall have
the run of our markets in the old way when the
war is over and he becomes a cheap and low-wage
competitor again. No manufacturer cares to risk
money on industries which would almost certainly be
snuffed out after the war, and his banker would be
afraid to lend him money, anyhow. Besides, the
Government strikes new industries with a special
War Profits tax, reckoning that it serves the new
industry darned well right for employing Australians
here, at union wages, instead of Ah Sins in Canton.
(9) AVe get few desirable white immigrants be-
cause we have so little employment to offer to the
highly-skilled artisan who wants to help in carrying
our debt burden, and defending our country, and
desires to be a handy, reliable customer for our
wheat and rabbits. No Six Hatters clamor at our
door these days—or at all events they don’t clamor
much.
(10) Also we will get the curse of posterity when
it inherits the bill for the old man’s blither, luxury,
laziness and incapacity—when it inherits the bill
after “wireless” has made the telegraph lines mere
scrap-iron, and the fast-growing power of aerial
navigation has made most of the railways mere junk
and the present “permanent and reproductive works”
are deader than Antiquity’s mother. And if pos-
terity doesn’t dig up the old man’s bones and hang
them on a tree it will only be because the exertion
isn’t worth while.
* * * *
Australia has had little luck during this
war. In Britain, Canada and the U.S.A.,
which have also escaped actual invasion, the
artisans, as a set-off to their war troubles,
are at least earning the best Avages on record
by doing skilled labor at high pressure in
munition-factories, shipyards, machinery-
shops, etc. Canada and the U.S.A. are turn-
ing an honest billion dollars or so by deliver-
ing to the Allies in Europe food, machinery
and munitions at war rates, and by doing
some sea-conveyance for the Allies, with
their own ships and charging war freights.
They have even lent the Allies a good deal
of money or money’s worth at a good deal per
cent. Australia has missed the ’bus—the
largest ’bus in all history. It has to show for
its share a very great increase of State debt
(not war debt, but purely loafing debt) ; a
very great addition to the annual interest
bill; a decreasing area under cultivation;
manufacturing stagnation; shipyards which
are still empty; an arms factory (not a big
one) in a partially-suspended condition ; more
strikes than usual; more imports than
usual; a fair possibility of national insol-
vency, which will be more than a fair possi-
bility unless some kind or degree of reforma-
tion sets in. And the Go Slow policy.
MOTHERHOOD.
Thf condensed remarks of various writers:
“Quill” : I reject “C.N.R.’s” definition of moral-
ity as “the course of conduct best calculated to serve
the welfare of future generations.” All the political
obscurantists, religious bigots, military tyrants and
social brigands—the whole unclean brood of Igno-
rance and Oppression—that have infested the world
since the days of Adam have found in just such
machine-made phrases pious justification for their
grossest infamies. There is no crime so base, no
vice so abject, that it cannot be condoned if the
rule of conduct is framed first and the definition of
morals put together afterwards. What I wanted from
the lady who yearned to gratify what she considered
a “perfectly legitimate appetite” and to simultane-
ously interrupt a physiological function was a defini-
tion of a certain distinction, according to her ethical
standards —not an assurance from someone else that
the moral distinction between two deliberate acts is
to be found in the cheapness of one and the ex-
pensiveness of the other. This reduces the thing to
banality. And just as “C.N.R.” has failed to under-
stand the point of my remarks he has read Havelock
Ellis without comprehension. Certainly Ellis con-
demns a reckless and unrestrained profusion in pro-
duction. Who does not? But Ellis no less emphati-
cally condemns the attitude of such people as
“Lysistrata” and “C.N.R.” Talk about “the fall
of the birthrate following the increase of civilisation”
is nonsense. Civilisations have died before. The
one w r e are most familiar with is not in the best of
health. Those that have died have all had one
feature in common. Possibly the famous saying
“Suffer the little children to come unto Me” has
been mangled in translation. He may have meant:
“When you get more civilised and can’t feed them
don’t suffer them at all.”
* * * *
“Vectis” : “Quill” has read his Havelock Ellis
to little purpose. That authority insists, again and
again, on the plain truths that sexual relations in
themselves, for their own sake, are physiological and
beneficial; that chastity is unnatural; and, above
all, that the supreme expression of the love of man
and woman is not a shameful “something else” to
be hinted at in a whisper. The final answer to it
all is that we are civilised, or are trying to be;
and civilisation is simply the result of a long fight
between man’s brain and Nature’s cruelty. A high
birth-rate and a high death-rate are Nature’s way.
Humanity has begun to tire of that, and demands
fewer births and fewer deaths. It is suggested that
the woman who does not breed to the limit of her
capability “deserves little of the nation.” The
woman’s reply to the nation may very well be:
“Thank you for nothing. Much the nation has done
for me so far! There are two sides to a bargain.
Show me some practical recognition of my efforts on
behalf of the nation; arrange things so that each
new baby doesn’t mean so much more work and less
enjoyment of the decencies of life; and then we’ll
talk. In the meantime, don’t you dare call me a
legalised prostitute.”
* * * *
“Austra” : In answer to a perfectly reasonable
question “F.R.C.S.” asks: “Has she [‘Lysistrata’]
no heart or imagination for the perils and anxieties
faced by our men in the trenches? They’re doing
their job there; why should not women do theirs
at home?” The fact of a woman being possessed
of a “heart and imagination” at the present time
is sufficient to make her unwilling to suffer the risks
of child-bearing. How many normal (or even use-
ful) children are born of a mother so suffering?
“F.R.C.S.” accuses “Lysistrata” of selfishness be-
cause she would consider the child as much as she
does the mother. Is the child not to be considered ?
* * * *
“Lysistrata”: “Quill” (B. 2S/3/’18) asks me to
draw a moral distinction between prevention and
the abortion I “deplore.” I said nothing whatever
about “deploring” abortion: this is merely one of
the many inaccuracies in “Quill's” reading of my
letter. Moreover, I must decline to draw moral
distinctions, as “Quill” uses that term. I am trying
hard to think clearly and dispassionately about a
question which I recognise is grave indeed; but
this cannot be done if one’s mind is to be confused
by claptrap. Those actions are best called moral
which tend to the benefit and well-being of the race,
and those immoral which make for its deterioration.
Now, it has yet to be proved that prevention re-acts
injuriously on the woman who uses it to secure
a reasonable period between pregnancies. It might
well be held that it helps her to a greater degree
of physical and mental development, is advantageous
to iier children, and consequently a benefit accruing to
the race. Abortion, thus considered, certainly appears
racially immoral where it injures the health of the
mother and consequently the race through her. On
the other hand, can it be questioned that the fretus
begotten of a congenital idiot should never be allowed
to come to birth? Here, surely, abortion, con-
sidered from the view-point of the race, would be
pre-eminently moral. Something about that “indis-
putably legitimate appetite” disagrees with “Quill."
Probably its indisputableness. The maternal instinct,
he says, is one thing. This appetite is something
else. My contention precisely. “The question,” he
says, “that is really cogent and goes to the root of
the whole business, morally and physically”—it
doesn’t: it’s only the veriest beginning of the in-
quiry—“is this: ‘ls there any reason in the whole
wide world why a woman should mate with a man
unless she wants children?’ ” Emphatically, yes, there
is such reason. Surely none but a eunuch would
ask such a question expecting an answer in the nega-
tive, and only a eunuch who never guarded a harem
at that. Does he himself really hold that women
should mate with men only for the purpose of pro-
creation, that is to say, in the vast majority of
cases, for a period of not more than one month in
12? What would the men be doing during the
other 11 months? Of course such a scheme might
have worked when marriages were made in Heaven,
but it is hardly practicable with the locally-manu-
factured article. “Quill's” gibe about ability or
otherwise to write about motherhood “without think-
ing about prostitution” flies wide of the mark. Cer-
tainly one cannot imagine an investigator of
Havelock Ellis’s reputation disregarding prostitu-
tion as a factor when considering marriage and
motherhood. I have not read what he has written
later than the “Studies in the Psychology of Sex,”
but I find therein nothing to justify “Quill's” vague
innuendoes. But this need not argue that “Quill”
has not done so. If he takes the same liberties with
Havelock Ellis's text that he does with mine, he
wdll be able to find what he wants anywhere to
justify anything.
FIDDLING WHILE FREEDOM
SHRIEKS.
Nearly every day last week Australia found
herself plodding through long rigmaroles
about the intentions of numerous Govern-
ments. These included the intention of the
Federal Ministry to “keep Capital and Labor
in touch with each other” and to press on
with the scheme approved by Hughes for
establishing committees of industry and
appointing trade commissioners and so on.
There was also an intention of the N. S.
Wales Government to start saving calf-life
and rearing the animal salvaged from CockY”
until it had grown up; also the laudable in-
tention of the same Ministry to encourage the
production of wheat by guaranteeing 4s. a
bushel at country stations. According to
Premier Holman, protection for the calf and
the production of more wheat are war-win-
ning activities w'hich could only be neglected
at the expense of catastrophe. He may be
right. But this paper is regretfully com-
pelled to confess that, in spite of a most
ardent desire to be sociable, it cannot pump
up enthusiasm for these programmes. In the
days when copious argument about nothing-
in-particular was easier and more profitable
than doings things that mattered, the tragedy
of the defunct calf would have been a stirring
issue at a general election. But, unless com-
manded to do otherwise under the War Pre-
cautions Act, The Bulletin is going to re-
fuse to believe that it is the matter of first
importance now. The War Precautions Act
may be used to keep someone silent about
something. But it cannot silence the guns
in Europe nor their message to the Com-
monwealth.
Should defeat come to the Allies, what
will it avail Australia to know that in the
darkening hours a State had saved many
calves and grown wheat on spec., and that
the Commonwealth had a cut-and-dried plan
for doing something big when many other
bigger things had been finished? Less than
nothing! Yet, defeat is far from being a
remote possibility. The war is therefore the
only thing really worth bothering about.
Either the Federal Parliament can or cannot
decide upon a policy relating to enlistment.
If it cannot get the required men, then it is
guilty of something very near treason if it
prevents other people from trying. As for
the State Parliaments, their wild extrava-
gance is a scandal of the first magnitude.
Their rightful business is to cut down their
preposterously elaborate systems of govern-
ment and administration to a point reason-
ably adjacent to sanity. Australia stands
confronted by perils to which calves and
wheat and Red Tape have no more relation
than the missing hairs on Joe Cook’s head.
There are at least 90 per cent, more politi-
cians and 50 per cent, more officials than at a
pinch could grapple with the-work. The per-
centage of these that will be done without
if the war goes the wrong way is probably
even greater. Everybody knows this to be
solemn truth, and knows, too, that every citi-
zen in Australia whose activities, public or
private, are not determined in the light of the
desperate necessities of the moment is not
helping to win this war. He is helping to
lose it. This paper is not going to say that
Cocky should not grow wheat or that the
calf should die a premature death or that the
general industry of the country should go to
ruin. What it has said and says again is
that the war is the first consideration, and
that the growing of wheat and work of every
other kind, from the arrangement of public
documents in pigeonholes to the running of
railways, should be upon the basis of a war
programme. If the enemy had his feet on
Australian soil or his ships in the Pacific, it
would be. And it is only a geographical fic-
tion which prevents the public from seeing
that those feet and warships are actually on
and about us now.
Of course it is not a bit of good moaning
and groaning because Australian statesman-
ship failed in the past to see the bigger things
and do the bigger deeds. The past has been
in many ways tragic, with shrieking dema-
gogues in the market-place, sectarian bigots
masquerading as patriots, and the money-hog
fattening on the nation’s blood. But it is
even yet not too late for the political leaders
of the Australian people to formulate at least
some coherent war policy, and, instead of
keeping the community by the ears, bringing
it to realise the terrible dangers encompass-
ing the country. It is quite within the
bounds of possibility that the march of events
in Europe within the next few weeks may
settle the question of reinforcements for our
army. But in that case the war duties of
Australians would in a day change only in
detail and be more urgent than ever. It is
not impossible that an emergency may arise
demanding that the Federal Parliament
should make sweeping changes in. the States.
Is it ready for such a task? No; and the
danger of being overwhelmed in our own
chaos is almost as great as that of being
humiliated by the enemy. Continuance of
government on party lines is, in present cir-
cumstances, a scandal to the last degree.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Let Us Eat (Australian Food) and Drink
(Australian Liquor in Moderation) and
Be Merry (to a Certain Extent).
The demand for economy is not a doctrine
to be taken indiscriminately. Economy is
urgently required in many directions, and
the one where it is most required is the one
least mentioned. When we are exhorted to
follow' the example of nations which have
breadless days and meatless days and egg-
less days, a bee is talking in the exhorter s
hat. Our need is for some debtless (or
borrowless) days. The little that could be
saved if we became flat in the stomach and
shabby in the aspect would make small dif-
ference even if it reached Europe, and until
Hughes, the great dry-land Columbus, gets
his ships afloat, it can no more get there
than it can get back into the tenth century.
The racing of the Australian horse on Aus-
tralian grass is only folly, inasmuch as it
employs men on unproductive work and
beguiles the public from work that is pro-
ductive and essential. This rtile applies
to many other sports. In a general way
and w'ith some few reservations, it is reason-
able for us to go on our old path, and eat
and drink and wear very much as usual, and
forget that there is any old war —to do this
so far as concerns the things that can be
bought with paper money, w T hich means the
things that are wholly made inside the Com-
monw-ealth, for it is only there that our
paper money is good. There is no risk that
extravagance of this sort will drain the
country of its circulating medium. The
paper money never runs aw r ay, for it would
lose its value if it did. But economy is
called for, with no reservation at all, and
called for with a loud voice, in the eating
and drinking and wearing and otherwise
using of everything that has to be paid for


in gold—that is, eA'ery kind or sort or species
of imported goods. The same rule applies
to external loans (Avhich are imported
credit), for principal and interest have alike
to be liquidated in sovereigns; also it applies
to principal and interest of private bonds
and debentures held abroad, and to rents
payable' to absentee landlords and the divi-
dends of absentee shareholders. All these
call for the hard yellow coin which is becom-
ing so scarce. Already this country OAves
nearly £50,000,000 of coin to Britain for
the pay of our soldiers, and all the visible
specie in Australia wouldn't cover this lia-
bility or nearly cover it. Further, we have
renewed at nearly 6 per cent, a State loan
payable in gold in London, while the Real
market would gladly hav-e done it at 5i —
only the one kind of money which the local
market can put up in great abundance
doesn’t pass current to pay off loans in Lon-
don. A Avhole £40,000,000 of it wouldn’t
cover a Aveek’s Avages of one soldier in
France, though it would remunerate a
whole army in Australia. It is possible
for this country to be locally bloated Avith
Avealtk and yet have to call a meeting of
its creditors in England over a liability
which doesn’t equal a year’s income of the
X. 8. Wales State Government alone. Even
if a man Avere to eat six loaves and 21b. of
meat and 41b. of butter and 12 bananas at
a meal, and Avere to drink a gallon of local
wine or beer at a sitting, be wouldn’t do so
much harm —except to his stomach —as if
lie bought one imported collar. And he
might back 10 losers at the races without
doing the same damage to the nation’s re-
sources as Avould arise from his Avife’s pur-
chase of a single imported frill. Loans and
interest and dividends and all sorts of bills
which are purely local in character can be
settled for with paper, but if the supply of
gold runs short and the stuff can’t be bor-
rowed —not even at 40 or 424 per cent, or
more—the public shutters go up so far as
concerns several hundred millions of outside
liabilities. In 4916 this country took
nearly £3,000,000 from its diminished stock
of the only medium which will pass in settle-
ment of our outside bills. In other words,
the foreign interest account and the insane
and stupendous purchases of foreign fluff and
other matters absorbed £3,000,000 over and
above the value of exports and the year’s
outnut of gold and the amount of new loans
raised with difficulty in England. In 4947
foreign interest and insane foreign fluff and
sundries reduced the stock of specie by
another 5| millions. The progress towards
the day of judgment (w’ith costs) is rapid.
The situation is new to us. When hard
money was the common currency here and
in England, the local stress gave warning of
trouble ahead. But, as already pointed out,
Avhen there are two currencies, a futile, un-
seeing, thick-headed Freetrade generation
may be surprised in the midst of local
revelry to learn that we have gone bankrupt
in England for Avant of a mere million or so
of gold.

The Blundering Over Ireland.
The conscription of Ireland has come at
last, Avith all its dangers, at a time when the
Avar has reached its crisis, and when there is
the call for the wisest and most wary states-
manship. Irish enlistments have been
lamentably few; recruiting has failed even in
those fields Avhere, for ages, fighting armies
Avere most easily raised. In the eighteenth
century alone half a million Irishmen died
in the service of France. To-day half a
million Irishmen have failed to come volun-
tarily to the aid of France when her very
existence as a nation is threatened, and
though Irish sentiment from Donegal to Wex-
ford is in complete accord with the feeling
Avhich has caused the Frenchman to put up
his present heroic struggle. On the face of
it. there has been something radically wrong
with the manner in which the appeal to
Irishmen has been made; and the
Avisdom of forcing conscription on a
people after failing to move them in
the direction of their own natural enthusi-
asms must be very gravely doubted. Con-
scription is a proper thing if a people will-
ingly consents to it. It may be the right
thing if the majority of the thinking leaders
of a nation consent to it. It cannot
be defended if both people and thinking lead-
ers are united against it. Rightly or Avrongly,
the majority of Irishmen declare that they
will not have this thing imposed upon them
by any Parliament but their own; and, in
the face of the tremendous difficulties and
dangers of the war crisis, to force it upon
them may be fraught with shudder-
some consequences. Two years ago 4500
Dublin rebels put up a grim and
lamentable fight for several days against
more numerous and better-armed forces;
and, even on this basis, it looks as if
every conscript obtained in Ireland Avould
require the withdrawal of at least one trained
man from the fighting front. Yet Irish
soldiers might still be obtained. Let a
reasonable measure of Home Rule be immedi-
ately granted, and give the new Irish Parlia-
ment the opportunity of raising an army in
its oAvn way for the defence of the real Irish
interests Avhich Home Rule would automati-
cally create. Returned Australian fighting
men tell of the splendid service of the
Irish troops. The tragedy is that there are
so feAV of them. English blunders at the
beginning of the war repressed the recruiting
of Irish by Irishmen; but that does not
excuse non-enlistment when the crisis height-
ened. By standing aloof Ireland has lost
many friends and has made none outside
Germany; and, since that only adds to the
difficulties of the future, it is to be deplored
almost as much as that the nation was not
long ago put to the test and given an oppor-
tunity to conscript itself.
The Drought and the Boots and the
Land.
Dear Bulletin, —Right alongside certain
impassioned appeals to people to “go on the
land,” and to “grow more,” appeared a state-
ment by the principal of the Hawkesbury
(N.S.W.) Agricultural College that the next
drought is about due. It seems quite pro-
bable. Some day, perhaps, Australia Avill be
better supplied with drought-proof indus-
tries, but that is a matter for the future.
There are points about any nic,e big indus-
try which will yield steady Avages, Avhere the
worker doesn’t require a heap of capital and
get in fair seasons a bare artisan’s pay for
12 hours work per diem, where the crop isn’t
a gamble Avith hail and fire, insects and rab-
bits as Avell as dry seasons and wet floods,
and where there is a reliable demand right
at the door instead of a small local demand
and a precarious market 10,000 miles away
Avhich at times can’t be reached for Avant of
ships. Avhile there is the added chance of the
vessels, Avhen there are any, being Hunned
on the high seas. The sort of industry that
is looked for is one which remunerates the
hired man better than the land does, and
where the product isn’t commandeered by
amateurish politicians and stacked for an
indefinite period in the open air—one that
doesn’t involve exposure to all sorts of
weather, and to snake-bite and ant-bite and
horse-bite, and similar drawbacks. There are
many such industries, mostly carried on in a
small way but capable of indefinite expan-
sion if this couiitry had a tariff like that of,
say, the United States. The difference
between that country and this one is that
the U.S. buys foreign goods at the rate of
less than £4 per inhabitant per annum, while
Ave buy at the rate of about £ls 10s. So if
the consumption of all kinds of goods aver-
ages £4O per annum for everybody, including
the baby, and it looks a liberal estimate, the
U.S. person has £36 worth of employment
per head, including the baby, Avhile the Aus-
tralian has only £24 10s. worth. Conse-
quently the steady, well-paid, drought-proof
job which has a roof over it and isn’t liable
to be eaten by locusts is much more abun-
dant in the U.S. than it is hereabouts. And
the U.S. individual isn’t urged by apostles of
loose eloquence (and sometimes of loose
habits, and sometimes of tight habits) to “go
on the land.” With a big market at his door
—the market supplied by having the people
Avho make his boots and such-like at his door
—he just goes on the land. And he is hard
to drag off when he is there.—Yours, etc.,
Henry llorsecollar.
How Not to Encourage Production.
The world’s area under flax has diminished
from 19,000,000 to 13,000,000 acres, and the
British Government announces that it will
buy the goods extensively at £l7O per ton
delivered in England. Flax, which is to a
great extent the product of cheap Russian
labor, has no Protection in Australia, so the
local high-wage industry never had much
show. True, a bonus was granted of 10 per
cent, on the market value, but that didn’t
nearly counterbalance the wage difficulty.
Then (1) the bonus was only fixed for five
years, and a very inadequate measure of
Protection for five years was a poor induce-
ment; (2) not more than £BOOO could be
paid in one year on flax and hemp com-
bined, so that at recent prices it covered
about 3020 tons. The person who grew ton
No. 3021 was liable to be left out in the
cold, and nobody cared to risk being that
individual; moreover if the hemp man got
in first the flax individual might never have
a look in. Then (3) the collection of a
bonus from the Treasury is a weary and elab-
orate business. So last year the quantity
of flax and hemp (the two are lumped
together) recorded by the bonus department
was just 238 tons. The amount imported
was 8005 tons, and £372,000 of good but
scarce Australian gold went to pay Bol-
sheviks and others for it. Now all is to be
changed. Nothing is said about a stiff
import duty to insure that the industry will
have a chance to live when the Avar demand
is over and the cheapest labor in Europe
gets to work again. Nothing is said even
about a renewal of the silly little bonus after
the Avar, in order to ease the fall. Nothing
is said about lioav the flax is to be got to
England, seeing that the ship famine grow’S
worse rather than better. But a Flax In-
dustry Committee is to be at once appointed,
and everybody Avho owns or “controls” more
than 2cAVt. of Victorian flax seed (flax-
groAving is a purely Victorian industry here)
is to register and supply a “declaration”
about something or other. The Government
guarantees £45 per ton for green flax 30in.
long, and any profits wflll be dealt Avith as
the Prime Minister and the committee see
fit. It is to be hoped they AA’on't be applied
to the purchase of more Ministerial motor-
cars. As there is only enough seed in the
country, so far as is known, to plant 1500
acres the committee and the declarations
and all the rest seem to be a case of much
howl and very little sheep’s overcoat. Just
by wav of giving the two good causes of
agriculture and linen manufacture a leg up
apiece (two legs in all), what is wrong with
a 50 per cent. duty, to yield some much-
needed revenue and reduce that bill of
£372,000 for imported flax and hemp?
The Gross Dishonesty of Party Politics.
Confronted by a resolution moved in the
House of Reps, last week by James Boyd,
of Henty (Vic.), the gist of which was that
Pearce should no longer be Minister for
Defence, the Phewsion, through the mouth
of Cook', raised a clamor that if the resolu-
tion was carried the party wmuld “fall,” that
chaos and other calamities would be created
by “handing the finances over to the Opposi-
tion,” and that it was the patriotic duty of
all men to assist in winning the war. Boyd
had not asked the Phewsion to “fall.” He
did not suggest handing the finances to the
Opposition. He had not denied the expedi-
ency of winning wars. His case, and that
of Gregory and Fowler, who supported it,
was that Pearce had already created chaos,
that nothing could be more calamitous than
what had already been revealed, and that
part of the job of winning the war consisted
of removing messers from positions of respon-
sibility. Wherefore Cook’s talk about “fall-
ing” and interpreting a proposal to shift
Pearce into a scheme for throwing the whole
Ministry on the scrapheap was grossly dis-
honest. Cook' knows as well as Boyd that
Pearce’s failure has been monumental —
that it has involved the nation in huge
losses of cash and losses that are not to be
measured in money. Every member of the
party knows the same things. Yet in obedi-
ence to a dictator, who must, of course, have
one or two trusted vassals about him. all
but half a dozen of the crowd are ready to
vote like marionettes. This is the party of
“Good Government,” of “Efficiency,” of
“Freedom”! As 22 out of the 48 on the
premises are drawing pay of some sort in
addition to their Parliamentary allowance as
members, it is also a party to which “Pearce
at any Price” naturally makes a powerful
appeal.
The Art of Missing the ’Bus.
The advantage of doing things at the right
time has been proved by the Germans all
through the war; and the neglect of vital
matters in days when they were easy hits the
Allies harder than any h.e. shell. There is,
for example, the matter of the Channel
tunnel, which was bulking large in English
politics in the years before Gladstone in-
troduced his first Home Rule Bill: had that
hole in the chalk bottom of Dover Straits
been completed before the war, it would have
been worth many army corps to the defend-
ers of the Western line. Troops could have
been slipped through into France with such
ease and wounded soldiers been returned with
such speed and safety that the British task
on land would have been enormously light-
ened and the guardianship of the sea made
infinitely more effective. The old arguments
against the project look thin and weak to-
day. It was feared that the enemy might
invade through it; but with ready means of
flooding the hole, no one would regard such
a possibility seriously in the light of later
developments. Again, it was urged by the
Commander-in-Chief at the time that a
garrison of 10,000 men would be required
permanently at Dover. To keep as huge a
body as that idle in one place seemed to the
British taxpayer a suggestion of the Devil.
So the work was hung up, and the. great and
bitter day of necessity found the circling sea
a menace to England rather than a defence.
Australia has a few little projects similarly
suspended. When the safety of the Northern
Territory depends on troops and munitions
sent along a camel pad, even our politicians
may lament the absence of the North-to-
Soiith railway which might by this time have
been completed. When the military conges-
tion at the Victorian and Queensland borders
threatens the very existence of the Common-
wealth, somebody will weep tears over our
broken railway-gauges. We could put these
things right even now but that our Win-the-
War statesmen are too eager to get to the
talking front.
Billjim’s Mail.
Ted Colles says a word for the Army
Post: —
I marched down to the A.I.F. Base Post Office in
London to get a bit of my own back; but in the
end I evacuated with a suspicion that, while the
Army doesn’t have half the truths told about it that
it deserves, the postal branch has been getting it in
the neck more than it has qualified for.
I found the 8.P.0. housed in Bass’s big ex-
brewery up London, N.W. The sorting racks, re-
direction tables, etc., were spread in vanishing lines
over a couple of acres of clean, unfurnished floor-
space where even the soul of a profiteer could hardly
get mislaid or overlooked. And the work of sorting,
despatching and (very important) re-direction of
mails to men who had become mislaid from their
regular addresses by reason of sickness, wounds,
leave, special duties elsewhere, transfers to other
units, clinks, attachments and other accidents liable
to bodies like the now sadly-diminished Australians
—all was going on with a calm, automatic precision
that would have appalled anyone but a Tommy
R.S.M.
In the course of my offensive I was neatly coun-
tered by my opponent. He called my name and
number, and from along a far line of mute card-
cabinets a piece of pasteboard rapidly came our way.
It recorded startling particulars and dates of my
movements in France (my duties had placed me
with several units, including Imperial troops) up to
the time of my arrival in London; and the facts
that (a) I was but one of a few hundred thousand
Australians on this side of the world, (6) my army
movements had been much more irregular than those
of most men, (c) I’d never once been guilty of notify-
ing the 8.P.0. of any change of address, all made
me realise a respect for the system that had tracked
me down so effectively.
In the end I became almost amiable towards my
enemy; and was shown a wired-in compound occu-
pied only by the staff which dealt with registered
mail; and another isolated compartment where par-
cels for men definitely known to be killed or missing
were being opened by a small, specially-sworn-in
staff, the contents being recorded and signed for as
they were extracted and then packed in sealed crates
for despatch to the Australian Comforts Fund Com-
missioners. (I was told that this is done by autho-
rity of War Precautions Regulation No. 003-x ? .)
I also saw neatly stacked Australian mail that
had been addressed—for safety—to people in Eng-
land, to be forwarded by English post to men in
France. One typical sample had been travelling all
through the Toning' army, because the English sender
hadn’t tacked on “A.1.F.” to the army unit mentioned
in the address.
Finally my polite opponent produced a return of
the last month’s parcel delivery in France, showing
that the 8.P.0. actually held receipts for the delivery
of 97 per cent, of the consignment. Taking the
casualties and accidents of the field into considera-
tion, this was a remarkable record: no doubt mainly
due to the fact that, whereas the postal corps once
controlled mails only as far as battalion headquarters,
where they fell to the tender mercies of casual
orderlies, to-day unit-orderlies are controlled by the
postal corps, and are accordingly responsible for
the delivery of mail into the digger’s hands. So
no longer should undelivered mail be found carelessly
dumped in the mud and forgotten by casual officers
and their orderly-room staffs—as I have seen it in
France on the occasion of changing and taking over
parts of the front line by different battalions; for
which many curses were no doubt later heaped on
the heads of the p.o. people by Australians in Europe
and at home.
At the same time, the postal people can’t hope to
deal so effectively with all war risks. When I think
of these—from the mails lost at sea and the occa-
sional field post-office or mail-lorry blown up by
Fritz, to the fatalities and ills liable to overtake
postal orderlies, dug-outs, diggers and things in
general near the firing-line—l have an absurd feeling
that hereafter, whenever a letter from home arrives
safely through these perils into my hands, I will
regard the reinstated Australian postal corps in the
light of a loyal, solicitous and resourceful friend,
and will try to forget the unpublishable things I
have said about it in the past.
Canada during the last three years exported
goods to the value of £210,097,000 more than
its imports; in other words, it put on a
spurt and had £210,097,000 available with
which to reduce its foreign debts, to pay
war expenses, and to finance new industries.
It had all that surplus earned, not borrowed.
And in the same time poor Australia was
importing far more than it exported, and
was floating loans here and in England to
pay the difference (while Canada was re-
ceiving the difference), and in addition was
drawing on the cash reserves in its banks so
that they went down suddenly from
£36,000,000 to £23,000,000, though, as
against that there was a slight increase of
metal in the Treasury. Also the country
spent the loose gold which people used to
carry in their pockets—they carry paper now.
Canada has increased its exports by 400 per
cent, since the war started, while ours have
risen about 10 per cent.; and Canada’s in-
crease of exports is largely manufactured
goods, while our export of manufactured
goods is hardly worth mentioning. Canada
builds ships at a fast rate. Australia talks
about ships at a fast rate while its manu-
facturing population decreases. But then
Canada, like the U.S. and Germany, has
High Protection, and Australia hasn’t. One
day lately the Freetrade Sydney Telegraph
published an outpouring of the soul re a
Sydney firm which, after 10 years of fight-
ing, has so far overcome what the ’Graph
called “the inexplicable aversion on the part
of the public to the locally-produced article”
that it is now producing in one year a
million gallons of linseed-oil. A whole million
gallons! This fighting of the prejudice
against Australian goods is an awful waste
of time. Let the unnatriotic section of the
public keep its prejudice if it wants to, and
let it d—n Australian goods if it feels that
way, and let it use only foreign goods if it is
so disposed. But let the tariff make sure
that it pays 75 per cent, more for its im-
ported goods, or 100 per cent, more if neces-
sary. Then, even if it forms a great society
for denouncing all Australian products by
bell, book and candle, also by gas and elec-
tric light and every other illuminant—why,
let if! There is no Customs or excise dut y
on denunciation.

The man who makes two ears of wheat
grow where only one grew before is usually
considered a benefactor to his country; but
that is quite wrong. Billjim who comes
back from Palestine can tell you better.
The great country is the country where agri-
culture is carried on with a crooked stick
for a plough, said stick being dragged by a
bullock and a mule. It thus takes 10 men
to produce as much wheat as one Australian
produces with his steel discs and his strap-
ping team. It is true, as Billjim may
remark, that the crooked-stick agriculturist
looks miserable, and his surroundings all
howl to heaven of poverty and wretchedness;
but maybe that is camouflage. Anyhow, the
Waterside Workers’ Federation reckons that
the less wheat each man produces the better,
because there is then more for other people
to produce. On that principle it recently
asked that certain stevedores be fined for
working “double” winches with one man.
The working of these winches by one man
obviously releases one man from each winch
for other work, but the W.W. Federation
couldn’t see that that was a national advan-
tage. Justice Higgins could, though, and
told the applicants that unless and until
they could prove that the process placed an
undue strain on the man they had better get
busy with the winches. Really what the
Waterside Federation ought to protest
against is winches —not double winches, but
winches. If they were abolished and cargo
were carried up by hand, there would be
work for thousands and thousands of men.
What the thousands and thousands would
get for it is another matter. On that point
Billjim, who has seen how the crooked-
stick agriculturist lives, might be called as
a witness.

The Requiem of the Guns.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"The German bombardment of the Australian posi-
tions at Albert was the worst since Pozieres. At a
critical moment eight Australian machine-guns and
their crews withstood the full German assault. After
having inflicted tremendous casualties, the Aus-
tralians died where they stood.”
A blood-red jewel these have set
In battle’s diadem.
And, lest the living shall forget,
The dead now speak to them.
Silent beside their silent guns,
With hands that clench the earth,
They challenge, as Australia’s sons,
The land that gave them birth.
Outgunned, outnumbered, to the last
They strove to stem the tide —
The great grey sea that broke, but passed
The shambles Avhere they died.
Against the impossible arrayed,
They faced it, breast to breast,
Nor here in thought nor deed betrayed
The faith their death professed.
Their gunfire kindled there a light
That shall afar be shed
Where we, their kin, abjure the fight,
Secure behind their dead.
But, toiling till they died, they willed
The fixing of the gleam :
It rests Avith us to see fulfilled
That heritage supreme.
So shall our answer serve their need
In the dread days to come,
When we go forth, Avith hearts that heed
The call of lips now dumb;
Can Ave forget how, maimed and. mired,
Where blood and SAveat had run,
A dying gunner crouched and fired
The last Australian gun?
