The Slanderers of Barabbas.
Thebe is a streak of pathos and there is
also a streak of misery about the 27th
annual report of Australian Labor (N.S.W.
branch). It complains bitterly, among other
things, that “the peace proposals have not
been discussed in Parliament.” One might
think it a small, humble idea to discuss
peace proposals when the fellow who made
the war won’t have peace, except at the
price of a complete surrender, an incredible
indemnity and a vast transfer of territory
in which Australia will probably be in-
cluded. It is as futile as offering an illumi-
nated text to a crocodile to induce him to
leave off; and it suggests those burning
patriots invented by Dickens who regu-
larly sent a copy of their paper to Queen
Victoria, care of the Tower of London, for
Berlin would hardly hear an echo of the
discussion. Still some people find balm and
solace in words. There is a bitter denuncia-
tion because the N. S. Wales Government
“stopped most of the railways under con-
struction and other public, works, thus
throwing thousands of men on the labor
market.” No allowance is made by these
gushing children of nature for the fact that
even the most strenuous Ministry can’t get
blood out of a post or loans out of a stone.
It managed to “blew” £0,862,179 of borrowed
funds during last financial year, though the
job of raising the money was like dragging
meat out of the jaws of a tiger, and it is
going along this year, so far ag nine months’
returns show, at the rate of nearly
£5,000,000, though it hardly knows itself
how it got the money. Bull has refused to
look at a public works loan, and the Federal
War Loan is using up the local market,
which is mostly paper money at best. It is
base ingratitude to abuse such a Govern-
ment for insufficient public works, especially
as the N.S.W. Laborites first attained office
on their “scrap of paper” pledge to reduce
borrowing, which then stood at a mere frac-
tion of the present figure. Railway-building
has met with no very serious stoppage up
to date; the stoppage is to come. The report
also speaks with an intensity of bitterness
about a “diabolical and fiendish conspiracy,”
by parties who are still at large, “to bring
about industrial slavery by the creation of a
huge army of unemployed,” but details are
wanting. The “wage system” is strenuously
denounced, and there is a proposal to bring
the Labor movement into line with “the
most advanced thinkers among the organised
workers of the world” possibly the Bol-
sheviks and the I.WAV. And the report
cites Barabbas as a horrible example. Yet
all we know against poor old Barabbas
suggests that he was a good fighting patriot.
He was a “notable prisoner” and a man who
had “made insurrection” against the Roman
tyrant and the Roman capitalist. Some-
body had been killed, and it is a mighty poor
insurrection that doesn't kill somebody. Then
lie was a “robber,” quite distinct from the
two “thieves” who were crucified. “Robber”
was a handy capitalistic term for the man
who rose against Roman law and had to
take refuge in waste places, like Wallace
and Garibaldi and Juarez when their luck
was out. The Robbers got back some of
their own when they fought the capitalists
to the bitter finish during the siege of Jeru-
salem, and it is a low-down trick for the
party which devised that report to throw a
clod at the bones of the man who “made
insurrection.”
The Hell in Northern France.
For a day or two last week it looked as
though Hindenburg’s thrust at the northern
sector of the Allies’ line was going to twist
the situation thereabouts into something like
the figure eight. The blow was heavy.
The place chosen for delivery was well
selected. Trampling over the remnants
of Portugal the enemy made rapid
advance westward from the old battle-
ground uenr Neuve Chapelle, Fleur-
baix and Armentieres. But the defence
stiffened after the first couple of days and
after violent efforts to continue the journey
the German hauled oft' to get his second wind.
He had driven a deep salient into the defence,
widening the wings as he went, and without
a doubt scored a material advantage. The
head of his spear when the round ended was
dangerously close to a point which, if lost
to the Allies, would seriously imperil the
position before Ypres and bring the enemy
dangerously close to Calais and Dunkirk.
Clearly enough this is what the German is aim-
ing at, and for it he is prepared to risk enor-
mous loss. And it is equally clear that to
Britain and France protection of these
Channel ports, especially Calais, is, if not
absolutely vital, at least so important as to
mean the difference between relative safety
and strategical embarrassment of great mag-
nitude. The order issued by llaig reminding
the troops that they are fighting with their
backs to the wall; the rushing up of French
reserves to the threatened locality ; the speech
made by Geodes in the House of Commons
in which he pointed to the new factors that
would arise if the enemy reached the sea —
all these are evidence of the gravity with
which the happenings of last week were re-
garded. That the German will hit again
thereabouts, and do it hard, is apparently
accepted as certain. Heaven knows what his
losses have been, but the magnitude of these
recent operations of his leave no sort of
doubt that he still has great military
resources at his command and is using them
with skill and confidence. There are not
many Frenchmen who are not carrying a gun
now. Britain is meeting the hurricane by
calling up the greyheads and starting to put
the comb over Ireland —a step of mighty
doubtful expediency. Uncle Sam is speeding
up his effort to cross the Atlantic more
numerously. On the whole it seems a fair
thing to conclude that the sooner Sam has a
million men in France instead of in the
vicinity of New York the better for all
hands. The struggle of the Hun to break
the stranglehold on the West is quite a
serious business, and it is worth remember-
ing that the usually well-informed Loval
Fraser, writing in the London Times, con-
tradicts the stories about the German having
lately suffered vastly greater casualties than
the British and French. It is war at
the axe-and-bludgeon stage now. The exact
size of the Portuguese loss is a matter of
academic interest. At least the tiny force
had one run for its money. Wellington
used to call the Portuguese his Fighting
Cocks, and if they lived up to his opinion of
them, the final report will be that they were
small but gallant potatoes and few in a hill.
Two Sydney firms/ Farmer’s and Anthony
Horderns’, have, with the approval of the
Commonwealth Government, put up £2OOO,
which will go in cash prizes to purchasers of
£lO War Loan bonds at their shops until
April 24. With each £lO bond a registered
and numbered prize ticket will be given ; these
numbers will be drawn under official super-
vision on May 10 next in the Sydney Town
Hall. Having gone thus far. the Govern-
ment will presumably now reconsider its
former decision and seek to obtain £5,000.000
or some other amount under ordinary lottery
conditions, the interest, or portion of it, being
drawn for quarterly. If only 1 p.c. of the
interest were set aside for drawing, quarterly
prizes of £12,500 could be distributed; and
every holder of a bond would have a chance
of a big win, while still retaining his bond.
Hundreds of thousands of people go to the
races for a “flutter,” and hundreds of thou-
sands have a “flutter” without going to the
races. There is no earthly reason why races
should come into the matter at all. The plan
would appeal to vast numbers to whom an
ordinary War Loan doesn’t; and to large
numbers who subscribe in the ordinary way
it would make an appeal from another angle.
For Red Cross and similar purposes money
is being publicly raised in ways that wouldn’t
be approved if the need were not so urgent;
and the Defence Department’s need of money
is just as urgent as the Red Cross’s.
The great heart of the country is sound
but the great feet of the country are rather
slow. Therefore the body that is on top
of these feet is apt to arrive late. When a
real war crisis comes the nation probably
arises, but as things are nowadays, unless
it rises to the occasion at least 12 months
before the occasion it is liable to be a year
behind the occasion. Australia did pretty
well with its export of soldiers in 1014 and
1915. Then the Hun-employed or somebody
touched a button, and the supply of recruits
dropped wonderfully. But there was a great
increase in industrial disturbances, and an
uprising of strange folk who were willing
to fight when Ireland got Home Rule, or
when the I.W.W. law-breakers were let out
of gaol, or when all the differences between
capital and labor were settled, or when the
reconstruction of society was effected—and
not till then. Probably the Hun-employed
had touched the button again. Now the
tremendous news from France has given
recruiting a boost, but things are moving
rapidly in France, and it takes a year to
export and train a good soldier, and the
great heart of the people may be 360 days
too late to have much say in the matter.
“King to Wilson” was a recent big head-
ing in a Sydney paper. It was only about a
congratulatory cablegram, and probably our
ally didn’t mind. Still it might have been
“King to President” or perhaps even “Wind-
sor to Wilson.”
The Hun shells any place he can see. He
has been dropping shells into Monrovia,
population 6000, and capital of Liberia, the
only nigger republic on earth and the most
peaceful state that ever stole a fowl. Army,
including police, 600; navy, one gunboat
(laid up) ; only ambition three square meals
a day, including water-melon.
The Edith Cavell Memorial Association is
striking out a good line for its Rod Gross
stall on April 26. It asks for something of
historic —even the early mementoes of the
great war come under that label —interest.
Anyone who has preserved every badge and
button of patriotic appeals would have a
valuable gift to offer.
One of Ilindenburg s recent reports to the
Kaiser told him to have patience and wait
for the battle to “ripen.” Whilst corpses rot
the battle ripens, the German “work,”
according to lliudenburg, being on “a great
and sound basis.” A great basis of blood and
bones! The original of Berlin’s ridiculous
wooden statue is now an anxious rooster,
perched on the weather-cock of a charnel-
house and crowing about what lie's going to
do to the other bird when he comes down
next time.
“Solomon Malaprop”: An Adelaide jury
has, by two successive verdicts, endorsed
what United States people call the
“unwritten law” —that a man who kills his
wife’s paramour when caught in the act is
“not guilty.” It may be right or it may be
wrong, and I don’t even know who unwrit
the law. But it is loaded. It is rough on
the paramour if he happened to be 10 miles
away from the lady at the time, or if ho
had never met her in his life or even Heard
of her, for, being dead, he can’t prove an
alibi. If I looked like a paramour I should
never lend money to an armed man, or to
an ill-tempered man, or to a furtive, slink-
ing, waiting-round-the-corner sort of man,
and thereby give him an interest in my
decease.
Newcastle (N.S.W.) puts in a futile pro-
test. Its wool trade has been wiped out, and
its wool storage made a white elephant eat-
ing off its oxvn head by a simple-looking
regulation that all wool for export must go
to Sydney to be appraised. The appraisers,
being lame in both feet, can’t go out of Syd-
ney for appraising purposes. And as Sydney
hasn’t room for all this extra wool new
stores are to be put up, and as building space
is scarce a bit of Wentworth Park is to be
stolen. Newcastle desires to say politely
that, in the way of grabbing everything it
can reach, Sydney is 10 Huns rolled into one.
All these recruiting conferences are wrong.
The people who really ought to be got
together are the Possible Recruits; for the
tale of why they haven’t and don’t would be
the beginning of all recruiting wisdom.
The summary treatment of two women
candidates in S. Aus. does not show for cer-
tain that ladies are barred. Neither was a
party candidate; and for the Independent
there was no hope, male or female. In
Sturt, where the winners were all well over
6000. even ex-Premier Crawford Vaughan,
standing as an Independent, got only 2000.
The one place where a breakaway really
affected results was up-country in Wooroora.
The old Liberal members, Miller, Pick and
O'Loughlin, took a fancy to stand as repre-
senting the new Farmers and Settlers’ Asso-
ciation. Thus there were throe parties in
the field. A solid vote put three Labor men
in, each with something over 2400. The
three fresh Liberal candidates were only
about 100 behind, and the three old members
all got just under 2000. Whoever was re-
turned was sure to represent a minority, and
the case will turn a lot of thoughts towards
a more scientific system of voting.
“Good Australian”: The U.S.A. Consul
advertises a list of goods which may not be
imported into his country without special
permission. The list of prohibited articles
includes most of the things the Yankees
can produce or make themselves, and
other things they know they do not want in
war time, or should not buy even if they
did want them ever so badly. This is a
course the Commonwealth Government ought
to follow promptly. If it doesn’t we shall
be drained of .so much more of our small
stock of gold that, in spite of all Willy Watt
may say, we may have to default. It is
worse than futile to say Australia will never
default and yet take no measures to abolish
the conditions which may compel us to do so.

Something transpired at Sydney Criterion
last Saturday in which large slabs of the
Hawaiian language, Muriel Starr, brown
brother, drink, revolutions, leprosy, a Bird of
Paradise, a beachcomber, a ballet and a vol-
cano were seriously implicated. “The Bird
of Paradise” looks like a sex-film gone wrong
and succumbing to a severe attack of Angli-
cism. It lias nine distinct morals and
some very nice girls. The programme con-
tains a long excerpt from Mark Twain’s
works, a glossary of the Hawaiian language,
and the statements that the part of Lilioki is
taken by ICaneko Liholiko Kamapua, and
that the play is produced by George Barnum.
The entertainment begins with Muriel Starr
in a costume of furniture polish and ensilage,
and ends with Muriel Starr inside the crater
of a volcano. At least, it should end there;
but a slab of English landscape descends into
the crater, and in front of it Muriel Starr
bows her acknowledgments. Doubtless the
volcano is too large, and possibly too modest,
to be led on by Muriel Starr to take the call ;
but the volcano and the lady are all that are
left of quite a large collection of leading men
and missionaries. These mysteriously dis-
appear in the midst of a revolution or some-
thing early in the evening; and nobody knows
what the rebellion was about.
* *» * »
Muriel Starr, the lady in the chocolate
smile with vegetable trimmings, has either no
chance in this uproar or fails to make the
most of her chance. That remembered mag-
netism of voice and manner is lacking; she is
charming and passionate and childish and
petulant, but she doesn’t thrill; still, it must
be rather hard to thrill in such chilly clothes
and in such unsympathetic surroundings.
Frank Harvey is a picturesque beachcomber
in the first act; in the second and subsequent
spasms he is Frank Harvey, struggling hard
to squeeze drama out of vapid dialogue.
Louis Kimball is shaven and sober in the first
act; in the second lie is unshaven and drunk ;
in the third he is shaven and drunk. Yet
people say the bars close at six. James
Hughes is a noble' though rather too well
nourished specimen of naked brown brother ;
and .1. B. Atholwood’s ancient priest is the
real thing. George Bryant and Edwin Lester
make frequent speeches—but then they all do
that! Still, these two make their speeches
more loudly. Maggie Moore is an ancient
brown crone, and a delightfully motherly
one. And Tein Hogue is a pleasant blond
relief against so much furniture polish. Some-
how this critic does not think the Criterion
crater will remain active long.
It’s time he had a show. “The Sorrows of
Satan” will be recorded at Sydney Opera
House on Saturday.
“Betsy Ross,” that spirited little Quaker-
ess, is living her love-story over again in
dumb show at Sydney Grand, and inciden-
tally making the first “Stars and Stripes”
raised in the cause of liberty by Washington.
Israel Putnam, Artemus Ward and eo. in the
days when the best people still drank tea out
of their saucers. Alice Brady plays the part

The right of a hero to sneer—hitherto
strictly the perquisite of a villain —is estab-
lished bv “The Easiest Way,” in which
Muriel Starr has had great success at
Adelaide Royal. Four acts have shown the
heroine going wrong through, as she claims,
overwhelming circumstances. Then, just at
curtain-fall, the hero carefully explains to
her that she is no good, and has taken the
Easiest Way because she has not the moral
fibre to take any other. And. when she
swears to shoot herself on liis going, he
dares her to do it, and stands in the door-
way to watch her fail to do it. It is plucky
and original writing, and finely acted.
Kimball has never done anything better,
and Miss Starr, artistic as of old. is more
physically attractive than she used to be.
But there is somewhat too much just now
of this drama of the going astray of woman-
kind. It may be drawn with cynical _ can-
dor, as in this instance; or with sentimen-
tality, as in the Andersonian “Lured from
Her Home” at the Tivoli; “The Manx-
man,” beautifully (but at too great a
length) screened at West’s; and the
locally-filmed “The Woman Suffers,” still
doing immense business in S.A. towns; or
with the moral and didactic touch, as in
“Enlighten Thy Daughter,” now at West’s.
It is only fair to add that the public seems
to like it, and crowded houses are the result
all round. Fred Bluett at the Majestic
brings in some welcome comic relief, and
Le Blanc continues to be the same old Jew
in differing surroundings. As for _ the
Humouresks at Queen’s Ilall, their business
is not to make you think but to amuse, and
they do it effectively. And, in plain
romance, one has William Hart (with horse
and “gun”) at Wondergraph, and a Muni-
tion girl (with aeroplane) at the I*av.

“the Bing Boys Are Here” at Melbourne
Majesty’s, with the reputation of 15 weeks’
run in Sydney. If a suspicion of disap-
pointment haunted the crowded audience last
Saturday night it was due to the much-
advertised length of the Sydney run. People,
as a rule, looked for something more than a
revue or vaudeville show, even though they
didn’t expect a sustained musical comedy
with a backbone of human interest. The
Ring Roys—Leslie Holland and Jack Can-
not —are neither here nor there in the mat-
ter of human interest, nor is there any
reasonable connection between the Minnie
Love of the first panel and six various edi-
tions de luxe of Minnie that follow in undue
course. The attractions oi the entertain-
ment are its never-failing brightness,
rapidity of movement, excellence in dancing
items, and fine spectacular effects of scenery,
lighting, dresses and girls. All the parts ot
the dresses are adequately filled, and the con-
tents of innumerable stockyigs are an mvari-
able success. One prefers to forget that the
bill describes “The Bing Boys Are Here’ as
a “picture of London Life in 11 panels.
London life has changed for the gloomier
since the revue was written.
* * * *
Minnie Love is an admirable chief lady
for an affair of this sort, because her dainty
spirit of gaietv has a wide range of expres-
sion, and her personality is appealing. What
she lacks in singing ability she makes up for
in charm of manner. Any sort of glittering
costume harmonises with her dazzling smile,
and Minnie ever seems of more actual import-
ance than her clothes. She even holds her
own as central figure in the final Chinese
Lacquer Scene, where she squats on a vast
cushion whilst lines of Chin Chow girls pass
up and down ladders in the background,
keeping time with her ditty. Hilda Quiver’s
strongest item is the Doll Girl song in Panel
5, in which she does fair justice to the
melody, but distinctly misses the sentiment.
Clyde Copk and Maie Baird are associated in
a strikingly artistic acrobatic dance, and
Cook has a grotesque dance to himself. Jack
Quinlan’s Firelight Fancies are the excuse
for Panel 3, and winsome Nellie Payne is an
occasional joy. Also Florrie Allen does two
or three things, including a song on a swing,
which is a somewhat worn form of entertain-
ment. As for Holland and Cannot in the
name parts—the latter is his own usual com-
bination of bags, gags and jollity, whilst
the former revives recollections of his Peter
Doody. The most humorous item in his
bunch of tricks is his parody of a “character
impersonator,” but Holland seems hardly the
right man for that particular sort of fool-
ing. Next Monday afternoon Fighting Mac
will deliver an address at Melb. Majesty s
on his war experiences, and some of the Bing
Boys and Girls will also assist the Red Cross
cause. A shilling admits to any part of the
house.

This is the third week of the first edition
of “Samples” at Melbourne Tivoli, where
the performance just now is twice daily.
Next Saturday, presumably, a few fresh
Samples will be substituted for some that
are now being shown.
The resumption of vaudeville at Mel-
bourne Bijou is being bruited abroad by
divers instruments of music. Tom Rees is
anchored to a trombone that blows holes in
the strongest ragtime measure; whilst the
two Bentleys smite an instrument that
seems to be the giant son of a xylophone
and sounds as if it is heartily ashamed of
its father. Also there is a Miss Veronica
who tickles the piano in a tender spot,
helped by a fugitive from the hairdresser
who scrapes a fiddle and needs only ability
to become a maestro. The people who rely
on their own sterling attributes without
artificial aids are Eddie Burns, a capital
wooden-mat dancer, Con Moreni, monologue
entertainer, and Skipper Francis, the proud
author of “Australia Will Be There.” On
the acrobatic plane Rose and Dell, trick
bikists, have the stage to themselves.
“The Rainbow,” produced at Melb. Royal
last Saturday, has no promise of durability
in its name, which is rather far-fetched, by
the way. There is a moral in “The Rain-
bow,” to the effect that when a grass-
widower of long standing would like to re-
sume calm domestic life with his wife and
young daughter he finds himself horribly
embarrassed by a circle of strictly disrespect-
able friends. A lot of dialogue on this point
would be more effective if it didn't suggest
little sermons learned by heart. Charles
Waldron, as Neil Sumner, the sadly mis-
understood husband, is a too obvious imper-
sonator of a Beautiful Nature struggling
against circumstances over which his absen-
tee wife has much control. He poses as he
preaches, and his sister (Emma Temple)
preaches also, but in a less picturesque style.
They remind one of how Aubrey Tanqueray
and his old friend Mrs. Cortelyon might have
discussed the unfitness of Paula and her
former associates to breathe the same atmo-
sphere as “saint” Ellean. But the situation
here has been created by artificial means.
Virtuous Mrs. Sumner casts off her husband
because she thinks he led her dear brother
into temptation and trouble, whereas her
dear, unpleasant brother is a notorious rotter
who has embezzled her fortune and whose
sins are being hidden from her by the hus-
band who pays his debts. Such things are
impossible outside the world of novelette fic-
tion that women writers have created for
women readers. Feminine sentimentality
delights in long-drawn-out misunderstandings
that suppose restrained tenderness on the
part of a fpolish wife, and preposterous
chivalry on the part of her suffering hus-
band. The truth is revealed, and she for-
gives him her mistake in the last chapter.
* * * *
Katlilene Macdonell contributes a convinc-
ing sketch of ingenuous gilt-edged girlhood
to this poor little comedy, and Beryl Bryant
is often suggestive of a straight-laced spouse
who might desert her husband on slight prov-
ocation. She has purity in her tones and
heaven in her eye, so to speak, but she hasn’t
the deportment of spotless middle-age. Eily
Malyon acts the character of a somewhat
raffish Jane better than she looks it, and
Kenneth Brampton (the beloved, objection-
able brother), Redgrave, Styan, Wilson and
Cambourne are among others that also run.
Cambourne’s caricature of an American con-
sul in France, who knows no French and
can’t make a speech in American, seems a
bad advt. for Australia’s belated ally in the
war. The scenic accessories, which include
two interiors with several doors in them, are
more than good enough for the piece.

The Hum a n
Female is the sub-
ject of conversa-
tion in “The Mad-
ras House,” staged
by the Gregan
McMahon Reper-
tory Co., at Mel-
bourne Playhouse
on Saturday. The
argument is carried
over four acts and
is a wordy elabora-
tion of every pos-
sible view, ranging
from conventional
sex-worship to the
philosophic anti-
feminism of Shaw,
Nietzsche and P>ax.
The latter side of the case is presented
by Constantine Madras, who scorns the
enormous waste in dressmaking - , mani-
cure, perfume and accomplishments em-
ployed to enhance Woman’s fraudulent
place in society. His views drive him to
embrace the faith of Mohammed. Accord-
ingly he abandons his missus and an amour
which threatens a baby, ijmd clears out to
Arabia. Constantine is only a detail a
play containing enough discourse for 11-
characters ; but he is so god-like in the ex-
pression of his ideas that he was probably
made in the likeness of his creator. At any
rate, he attained mental ascendancy through
Gregan McMahon, who talked his audacities
with the dialectical nonchalance of a Shawian
Super-Nut training for a discussion in the
Fabian Society. McMahon gave a further
emphasis to Madras’s posturings with a
delightful make-up in which the influences
of Armenian and Baptist ancestors had pro-
duced a cross between a church elder and a
Khalif of old Bagdad. Constantine’s son,
Philip, maintained an attitude to his wife
which revealed a strain of paternal senten-
tiousness with a stronger clash of intellec-
tual brutality. Major Thomas, who is mostly
kissing in haste and repenting at leisure,
was a melancholy human document covered
with noughts and crosses by Helen Daniel!.
Thomas Skewes deprived Henry Huxtable
and the audience of considerable repose, but
all the same he didn’t miss the humor of
Henry’s vulgarity. Mrs. Edith Eccles and
six others represented the superfluous rein
nant of the Huxtable family, and Miss Allie
Robson played Philip’s wife, Jessica, with
smiles and jewellery. For the rest, there
were Mrs. Fannie Fowler discontentedly
snivelling as Mrs. Constantine; Norman
Carter impressively declaiming the raptures
of Eustace State; Mrs. J. E. Mackey as an
edition de luxe of Mrs. Grundy; and Audrey
Anderson defiantly explaining Marion Yates's
baby. Marion’s expected offspring had some
small bearing on the plot, but the frequency
of this sort of infant threatens to turn the
Playhouse into a Foundling Hospital.

A Melbourne art show; —
The show of the Australian Arts Club is solid and
decorous, except where Norman Lindsay hangs his
sections of Girl. Norman is more gleeful than
ever in hia keyhole confidences. Also his color has
become richer and more sensitive to the charms of
flesh, and in pictorial description is like the purple
vocabulary of a Huysmans or a Wilde. His etchings,
“Don Juan in Hell” and “The Lady with a Fan,”
show that he has found another outlet for his pagan
fancy. Lionel Lindsay’s performances are mostly
in water-color, but he has employed his needle on
brother Norman’s visage and ticked off the contours
of a well-fed girl. Hans Heysen’s worship for the
Australian gum surges in rapturous mixtures of
timber and sunlight, but he is just as entertaining
when he changes the subject to a ploughman or a
hilltop covered with sheep. W. B. Mclnnes has
dyed the landscape olive-green and has covered some
of the richest country with a sort of radiant gloom.
Nevertheless the combined result is very high and
adds extra celebrity to the exhibition. Etcher Sydney
Ure Smith is the old taking Ure of wood and drawer
of water in his studies of buildings and harborside
wharves. IV. J. Macnally has found the woodscape
erecting itself into a series of triumphal arches, and
relies on lavishness of detail to secure economy of
effect. All the same, he is an improved colorist and
would be a greater success if he took more risks in
his choice of views. There is manifest quality in the
seaside impressions of Elliott Gruner, but the
ordinary bloke will consider that he is lending a new
distinction to monotony. And really it looks as if
he finds it hard to extricate his meaning from the
swirling pigment. John Shirlow is stronger in etch-
ing facades than faces, whilst Percy Leason’s art in
“The Farm” is only colored photography. Albert
Collins’s temperament, which is subdued in the
achievement of some enjoyable little landscapes, gets
out of control in a bilious vision of a lady with a
lantern. The Norman Carter panel is a moderately
gifted summary of delicate times o£ day, most
arresting in his sketches around the Kurrajong
heights. James R. Jackson sprinkles sunlight on a
couple of ladies without keeping the eye from wan-
dering elsewhere. G. V. F. Mann’s set of smaller
canvases might be memoranda for larger productions,
but they are all painted in a convincing manner.
Lloyd ltess, J. D. Banks, the two Clints and H. M.
Burton fill in the remaining wall space with some
modest efforts.
The 13th and 45th dames, after long weeks
of preparation, bring their fete to a head this
Friday. The raid begins—Sargent’s Rooms,
Market-street, is the address —in the after-
noon with Lady Davidson to open the sale.
And you can guess who —the benevolent
being at the head of the firm—has been very
kind.
For the first time the Maoriland Alliance
for Sweeping Derrink out of the Country
has agreed to talk cash to Bung. It last
week announced that the time was ripe to
take a poll for determining by bare majority
whether the islands should be wet or dry,
Bung to collect a dividend if suppressed.
This seems to suggest either a remarkable
conversion or that pourparlers have been
going on in a cool cellar. Hitherto organised
resistance to the Alliance has derived its
greatest strength from considerations relat-
ing to compensation, Bung being a long, way
more concerned about his pocket than his
customer's thirst. So the future of the pint
in M.L. is, to say the least of it, precarious.

“Umntu” : Our mascots —we gave Orchard,
the new Minister for Recruiting, a chameleon
the other day—are not the real thing. Either
we confuse a mascot with a pet, and desire it
because it is charming and not because it is
a charm, or we commercialise the thing and
dissolve the charm by spreading it over
millions of examples. The Red Indians knew
the rules better. A mascot is a rare thing, so
rare that there is only one of it. The Indians
supplied Napoleon with a true mascot, and
that was why he was able to stalk through
Europe and conquer his enemies. It was the
single joint of the vertebra* of a sea-serpent.
It was the hereditary fortune of an Indian
family, all the males of which had died out.
and the talisman only acted in the hands of
a man. Some Russians landed on Vancouver
with French prisoners. The Indians gave
the mascot to the Frenchmen to take to
Napoleon. It enabled the Frenchmen to over-
come their captors, and they eventually
reached France and handed the great gift to
the little man of Elba. Then he became
irresistible, and would have become Emperor
of the World but by the rottenest piece of
bad luck he lost the precious bone just before
the battle of Waterloo.
* * # »
A week or two ago two prisoners tried to
escape from Pentridge prison and failed, and
ever since the Vic. penal authorities have
been floundering in red-tape. As the outcome
of correspondence, Freeman, Inspector-
General of Prisons, held an inquiry with
closed doors and sent a report to Callaway,
the Under-Secretary. Callaway's job is to
initial the report and post it to Morrison,
Public Service Commissioner. Morrison is
then going to inquire some more; in public
this time, and “assisted” by numerous Gents
One representing the warders, the Crown
Solicitor and anybody else who feels disposed
to provide a fee. After this, somebody will
probably be told not to do it again, and yet
another inquiry will be held, before a P.M.,
to decide what shall happen to the prisoners.
Then the public will get a look in. But its
job is very simple—merely to foot the bill.
* * * *
“W.R.H.”: While camped near the Pools
of Siloam, in the vicinity of Bethlehem,
parties from our brigade were given an
opportunity to visit the Church of the
Nativity and other show places in the town.
Close to the Church is the Grotto of the
Milk. Tradition has it that this was the
place to which the Virgin Mary adjourned
when she wished to suckle the Child. The
interior of the place is composed of a soft
milky-colored stone which when pulverised is
reputed to increase the flow of milk. This
fact is impressed on all visitors and a little
of the stone is invariably handed round by
the attendant. A party of Billjims had lis-
tened attentively to this recital for some
time, when one of them exclaimed: “Cripes,
wouldn’t the cow cockies rush this if they
knew about it!”
* * * *
“Pin” : On our way to the wars last De-
cember we put in at Dakkar, the chief
French port on the western coast of Africa,
just above “the line.” Quite close to us a
liner was anchored and we could
see the stewards flying about
with liquors, while we had noth-
ing better than warm ginger-pop.
Naturally a great thirst arose,
and nagged exceedingly. Pre-
sently one of the mob, naked ex-
cept for a pair of breeks, plunged
into the sea, and in a few strokes
was at the companion-way of the
floating pub. Amidst cheers he
ran up the steps and along to the
stern of the ship, where some pas-
sengers were tickling a tray of
long, cool drinks. With water
dripping from him he looked the
picture of happiness as he lifted
a glass and, turning to us, grin-
ning all over, made a silent toast
before drinking. Next minute the
sea was alive with men in all
sorts of impromptu swimming
togs. Dozens got aboard the Good
Samaritan before the “heads”
tumbled to the performance. Then
a piequet was hastily formed,
and went over to close the scene of joy.

Here is a curious point of law or logic.
The Musicians’ Union charges its one-time
friend, the Melbourne Eight Hours Proces-
sion, with something like “scabbing,” because
it decided that unionists, even if they don’t
belong to the Musicians’ combination, may
play in its ranks on the annual gala day.
There are arguments on both sides. It seerhs
RECOLLECTIONS.
THE ARTIST (proudly) : “Did you ever sec anything like that before?”
THE OTHER: “ Er —let me see. Yes, I saw that design on a tank in France."
hard that a union bricklayer shouldn’t be
allowed to blow his own cornet in his own
demonstration on a holiday, yet if he found
a professional cornet-player laying bricks on
a Good Friday he might object. Of course
the man in the procession isn’t blowing for
hire, but then it wouldn’t improve things
much if the cornet-player laid bricks gratis.
Assuredly there are a certain number of
bricks that must be laid, so the cornet-player
would be doing another man out of a job,
while there isn’t any fixed amount of music
that must be blown, so an amateur might
clow a sample or two without depriving any
other man of a crust. And the bricklayer
probably says that the cornet-player couldn’t
lay bricks decently if he tried, to which the
cornet-player possibly replies that the brick-
layer can’t make music and only thinks he
can. To some extent it is a struggle between
Art and Materialism. Music properly
played is capable of arousing the highest and
noblest emotions of which the soul is capa-
ble ; a brick even if properly laid isn’t. And
soul isn’t a thing to be lightly despised. But
here the tangible bumps against the intangi-
ble. Nobody has seen a soul, while almost
everybody has seen a brick.
A tale of mean streets and small things
was told recently in Sydney Divorce Court.
Here is an epitome of the assertions: —
He.
They were married at Newtown. He had a grown
family and so had she. His wife and her family
assaulted him. His wife’s sons and sons-in-law took
away the furniture. Somebody (possibly his wife’s
grandfather) held him while the furniture was being
taken. His wife used to throw herself down and
roll on the floor. She danced round the table. One
of his wife’s daughters called him a “cow.” Some
female came up behind him and hit him with some-
thing hard.
She.
She now lived at Enfield. There was insufficient
maintenance. He used to cook his food in the stable.
'He threatened to shoot her son. He knocked her
down.
He.
She fell down.
The Court refused the application whatever
it was. The moral is that when two bad-
tempered families live together at Newtown
anything of a cheap description may happen.
And it is much the same at Enfield. It is
difficult to yearn after higher things or soar
to the loftiest heights of art, culture and
imagination at Enfield.

From far Bagdad :
We exiles in Mespot want to know if Armenian or
Kurdish wives would be,allowed into Australia. You
see, we have heard that our fellow p -AussieS in France
and England can get married whenever they feel
that way and have their wives carried to Aussie for
them. If like permission is given us we shall at once
advertise in that all-powerful organ, The Bagdad
Times (one sheet and mostly “This space to let”) :
Handsome Australian owning mangrove plantation
near Sydney would like to make acquaintance of
young lady, Armenian preferred, but Yahudie or
Arab not objected to, with view to matrimony.
Now that Conscription has been heaved out, why
not? An Armenian tincture might improve the
strain.

As members of the Miners’ Federation are
at work they did not pass this resolution,
That the question of the remuneration for services
rendered by members and other conditions of contract
under which these services are given is not one which
the Federation can consent to submit to arbitration.
But members of the British Medical Associa-
tion are on strike in Vic., and it is they who
resolved to this effect. Next time there is
an industrial upheaval and Sawbones, dis-
covering the gas cut off, the electric light ex ■
piring and his kitchen barren of food, wildly
demands to know why the recalcitrant
workers are not forced to submit to arbitra-
tion, recollection of his own truculent atti-
tude on the subject ought to bring comfort
and joy.

OUT OF KHAKI.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I slung me khaki suit to-day.
Civilian now from heel to chin
I ’op round on a single shin;
At home in peace I’m bound to stay,
’N’ so they’ve took me duds away.
It ’urt like strippin’ off me skin !
1 put it on three years ago,
The ole brown rig. There wasn’t then
A prouder chicken in the pen.
Jist twenty turned, me nibs you’d know
For how I give me chest a throw,
A man among the best of men.
Me little mo the touch I give,
Me chin’s ez solid ez a rock,
’X’ level with the Town ’All clock,
A five-inch grin across me chiv.
“Lor’ love us, this is how to live,”
Sez I, ’n’ felt I owned the Block.
Glad eyes was ever on the lurk,
’N’ little ’earts was thumpin’ warm
For nippers trainin’ with the swarm
To swat ole Kaiser Bill, or work
A toe-hold on the heathen Turk.
Fair dink, I loved the uniform !
I soused mine in the brine that day
When all ’Ell spilt, ’n’ in the roar
Of shells that split the sea ’n’ tore
Our boats to chips, we broke away
Up through the pelt of leaden spray,
’N’ got our first real taste of war.
They shot me tunic all to rags;
Then in the perpendie’lar spree
Me trousers wore off to the knee.
The right-abouts of many bags
Was ground off in the dust ’n’ crags
A-sittin’ in Gallipoli.
I wore the khaki on the Somme —
Most time ’twas jist a coat of mud ;
I once come through the battle scud
Stripped mother-naked by a bomb ;
’N’ once it took its color from
Me own ’n’ one good cobber’s blood.
They cheered the khaki through the street
When we come home with pipers gay,
But now I’m jist a bloke in grey.
Harf-lost, lob-sided, incomplete,
It’s nothin’ but me spook you’ll meet,
Ghost-walkin’ in the light o’ day.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

The Hun isn’t wholly deaf to the voice
of freedom. For instance, the conquered
provinces of Alsace-Lorraine have enjoyed
the blessings of representative institutions for
48 years. That is to say, the Kaiser appoints
the' Statthalter. The Statthalter appoints
the Ministers. They and a number of mem-
bers directly appointed by the Kaiser com-
prise the Council of State. The Statthalter
presides over the Council of State. Alsace-
Lorraine has also four members in the Im-
perial Upper House. They are nominated
by the Kaiser’s nominee, the Statthalter, and
have no votes.

“P.X.”: Though Johanna’s idea of
attaching an orchestra, a “producer” and
other theatrical properties to the Federal
Parliament hasn’t been adopted, Ministers
have gone this far: like other theatrical stars,
they are hiring press agents to boost them.
Carmichael, who is whooping up a thousand
recruits, was the first Australian Minister
to recognise that a
trained pressman could
do as much for a politi-
cian as for any other
play-actor or circus, and
with cheek enough to hire
him to do it at the public
expense. Like the em-
ployment of motor-cars
the idea was wolved; and
now if you look you’ll
find a press agent on the
staff of most Ministers,
sometimes camouflaged,
oftener quite naked and
unashamed. Hughes has
hitherto hired claquers
only for special jobs. He
borrowed one from J. C.
Williamson, Ltd., to go
with him to England in
1916; and to him Aus-
tralia is mainly indebted
for having its leg pulled
so hard over the little
man’s visit. Subsequently
he engaged other press
agents for other seasons,
such as the first and sec-
ond Referendums. Now,
however, he has a per-
manent booster from the
Argus on the premises;
and E. Y. Robinson,
another man from the
same paper, has been
attached to the Repatria-
tion Department. It would be only a
fair thing now, since Parliament pays
the Opposition leader a salary, to hire
also professional cursers. Certainly the
public would be better served by them than
by the professional glozers.
**. * *
Four years in a fairly-comfortable gaol
was the sentence passed at Bundaberg (Q.)
by mild and very old and rather perfunctory
Justice' Real on two German aliens
who cheerfully admitted that they had
been setting cane crops on fire. They
might have got 14 years but didn’t;
and they mav win their release after a month
or so, for Hun influence is strong in Queens-
land. Perhaps it was ignorance, or perhaps
it was lack of a railway fare, that prevented
these two Fritzes coming south and setting
fire to .omething much larger and more valu-
able than standing cane. Or, again, it may
have been the horrible thought that they
might meet his Honor Pring in N. S. Wales.
It certainly was a lot wiser to stick to his
Honor Real.
* * * *
There is a snub the size of an elephant
due to fall on the Australian Council of
Churches. That well-meaning body has by
resolution asked the similar organisations
in the U.S.A. and Canada to combine with
Australia to “protest against the facilities
for obtaining alcoholic liquors by soldiers
in England and France,” and generally to
urge John Bull and Joan of Arc to a more
stringent control of their liquor traffic, “in
the interests of the soldiers and of the home
life to which they will return.” The inevit-
able reply will be a polite inquiry whether
Billjim isn’t further from alcohol in England
and France than he will be when he returns
to the “home life” that has a pub at the
corner and a few bottles of poison m a
thousand places less desirable.

“The Gunyah”: When a soldier is hit
by a shell it is customary, in order the better
to observe and bandage the wounds, to cut
off the clothes from the victim. I was bunco
(prematurely) at Bullecourt and the usual
procedure was adopted. In the pockets my
wallet, etc., were left in the dug-out were
French coins to the value of 00
francs. When I was ready to
leave the hospital fresh clothes
were issued, iny wallet, etc.,
handed back, and orders given for
my return to Australia. 1 arrived,
and while I was hobbling along
Swanston-street (Melb.) the
other day a returned Anzac
tapped me on the shoulder with:
“Hullo, matey, wasn’t you at
Bullecourt?” On my replying
“Yes” he said : “Here’s three quid
I owe you.” “Not me, old sport,”
I replied. He said: “Don’t you
remember me? Why, I’m the
bloke that dug you out at Bulle-
court.” On mv replying “Well,
suppose you did, where does the
three quid come in ?” He said:
“There was 90 francs in your
pockets, but I lost sight of you in
the stunt.” The three quid came
in handy—we split it between
us. I didn’t know the hero before, but I
know him now! He has only one leg, too.
* * * *
“Copy Boy”: When Labor was in power
it was an Argus habit to blither of “Lygon-
street government” ; but although the Trades
Hall might have been a hatchery for party
political schemes it was never the venue of
Executive acts that the Melbourne Club has
become. It was here that the move to throw
out Peacock’s financial proposals was de-
cided on and that Irvine was offered the
Chief Justiceship. It was also the scene of
a recent meeting of the State Executive
Council. Budd, who murdered a woman at
Port Melbourne, was to have been hanged
on Foundation Day. Protests against the
inappropriateness of the date were made
and the Cabinet resolved on a postponement.
At the Executive Council meeting the mat-
ter was overlooked. Governor Stanley had
adjourned to the Club, so a couple of Minis-
ters who are also members of the exclusive
Tory junta, bustled down to the Collins-
street capitol and another Executive Coun-
cil meeting was held and the postponement
arranged. Pressmen trying to get hold of a
State Minister’s decision on a matter of
urgency are asked to “See me at the Club
after lunch,” and the impression has gained
ground that the decision arrived at depends
to some extent on advice tendered at the
luncheon table.
In Canada an order-in-Couneil has been
passed to the etfect that no Canadian shall
accept an hereditary title. So in future the
Canadian will have to arrange that his title
shall be thrust upon him against his will. A
good many are said to have happened
that way. Incidentally, the Chinese had
(perhaps still have) an idea whereby, when
a man deserved well of the State, he might
be ennobled, and his ancestors for as many
generations as his merit demanded. The sons
of Han have thoughts that soar beyond
“spling callot.” It is safer to ennoble the
tenth great-grandfather than the grandson.
And it is more dignified to be the last of a
hundred earls than a jumped-up peer without
a past and with great doubts about the next
peer’s future.
“Kopai”: My friend in the Railway
Offices, Spencer-street (Melb.), smiled when
I talked of the Wynne disclosures. “Dis-
closures, I don’t think!” he said. “We in-
side the service could make the disclosures
if we liked !” He smiled again when he men-
tioned Johnson, the imported expert. In
fact, he grew reminiscent about Johnson.
One of the legacies of the Tait speeding-up
and efficiency regime was a herd of engines
with, as he neatly put it, the “insides driven
out of ’em” during the hustle-period. They
had been laid up ever since in a shed at
North Melbourne. When Johnson’s inspec-
tion became imminent there was frequent
and stealthy movement by dawn and dusk
about that engine shed, and the derelicts
drifted away to sidings and dead-ends on
remote and weed-grown wayback stations.
There they rusticated until the inspector
passed on. Johnson was rather eulogistic
about the management of the department.
There was nothing wrong about the stage-
management, anyhow.

A POET’S EOVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Base in the world’s eyes is the wretch
Who when he kisses tells ;
But poets must have bread and meat:
Verse packed with sighs and kisses sweet
Is still the sort that sells ;
So, shamelessly, I write of how
I kissed Miranda on the brow.
I’ve sung my love for Lalage
And earned a fortnight’s rent ;
Bold Bertha, with the wicked eye,
Helped me some furniture to buy.
An ev’ning that I spent
With Mabel, when reduced to verse,
Put fifteen shillings in my purse.
Of how I cuddled Dorothy,
And flirted with Chlorine,
And dallied for a while with Ruth,
I've written; but the painful truth
Makes me look still more mean.
The plain, unvarnished fact is this —■
Although 1 tell, I do not kiss I

MELBOURNE & SYDNEY.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
“Ato”: The public will never know all
that “Jimmie Pannikin” did to make little
Australians better Australians. For instance,
1 he induced hundreds of bush children in the
Outback to put about, for the birds, numer-
ous jamtins, which they filled with water on
their way to school.
“N.F.S.”: So “Jimmie Pannikin’s” dead!
He was the ideal school-inspector. When
lie entered a bush school there blew in with
him a great breeze of alertness and vigor.
He exuded cheeriness and good humor. He
laughed and joked and talked bush-lore from
his arrival to his departure. He stimulated
with the essence of the joy o’ life lonely, dis-
pirited teachers and cow-tired children. His
visits were joyous holidays to the pupils,
doses of tonic to the instructors. He loved
to call himself a Big Australian, and a Big
Australian he always was to us bush peda-
gogues. He battled with an indifferent
Department for the introduction of Aus-
tralian literature into the public schools,
even arguing in private and public that, to
Australian children, Lawson was greater
than Milton. He was the champion of the
child against both the inhuman parent and
the type of teacher who uses his pupils as
counters to get promotion. And he was ever
a big brother to us poorly-paid, intellectu-
ally-starved pedagogues of the bush. A great
man has passed from our midst.
From the Gallery :
The prize for vociferousness in the House of Reps,
is divided between two new members—Nicholls,
representing Macquarie (N.S.W.), and Jowett, the
wool baron from the Grampians (Vie.). The crude
Luborism of Mr. Nicholls is voiced with thunder, the
softest sotto voce whereof is like the noise of a pass-
ing train. lie has not the slightest notion of tone
or melody, but pours out his griefs and aspirations
in unregulated quantities of sound made the more
appalling by a malevolent fluency. Jowett is hardly
less deafening, and when he “ventures to say, sir”—
he ventures to say, sir, once in every sentence—even
Nicholls can hardly hear himself speak.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: There is at
Haberfiekl (N.S.W.) an unquestionable cen-
tenarian or thereabouts, John Butler Wood,
born at Parramatta in February, 1819. At
16 he went to the Grenfell district, and later
became manager of Brundah Creek station,
owned by his father; eventually he became
the owner and passed it on to his sons. The
discovery of gold at Emu Creek and Weddin
Mountains played havoc for a while with the
squatters in the locality, but wheat-farming
and the development of the share system
helped the third generation of the Woods to
success.
Eighteen candidates have come forward
for Irvine’s old constituency of Flinders,
among them five lawyers and four returned
soldiers. One of the former is Tom
Brennan, once cable sub. and chief-of-staff
of the Argus, now editor of the Catholic
Advocate. He is a brother of Frank
Brennan, M.H.R. The best-known of the
soldiers is Captain Burkett, Federal Inspec-
tor of Recruiting. The political trainers
will do some sifting before the field faces the
starting gate.
Tetley Grant was
educated at St. John’s,
Oxford, early last cen-
tury. He is a typical
old English family
lawyer and Conserva-
tive politician. Elected
to the Speck’s House
of Dodery in 1001, he
has hung to the job
ever since, and been
President since 1907,
succeeding the beauti-
fully-named William
Dodery, who gave the
House its title. But
Grant has put in his
best work as Chancel-
lor of the little Uni-
versity of Tasmania.
In that office and else-
where aestheticism is
his strong point, and
he takes almost as
much interest in the
color of the wallpaper
as in what goes on in
the room.
TETLEY GRANT,
President of the Tas.
Legislative Council.
Politics run strongly in families in S. Aus.
At the recent general election the young
sons of three ex-Premiers were candidates —
Price, Verran and Butler. Price and Verran
(Labor) won; Butler (Coalition) was
defeated. Verran is the only one who didn’t
do the same as father. The old man voted
“Yes” and Labor crucified him. The son
stuck to the party that did it.
At 70 Judge Docker is a
marvel of physical energy. Recently he
was one of the regulars on the Blackheath
tennis court and played a fairly good game,
too. Five years ago, when the Little Brown
Jug was in the Western District (N.S.W.),
he struck Forbes during a hot spell. Though
the temperature was over the century, when
his court rose Docker toddled down to the
tennis courts, sentenced himself to his cus-
tomary number of sets, and finished very
pink, but fit. Then he put his head under
the tap and skipped back to his pub.
ACTIVE SERVICE.
ANNIE: "I ’m givin' yer notice, mum. I know
you engaged me as a general, mum, but
I’ve got so much to do that by the time
I’ve finished one day’s work it’s time to
start gettin’ breakfast for the next, an’
even the best of us, which is General
’Aig, ’as to retire sometimes.”
The time is approaching when Arthur
Robinson, M.L.C., Victoria’s new Solicitor-
General, will be shoved upon the Bench. A
pet of the A.W.N.L. these many years, he
belongs to the type of politician which read-
ily furnishes into a prim, voluble and plati-
tudinous bencher. Ilis dialectic manner is
as dry as sixpenn’orth of seed-peas. Also he
jests with difficulty, and has never been
known to break into mirth upon the plat-
iorm. Perhaps he may hardly be fat enough
yet for a judicial job, but a year or two on
a selected diet should ripen him. The late
C.J. Madden, by the way, held the Solici-
tor-Generalship for a time —the job which
gave Robinson the right to assist in obliging
the Phewsion by appointing Irvine as Mad-
den’s successor.
“Cherokee”: Though Frederick Bayley
Deeming was hanged and quicklimed 24 years
ago, he still lives in memory. When it became
known that, on April 12, a quantity of
bones had been found under a hearthstone
at Windsor (Vic.), where once lived that
marrier in haste and cementer at leisure, the
sleuths took up the old trail with avidity.
Two of them, accompanied by, a reporter,
whizzed out to the spot, where they met the
bones carefully laid out for inspection. With
bar and spade the Law raised every stone
in the house, but under only a few were
there any results. These comprised a sheep’s
shank, a bullock’s shin, a sheep’s jaw and
ribs, and a knuckle of ham. Whether
Deeming slew the ham and stealthily mur-
dered the shank in cold blood no one knows.
“0.K.” : W. Ernest Williams, late sergeant
in the A.I.F. and the old-established commer-
cial booster for Melbourne Pauncii, is in the
scramble for Irvine’s seat in the Federal
Parliament, and seems to have as good a
claim as any. He has been resident in the
Flinders electorate since his youth, and was
the district representative at the Boer war.
Besides, he is a local propputy holder in a
small way. When the big war broke out
Williams dropped his Paunch billet as
before, and did his bit at Gallipoli until he
was shot, knocked out of action, and num-
bered with the permanently unfit. He is a
practical patriot with the talking faculty.
In America, not long ago, he was offered a
billet as touring lecturer, which, considering
the fierce competition thereabouts, suggests
that he has some qualifications.

“Mustard Gold”: Westralia has two
hearty old ex-Chief Justices- —Stone and
Parker. Parker’s elastic tread as ho pat-
rols the Terrace daily is not the movement of
age, despite the fine old man’s advanced
years. Two of his sons went to the Front
early. One, who was Crown Prosecutor, will
never come back. Hubert, the other, who
had been practising law at Kalgoorlie, is an
artillery officer. The retirement of Parker
left the bench dour and hard, save for occa-
sional gleams from Burnside. McMillan,
present Chief, is always the judge, weighing
the pros and cons, knowing nothing of ex-
terior matters and a stranger to the general
public. Northmore is heavy and undistin-
guished, looking like a man who would take
his food with proper solemnity, a bib tucked
carefully over the shirt-front. The third
man, Booth, is also of the English pattern,
but breaks ground interestingly at times
vvith decisions which the Appeal Court recti-
fies, and casually indulges in unconventional
opinions. It was he who lately frankly con-
fessed that he wasn’t competent to do the
work of the Arbitration Court, and hinted
that ho didn’t know a judge in the neighbor-
hood who was.
OUTSIDE A PUB IN MARKET-STREET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Outside a pub in Market-street
I saw two blithered Anzacs meet.
One, lurching from the public bar,
Wore on his face, beneath a scar,
A smile benign and beautiful;
And thickly muttering, “Take a pull!”
He stumbled, with a blissful grin,
Against a drunk just going in.
They clutched each other, and among
A world of sober men they clung.
They proudly smiled ; they’d reached all right
The only blithered port in sight.
Then one saw on the other’s sleeve
His old battalion c010r5.... “Steve ! ”
The other solemnly blinked back.
The same old colors. “Who the. .. . Jack !”
“You ain't gorn west?” his cobber said.
“Gord blimey, Jack; so you ain’t dead?”
And swift around these blithered men
All Flanders flooded in again.
And something clicked, and memory
Unlocked as with a secret key
Strange livid landscapes lit at night
By star-shell constellations bright.
JOYNTON SMITH, Mayor of Sydney, hunt-
ing War Loan subscribers.
The street became a battered trench ;
The pub’s aroma was a stench
Of mates unburied, bloodied, wet;
The wall became the parapet.
The City Council’s dust was mud ;
“Wait here for trams” was spattered blood.
The traffic louder grew at once—
The cur-rur-rump of German guns!
A motor-cycle puttered prst—
They ducked beneath the leaden blast!
And peering on the sea of mud,
They muttered, “Christ, another dud!”
In their bleared brains they dwelt again
In distant continents of pain ;
Once more explored the under-side
Of human souls where fears abide;
Once more they saw the splendid whole
Of simple man's immortal soul.
And these disgraceful, drunken men
AVere fearless heroes once again ;
They breathed a clean, more spacious air ;
And manhood, pride and faith were there.
They smiled a fatuous smile because
These mates were back where mateship was.
A spinster passed; she made a face.
Disgraceful in a public place !
It is not beer that troubles these:
They’re drunk on nobler memories;
These most immoral, shocking sights
Have stormed and stood on nobler heights
Of manhood, mateship, than you know.
Salute these drunkards ere you go!
They sobered up ; they saw the street;
They disentangled all their feet.
The unfamiliar place they scanned ;
AVhy, here they were in No Man’s Land !
Two blithered mates, by ill-luck hurled
Into a sober wowser-world ;
And round them like a nagging sin
Sober reality closed in.
Those colors told them they were men,
And mateship called to them again:
They winked a most sagacious wink
And went and had another drink.
x.s.w.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

Aug., ’17..19,679 14 „ ~ 14,700 4,979 277,353
Feb., ’lB.-20,750 14 „ ~14,840 5,910 283,263
The Commercial, Tasmania’s one bank now
that the little National has been absorbed
by the C. B. of A., has some of the most
solid business of the island ; and very profit-
able business it has been lately. You can
guess that from these figures:—
1913. 1917. 1918.
Notes .. £7,630 £4,577 £4,427
Deposits . . 1,878,870 1,872,300 2,107,817
Liquid assets .. 607,560 770,759 1,112,701
Advances . . 1,632,212 1,538,214 1,437,556
Premises . . 54,039 59,178 60,091
Jam, timber, copper, tin, wool, stud sheep
and Australian visitors who can’t go further
afield—all have been yielding fat profits. So
deposits have been growing, and as new
industries haven’t been springing up every-
where to call for overdrafts, advances have
been actually shrinking. Nevertheless the
bank has been able to earn very good inter-
est for £250,000 of its surplus money in
British and Australian war loans; also in
these days when many, perhaps most,
traders are making whopping profits, they
haven’t haggled at an extra 1 or 2 p.c. for
temporary accommodation. Hence the direc-
tors feel justified in hinting to shareholders
at the good time they are having by disclos-
ing a larger profit than any in the table —or
that would be in the table if it were three
times as long.
Tasmania’s boom hasn’t been due to Com-
monwealth War Loan money to the same
extent as that of several mainland States.
The briskness of the Speck’s trade has been
referable mainly to production : it has been
selling things and getting cash for them. But
from the tax-gatherer’s point of view that
isn’t going to make any difference. Jones,
who has £50,000 in a bank in Tasmania as
the result of selling—well, say, jam—is no
better off than Smith, who has £50.000 in a
bank in Sydney as the result of selling im-
ported goods to people who have got the
money from War Loan. Both are going to
be bled presently to pay interest and a sink-
ing fund on the War debt. There certainly
will be this difference, though : the communi-
ties which have been fluffing it most on War
Loan money will feel the worst bump pre-
sently when War Loan money ceases to be
spent. On the whole, then, Tasmanian assets
will probably slump rather less than those of
some of the mainland States. For there is
going to be a slump: the stoppage of War
Loan expenditure, the demand for much
heavier taxation, and the more or less sudden
revision of the war-inflated prices for staple
products make that practically certain. No
doubt the banks are preparing for it by
keeping a tight rein on advances and stow-
ing something away inside. Those that are
not doing these things are liable to be hurt.
This bank’s position is very solid : much
more so than it was a few years ago. There
are now liquid assets (including of course
public securities') for no less than 53 p.c.
of all the liabilities; and for the almost
exact million of liabilities not represented
by liquid assets there were on February 28
£1,497,000 of other assets. This exceptional
strength, from the depositors’ point of view,
is due partly to the fact that so large a
proportion of the assets are liquid, and
partly to the other fact that the public’s
money in the business is equal to only £4
10s. for every £1 of the shareholders’ funds.
The visible reserves are now much in excess
of the capital: on the total funds the divi-
dend is actually under 6 p.c.
* * * *
London Stores, Ltd., which bought a
men’s outfitting business in Melbourne, and
started with various goodwill and other in-
tangible items representing £26,368 of the
capital:—
Written To Re-
Feb., Profits. Dividends. Off. serves. Deficit.
1911.. £26,368
1912.. £7,293 Bp.c.= £4200 £3,093 23,275
£1666 added to goodwill items .. 24,941
1913.. 9,702 8 p.c.= 5600 £1596 2,506 22.435
1914.. 11,003 8 „ „ 5600 704 4,699 17,736
1915.. 15,132 9 ~ ~ 6300 645 8,187 9.549
1916.. 15,134 10 „.. 7000 604 7,530 2,019
Reserves.
1917.. 18,330 10 „ „ 7000 549 10.781 8.762
1918.. 19,040 10 „ „ 7000 496 11,544 20,306
It has been very nice for London Stores,
Ltd.; but it hasn’t been healthy. The Gov-
ernment has been printing notes and other
lOU’s, and this plethora of paper has pro-
duced such an artificial prosperity that traders,
observing that in some lines it was just
as easy to get £5 as £4, have naturally asked
for the £5. Hence the profits of this co. last
year were 75 p.e. more than in the year
before the war. Gross profits then were
£47.532; last year they were £67.740, or
£20,208 more. Meantime general charges
advanced from £36.529 to £49,120. an in-
crease of only £12,591; and a good deal of
that is income tax. Later on, of course, the
lOU’s will have to be met; but it does not
by any means follow that the people who
have got the lOU’s will have to redeem them.
Hundreds of thousands of people will have
no more assets at the end of the war than
they had at the beginning; yet they will have
to do their share in wiping off the debt. The
profit that these and other people are making
has a string to it; but that string will pull
comparatively little of it back.
Last year’s profit, after writing down
plant and providing for a sinking fund to
return the small amount of capital spent on
leasehold, was equal to just a shade under 25
p.c.; and as the profit for the previous three
years wasn’t much less, the co. has got very
nearly all its capital back in the period. In
the four years the co. has paid 39 p.c. in divi-
dends ; and meantime, while the increase in
cash and book debts outweighs the increase
in liabilities, no less than £45,000 has been
added to the stocks. Put it this way: in the
four years, after paying the dividend of 10
p.c. for the past three years and 9 p.c. for
the first year of the period, the co. has main-
tained its liquid assets and paid for £45.000
of stock out of profits. It is an almost amass-
ing performance on a capital of £70.000.
Going back to 1912, the end of the co.’s first
year, we get these comparisons :
1942. 1916. 1917. 1918.
Sundry creditors ..£7,310 £4,754 £5,410 £6,773
Sundry debtors . . 3,257 3,620 4,472 4.803
Stock 31.237 52.885 66,248 78.596
Plant and fittings . . 5,624 5,432 4,910 4.474
Cash, etc 14.728 14,805 12,547 15,212
Even with the public throwing its paper
around this couldn’t have been done on a
capital of £70,000 (of which, to start with,
only £40.000 was tangible) but for two
things: the co. put nothing into bricks and
mortar; and the rule has been strict cash-
trading. That a co. with such a turnover
should have only £4803 on its books, and
some of that money advanced to employees
to buy War Loan, is an exceptional perform-
ance even for a cash store. Presumably they
don’t expect it to last. It certainly won’t.
But a co. with over £90.000 of tangible assets
will be in a very different position compared
with a co. with about £45,000, and that was
how things were only five years ago. The
extra £45 000 won’t earn money by itself,
though. That takes management. And you
can’t swear that the concern which has good
management to-day will have it 20 years
hence. If you could you wouldn’t be looking
round for a gilt-edged 5 p.c. investment.

So between January, ’ll, and January, ’lB,
£50,193 has been added to reserves, of which
£4OOO came from premiums on a new capital
issue, and £35.000 has been transferred to
capital. Thus in the seven years the co. has
shown a profit of £81,193 after paying the
regular 10 p.c. dividend ; and the capital to
start with was only £BO,OOO plus £30,000 of
visible reserves. But perhaps it is hardly a
fair thing to say that all of that is profit
made within the period, because in 1913
inner reserves of £21,000 were disclosed, and
they may have been accumulating for years
prior to 1911. However, assuming the
worst, and knocking out the £21,000, it has
been a remarkable performance. The next
thing is to inquire what those reserves are
represented by; for there are reserves and re-
serves—often they are represented by plant
which has been put in to hold the business
against new competitors. In this case plant,
never very large, is obviously kept written
down hard; and judging by the inner re-
serves that are shaken out now and then, all
sorts, of other contingencies are very liberally
provided for. Go back 10 years:—
Jan.,’oB. Jan.,’l7. July,’l7. Jan.,’lB.
Liabilities. . . . £58,996 £35,223 £45,591 £44,642
Premises, plant, etc. 19,911 19,461 20,944 20,242
Stock .. .. 35,505 51,713 53,167 61,481
Book debts .. 90,566 82,736 89,119 88,038
McCulloch Co. .. 19,077 38.307 39,519 41.332
Public securities. . 20,000 20.000 20,000
Cash . . . . 5,351 24,673 28,008 21,926
The capital in 1908 was £70,000; and since
then £14,000 has been got in—£lo,ooo as
capital (4000 shares of £2 10s.) and £4OOO
as premium. This capital is represented by
reduction of liabilities; the £26.000 added to
stock, the £22,000 put into the McCulloch
business (the Victorian and River Murray
concern which the co. owns) and the £37,000
added to liquid assets (cash and War I.oan)
has all been made out of the business —a
nice little £85,000 of very solid stuff. Book
debts, which haven’t varied much in the
period, are heavy ; but that is the way with
all traders who keep country accounts open,
and it works out all right in the end. The
co. is in a business in which there is no
particular reason to expect that the next 10
years will be much worse than the past 10;
for there isn’t another class of the commu-
nity which has gained less from the pouring
out of War Loan money than those for
whom this co. caters and none likely to
suffer less from the re-action. The author-
ised capital is £150,000, and £25,000 of it is
unallotted. It might almost be said that it
awaits allotment.

“Following the action of the mainland brew-
eries we were able to slightly raise the price
of ale and beer in Tasmania towards the
end of last year,” the directors say; and
that no doubt had its effect on the profits,
though the increase wasn’t operating long
enough to do very much good. However, the
co. is gradually pushing into a much better
position than it held a few years ago. Look
at this five-year comparison:—
Liabilities.
Dec.,’l2. Dec.,’l6. June,’l7. Dec.,’l7.

The co. has thus shaken itself free from
nearly £40,000 of debt, partly by restricting
its dividend, partly perhaps by small sales of
the Cascade estate, and the rest by getting in
loans—book debts and advances have been
reduced from £30,153 to £19,484. It still
owes a lot of money, £84,547; but that is
mainly because on a capital written down
to £lOB,OOO it is carrying, in addition to
three breweries—Walker’s, Grade’s and Cas-
cade—-various pubs and a large unproductive
freehold, the lot valued at £174,187. Rates
and taxes have increased 50 p.c. in five years,
and now call for very nearly £4OOO p.a. For
every £3 the shareholders get. the taxing
authorities collect £2. And, though Hobart
is waking up to the fact that it ought to have
an industrial future, with its exceptional cli-
mate, its magnificent harbor, its compara-
tively cheap living, and its cheap available
motive power, there isn’t much prospect of
this co. unloading its dead-weight at a price
that will wipe out all the accumulated carry-
ing charges. On the other hand, not only
are there fair prospects of a larger local
trade, notwithstanding the reduced pub-
trading hours that have come to stay, but the
co. ought to be able to use Hobart’s favor-
able climate and water supplv to develop a
much larger export trade. That Australia
should have been importing so much ale and
stout from foreign parts with Mt. Welling-
ton looking out on the Antarctic is one of
our national disgraces. And there are large
possibilities in a cider trade, too. Many a
boy coming back from France is bringing a
taste for this very mild tipple with him.

No need of an iron-tonic for this old-estab-
lished co. —now aged 40. During the three
years prior to January, 1010, a net profit of
£53,950 was made after writing off £5275.
For the past three years the net profit has
totalled £75.634. Meantime ordinary (or
perhaps extraordinary) depreciation has been
wait ten off before disclosing profits—the
£5OOO set aside out of the past year’s surplus,
as shown in the last line, is towards modern-
ising the works. No doubt the management
is taking advantage of the beautiful weather
to put something aside for contingencies. It
is certainly protecting stocks against any
possible after-war slump, as for the past two
years that item has been shown “less re-
serves.” This co. had a bitter experience
once before in the matter of depreciation of
stocks. About 15 years ago the Maoriland
Government suddenly decided to reduce the
duty on imported drugs ; and this co. found
that practically in a night between £BOOO
and £9OOO of the book value on its shelves
had evaporated. It is taking no risks now.
A lot of the stuff has been purchased at war
prices ; and there’s no telling how the market
may behave when the big chemical factories
over the water switch their enlarged plants
on to ordinary trade lines.

Some of the decrease in the factories item,
compared with a year ago may possibly be
due to the sale of obsolete plant;
but there lias also been allowance
(amount unstated) for depreciation. The
management is at present modernising the
works at Burnside, where some of the machin-
ery has been earning profits for 35 years.
Obviously anything spent on this account
during last year was paid out of revenue and
written off. The considerable increase in book
debts, it is explained, is due to a rush of
business towards the end of the co.’s financial
year. But, add the cautious Dunedin direc-
tors, “these accounts were rigidly examined
and all classed Al.” The co. has made good
progress during the past 10 years. New capi-
tal amounting to £96.155 has been got in;
and liabilities have decreased by £46,750.
Meantime, £46,791 has been added (net) to
property and plant and £58,445 to book debts
and cash. Stocks, less the reserves before
mentioned, have been reduced by £14,589 in
book value. Meanwhile shareholders have
averaged a shade over 7 p.c. p.a. on their
capital.

Some time ago, owing to the Federal Gov-
ernment’s indignant, not to say furious,
denial that Australian tungsten-metal pro-
ducers had been made the victims of an atro-
ciously bad business deal, Tiie Bulletin,
which had taken no part in the controversy,
set out to procure the facts beyond doubt.
Shortly after the inquiries were started, Aus-
tralian producers were granted a small in-
crease —so small as to suggest that the prices
previously paid were very near highwater
mark. It is now established, however, that
the increase does not bring the prices up to
quite one-half of those received by Canadian
producers;—
Australia. Canada.
Molybdenum.—
‘Unit .. £5 0 0 £lO 0 8
Ton . . \5OO 0 0 1003 6 8
Scheelite. — \
Unit .. Y-10 0 5 8_ 4
Ton .. 250 0 0 541 13 4
Wolfram. —
Unit .. 210 0 5 0 0
Ton .. 250 0 0 500 0 0
This matter is so serious that it calls for the
immediate attention of the Federal Govern-
ment. Evidently, in his profound ignorance
of business, Prime Minister Hughes was led
into making a shockingly bad bargain with
the British authorities. Worse, however, he
compelled Australian producers to put up
with the consequences, refusing them permis-
sion to find a market for themselves. It is
incredible that the Acting-Prime Minister
will compel Australian miners to continue
under this grave injustice.
Some gold returns for the Commonwealth:
1903.. £16,294,684 1915.. £8,270,339
1909.. 12,604,509 1916.. 7,074,673
1913.. 9,376,573 1917.. 6,155,837
1914.. 8,729,949 | 1918* '5,421,000
*.Judged on the yield of first quarter.
The decline was continuous; in the years
which are omitted to save space the drop was
just as steady as in the years quoted. And it
affected every State. Even in the N. Terri-
tory the yieid fell from £61,600 in 1903 to
£2554 in 1916 (later returns not available).
This promises to be the worst year since
1891. After that time Westralia, which had
been almost virgin ground (unfortunately
there isn’t so much virgin ground left now)
came to the rescue. It reached top in 1903
(£8,770,719). Last year it was down to
£4.121,645. So even in its diminished con-
dition it stands for over two-thirds of the
total. For 1918 the Westralian return looks
like £3,810,000 or thereabouts.
“The lamb has been found, but the mint
sauce is wanting.” This sums up C.H.
Richards on Coronet Hill (N-T.)- You
have a splendid mining proposition in hand,
he says, "and if the development work be car-
ried out Coronet Hill has a brilliant future
before it.” What the lode lacks in breadth
(4ft.) Richards gives it in length (1$ miles),
with the best stuff to the south. Taking oxi-
dised ore down to No. 2 level, he reckons that
proper development will give 00,000 tons
going 10.7 p.c. copper, 12oz. silver and 14
p.c. arsenic. Disregarding the arsenic——
although it is worth over £7O per ton —he
gives the ore a recoverable value ot £l4 per
ton. With milling and transport requiring
84s. per ton, there is thus a nice margin.
Seeing that the total recoverable value of the
metals in ore reserves stands at £840,000,
there would be £735,000 to play with.
Against that Richards debits £27,000 for
mining and development, and £13,000 for a
pyritic smelting plant. This obviously pre-
supposes sulphides at depth, but what they
are worth and what is their size neither
Richards nor anyone else knows. However,
a lot more cash must be found before
Coronet Hill can light its beacon. For a start,
to connect the show with the railway 42
miles of line have to be laid. Also a dam
will have to be constructed and a pumping
plant installed. As the estimates are based
on copper at £llO per ton, a big writing-
down may have to be done in the valuation
bv the time the smelter is up and the railway
built. Shrewd heads are also a bit sceptical
about a lode li miles long working to a 10
p.c. grade. The good points of Coronet Hill
are that it has rich ore, that the lode goes
strongly underfoot, and that the Common-
wealth Government is a solid partner in the
show. So Gilruth may come to the rescue
with a railway proposal. If he contemplates
that he ought to look for proved ore reserves.
To get them a lot of development work will
have to be done, and, not least of all, the solid
prospecting of the sulphide zone. Tim paral-
lel formations should also be looked into to
see if they are likely to be a stand-by. While
that is being done the arsenic problem ought
to be tackled while every ton available is in
demand.
* * * *
Mammoth Molybdenite, Yetholme(N.S.W.),
has produced its first lot of concentrates.
The 10-head battery and separation plant have
been running a fortnight, two - shifts being
worked the second week. However, figures as
to the quantity of stone put through or as to
recovery have not been made available ; but
manager Cornell says he is “satisfied with
results.” Meanwhile an unexpected issue
has crouped up with the Lithgow Co., whose
leases adjoin the Mammoth’s, over the desti-
nation of the tailings. The gully into which
they have been shot runs through the Lith-
gow show, so Mammoth has been requested
to keep to its own back-yard. Mammoth will
now dam up its tailings, but in the mean-
while it hopes to continue running its plant.
Scarcity of suitable labor is the co.’s chief
trouble for the moment.
