Adding gold from the plates of the Car-
lisle. Ironbark, Hercules and Northern mills,
Bendigo Amalgamated last month got a total
of 25400 z. from 8683 tons. The jewel song
in Dyason’s monthly review is over the
uplift of Carlisle: spurs at 1240 ft. are taking
on a kindly hue, and it looks as if the 1040 ft.
and 1140 ft. stopes will soon be following suit.
A new shoot of gold has been cut in the rise
on the Bast leg at 1540 ft. at the Wind-
mill shaft, and a new and large reef
is anticipated. If it turns out all right
Dyason will have a little compensation for
missing the Virginia run of spurs at 600 ft.
into which the tributors butted directly they
were let loose in that mine. These spurs are
parallel to the old reef, so that it will be
easy to crosscut into them north and south
of the tributors. The third card being
played is the Hercules, where there is a will-
ing go at the reef at 840 ft. on the strength
of its showing some colors. To fill lip time
three new formations at Johnson’s No. 2
shaft have been marked up as worth a close
examination. At present some 50 faces are
eating up call money all the while, to say
nothing of the expenses of a big drilling pro-
gramme, yet no one growls.
* * * *
The rattle of the pumps is the only mine
music on the Langi gutter, Ararat (Vic.),
these days: the Union having prevented the
men going below to do development work
unless wash extraction is resumed. At
the New Langi, Bill Toole insists on the con-
tract. system and on his right to fix the
maximum rate. The Langi South crowd
don’t like calls if a bit of gold can be
scraped up; so they may dangle a bait before
the Union to get it to lift the embargo
against development, and permit extraction
to be continued. If not, the call-making era
will be extended a lot longer, to the joy of
the gentle- bear punter.
The strike at Jumbunna, Gippsland
(Vic.), reduced the colliery’s output for
the past six months to half that of normal
terms and put the co. £l6OO on the wronj
side of the ledger. The leading drives h
the mine have got past the faulted ground
better coal is showing, and the price o
fuel is bounding up. Liquid assets are £400(
ahead of liabilities. So the outlook could b<
a lot worse.
* * * *
Tributing has stopped at the Great Fingal
(W.A.), and the mine is likely to shut dovvx
shortly. This will mean putting up the shut
ters in most of the business-places in tht
township of Day Dawn, and frantic S.O.S
signals are already being sent out by tin
pub- and store-keepers there. The mine ha?
had a remarkable career. As the Day Dawn
and afterwards as the Consolidated Murchi-
son it never earned profits. Yet it had a big
reef milling lOdwt., and a strong shoot of
ore. These conditions lasted to 400 ft., at
which point there was an improvement, a
branch reef joining the main body. The
quartz widened to 15ft., of a length of about
600 ft., and for several levels values averaged
£6 per ton. Then the shoot went wrong, and
the mine became only a skeleton of its former
self. Several millions sterling have been taken
out of the property to date, and nearly two
millions have been paid in dividends.

Golden Butterfly (W.A.) announces that
an independent report is to be obtained on its
Blue Streak option at Tudkabianna. It is
about time some outside expert was called in,
but to make the report worth anything it will
have to be by someone of standing, particu-
larly as the reef in the property is. alleged
by some experts to belong to a series that
won’t make depth. If the Butterfly’s man
is convinced that it does, then the co. should
sink and open up at depth, and not follow
the usual policy of Adelaide-managed West-
ralian gold ventures of grubbing about on
reefs near the surface and not giving the
mine a fair chance.

To the end of 1917 about 90 Westralian
gold-producing cos. had paid dividends, but
only about a dozen now remain. Great
Boulder heads the list with £5,269,300;
Ivanhoe comes next with £3,636,750, and
Golden Horsehoe not far behind with
£3,375,000. Other million dividend-payers
are -Oroya Brown Hill. £2,146,241.; Great
Fingall, £1,746,875; Kalgurli. £1,615,500;
Boulder Perseverance, £1,461,116; Lake
View Consols, £1,448,750; and Sons of
Gwalia, £1.055,363.
* * * *
Bananaland’s Minister of Mines Alf
Jones, who has put more life into his De-
partment than any previous Minister, has
not let the Leg. Council’s rejection of the
Iron and Steel Works Bill prevent the birth
of the industry. This week the first fur-
nace will be tapped at Ipswich. The ore
comes from Biggenden, and the pig pro-
duced will be used for a start at the local
railway workshops.
The high price of arsenic sent Minister of
Mines Jones looking round Bananaland to,
see if his own State could not supply its;
own and anybody else’s wants at a much?
cheaper pilce. A find was made in the
Stanthorpe district, and tests having been
satisfactory the Department will erect a
furnace alongside the railway line close to
the mine.
* # * *
“D.” : The agitation in Westralia for the
registration or licensing of directors of min-
ing Boards to prevent the suppression of bad
news and the distribution of false reports
reminds me of an instance recoi’ded in one of
J. 11. Curie’s books. At a Board meeting a
report w T as read from the manager that the
l-eef had faulted and couldn’t be found. The
chairman was convinced that the reef had
been lost entirely and proposed that the
shareholders should be told so at once.
Another director and heavy holder opposed
the proposal. He pointed out that the reef
had been probably only displaced, and that it
would show up again by and bye. He argued
that the publication of the report would
cause a big slump in share values, that many
nervous holders wrnuld be put out at the
bottom of the market, that the reef might be
found again, and that the directors would
then be accused of rigging the market. The
Board considered this argument logical and
decided to withhold the report. However,
the dii-ector who induced the Board to sup-
press the manager’s report promptly un-
loaded his shares, and later on the other
directors quietly followed suit. Systematic
search railed to pick up the lost reef, and the
shares became valueless, which was bad for
the people wdio weren’t directoi’s.

The tonnage and values of the Zeehan
(Tas.) output for the first quarter of this
year are not exhilarating. The total value
is £26,000, after deducting freight and smelt-
ing charges. Lead ores totalled 987 tons,
worth £16.300 net. Tin contributed 48|

Carpathia, Ardlethan (N.S.W.), hands out
another sixpenny this month, making a total
of £31,000 distributed. At present the mine
is looking well: a lens of ore at the 200 ft.
level from No. 4 shaft is carrying a 6ft. lode
of high-grade stuff. The half-year to Jan.
31 gave a profit of £3465.

Sydney, April 15, 1918.
My Dear Akini, —
The river up Abbotsford way teemed with
a mosquito fleet for the Great Public
m- r oo nll “?h tta «, F °» T eks Gram-
’i niYh lf , S^ U '?, < as represented by
defi™ e Eu f ai lf Grammar) had shouted
dtnance at each other on the stretch of shin-
ing river where they trained. Vassals had
rubbed embrocations into the muscles of their
S e T s at , and dusk ' Plough Ignatius
and Joseph Colleges were in the struggle—and
the Joeys won a fine race for second fours—
the keenest rivalry was between the two
grammars. As launches manoeuvred for a
good place, raiding parties flaunting blue-
und-white streamers leapt aboard the blue-
wnVfvi( craft aad kad a hurried bout with
wateibags. But the Grammar boys, who
won the thu-d fours and the championship
eights, cheered their beaten foe and offered
it the balm of chocolate or toffee before the
sank oa the victory. Timber-man
Svixton gave the Grammar heroes a dinner
a - tlle Australia that night. Scots College
was amongst those who also sculled.
TT 9, n FniJay afternoon, April 19, the King’s
Hall will blaze with color. Mrs. Shimizu is
m charge ot this cabaret for the Ited Cross,
at which a whole Milky Way of theatrical
stars will twinkle. The little Japanese lady
is out for results that will beat her own
records. Remember her peach-bloom bower
in the lown Hall vestibule on a ’l7 day
when a swarm of kimono girls fell upon the
public purse with frightful slaughter?
IS ice-looking Cicely Hughes—younger
daughter of the late John Hughes, M.L.C., of
this town—took the necessary oath of allegi-
ance t 0 Surgeon-Major Curtin of our
A.M.O. on a wintry day in London. The
groom also hails from Sydney, being a son
of the late David Curtin. A warrior brother,
Captain I>. I). Hughes, M.C., gave his sister
to her new husband.
Word comes that Australian manufacturer
G- a . kas keen m ade a Liveryman of
the Yv orshipful Company of Musicians,
London, an ancient rite that carries with it
the freedom of the city, so I suppose he can’t
be enslaved within the metropolitan boun-
daries. Glancing over a long list of Worship-
nils, one sees that the list is most elastic. A
mere duke is allowed to be a Liveryman of
this order, and even millionaires—Waldorf
Astor _ is one—can squeeze through the
needle s eye of inspection. But mostly such
musicians as Elgar, Prof. Bridge and
Bir Charles Parry are the backbone of the
august stables. . O. C. Beale, of course, makes
the raw material of music in the shape of
pianos. The Musicians Company is a small
but, honorable affair with £490 per annum
of income. But quite a few Liveries have
no income at all.
Archbishop Kelly’s garden party at the big
White Palace on the Manly hilltop had as
guest of honor the Catholic Women’s Associ-
ation. The picturesque purple robes of
Archbishop Cattaneo were absent from the
sunshiny gathering, as the owner of them
is at present in Tassy’s pleasant isle.
When Mrs. Prank Moulden—who was the
Avidow of the knighted Hackett (W.A.)
until a week or so ago—compiled her Aus-
tralian household guide, she was her own
keen press-agent. Writing personally to
reps, of every well-known paper, she stated
the ease in brief and “on-the-spot” remarks.
The cash from the sales was for some patri-
otic purpose, but Avhether Australia plunged
heavily in learning the right way with a
vacuum-cleaner or the proper control of a
Mary Ann is not known hereabouts.
Mrs. Hugh Ward’s gift afternoon for the
Red Cross, in the spring of this week, was a
big affair on the stage of Her Majesty’s. In
case you have any doubts as to what a gift
party is, recall the Australia Day furore.
When our Avomen tore the coats (coney seal
at that) off their backs and the rings off
their fingers to hand them as hasty tributes
to the fund, the Avestern horizon wasn’t so
dark as it is to-day. Some of those three-
guinea boots will be melted down for cash
before this struggle is over.
In these later days Canon Garland, V.D.,
the busy, buzzing Queenslander who Avas on
the old recruiting committee, is representa-
tive of the C. of E. Fund for Soldiers Over-
seas, with headquarters at Cairo. The cam-
paign in Palestine doesn’t attract much atten-
tion from outside agencies, and as the pro-
portion of Australians is large—much larger
than it is in any other field —the Canon
found that Billjim wasn’t at all sufficiently
catered for in Cairo. So five shops have
been turned into a club—the Australian
Soldiers’ Club —and 50 beds have been made
and billiards put in and all the rest of it;
and what you're wanted to do now is to send
the weeklies and magazines you’ve finished
with.
A sad old father in a lonely home at
V\ ahroonga—much too big now the fledglings
are gone—has only memories left of two
lively boys who used to make the house ring
with their uproar. Dr. Antill Pockley’s
youngest son, Jack, has just gone out in the
struggle in France. A lieutenant first in the
A.A.M.C., he was a brother of the chival-
rous Brian, also of the A.M.C., killed in New
Guinea within a few weeks of the outbreak
of war. Phyllis, the fair-haired beauty,
married a naval man in England a while
ago, and the family is scattered like thistle-
down.
Highbrows and others—everybody, in
short, with a semi-quaver to stand on—
crushed into the Conservatorium the other
night to hear Verbrugghen’s story of his
travels. The little man has a biting but not
unkind wit. He tears the tinsel off false
musical gods without scratching their thin
skin too deeply. For instance, he agrees
when Uncle Sam sits at the satin slippers of
soprano Galli-Curci—up to a certain point.
Then Itoland Foster slipped the lady’s latest
record into the musical-box. A luscious
voice pealed out, but ended in a shake that
had the dull tonal caterwaul of Juliet
Meow. As an artist Melba is still unbeaten,
according to the Conservatorium critic; and
lie has just compared her trills with those
of much younger artists in the IJ.S.A. Had
a blue pencil chopped out superfluous detail
Verbrugghen’s brisk puncture of unduly-
swollen musical balloons would have sent
the company home asking for more.
, he Burton Dibbs, who spend much time
in \ ictoria these days, have lost a second
son, Owen, lately of our Stock Exchange, in
lu-ance. On April 1, the date of his death,
Ins young wife and her sister Dora (Mrs.
Walford) were taking a leading part in the
revue for Light Horse comforts. The news
came later. Grandfather Tom Dibbs has
seen the sands run out for many brave lads
in his clan since 1014.
Marie Tempest said good-bye to Sydney in
a blue sports coat and a striped silk skirt
with the same cerulean touch. A packed
theatre, and the usual stacks of wired blooms,
followed by a sneech from Marie, finished the
last hole in the “Pair of Silk Stockings” at
the Criterion.
A daughter of the
late Dr. Gilhooley of
this city, the clever
wife of Thomas
Hughes, M.L.C., is
one of those earnest
Australians who have
put aside every
pleasure to get a
tighter grip on war
work. Her favorite
hobby in the days of
peace was wood-
carving. The Hughes’
home at Rose Bay
has many oimate
specimens of her
handicraft in solid
chairs and settees
carved after Eliza-
bethan devices. But
the knitting-needle has replaced the chisel,
and an anxious mother’s thoughts hang
on the news from the Western Front.
As a member of the N.S.W. Red
Cross executive, and with her special task
•amongst the convalescent home committees,
the capable lady has no idle moments. When
“Tom,” M.L.C., was Mayor of Sydney in
successive years, his wife’s tact kept pre-war
women’s gatherings immune from conversa-
tional acid-drops. There was a knighthood
later for him, and a sheaf of good wishes
for the lady whose little photo, is here.
“Conferences” are the autumnal joint.
Governor Stanley (Vic.) came this way for
an unofficial chop with the Davidsons. His
term is up in January next, but he is not
likely to stay beyond September. A few
side-shows are arranged for his delectation,
and the Hawkesbury College hen is con-
sidered worthy of special notice.
Dolores, after a long silence so far as this
town is concerned, will sing here on May 4.
News drops in that Melba’s friend, Lady
Susan Fitzclarence—she’s a young war widow
—has gone on the stage. Cyril Maude, a
frequent visitor to Coombe Cottage (Vic.)
during his Melbourne season, knew that was
in the air. The lady was an average ama-
teur and keen to go on the regular boards.
A title helps.
Sydney Uni. gave its blessing—per the
vice-regal hand—and the usual degrees, in its
big stone hall, the other afternoon. Vice-
Chancellor Purser (Chancellor William
Cullen is holidaying in Maoriland), in some
majesty of black and gold, named the con-
quering bookmen. Medicine handsomely beat
law and science as a popular profession.
Law was at bedrock for once, with eight or
nine degrees to its trencher-boards.
This smart-looking
young bird is Geoffrey
Forrest Hugiies,
M.C., recently deco-
rated for valor. At
22 he is Captain in
the Flying Corps
with two years’ ser-
vice to his credit.
Younger son of
Tho m a s Hughes,
M.L.C. (his hand-
some mother is ijn
the previous column),
Geoff was in his
second year Arts at
Sydney Uni. when
he made up his mind
to attack the enemy
from aloft. After
months of waiting
to get into the Australian Flying Corps he
went to England. With the impatience of
youth he was afraid the war would be over
before he got his wings. But the boy has
had his two years at the game. His only
brother Roger (there are no sisters) was
with the Medical Corps in France when a
bullet finished a promising career.
„ Dr. Bestic, a stepson of barrister Arthur
Kelynack, tucked his best girl’s hand in his
arm and led her down the aisle the other
afternoon. Hunter’s Hill noted that Super-
stition was cast to the winds. For the
wedding was on the 13th—it happened to
be the birthday of bride and ’groom—and 13
guests were present at the quiet knot-tying.
The heroine of the party was Enid, a
daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Muddle, of Lane
Cove River, and she believes 13 is her lucky
number. I see nothing wrong with the
figure myself. A girl whom I knew slightly
inherited £13,000, and she is still in good
health.
Conductor Joe Bradley, of the Phil., de-
serves all the kudos he wants for putting up
a bold fight against the encore pest. Every
good, bad and indifferent performer is
brought back—or comes without much bring-
in £ to repeat his oi' her good or worst per-
formance. But there are exceptions. And
the row for a second helping of Cappelli’s
liquid notes at Saturday night’s “Pop” was
one. After his rest from the hard and almost
incessant strain of singing in the defunct
opera co., the tenor’s voice is a joy to
music-loving Sydney. Besides, the owner is
going to warble presently <behind a gun)
on the Italian Front. But the Phil, con-
ductor wasn’t quite big enough to give way
graciously. Cappelli sang his remaining item
as , an , encore an< 3 then took the next tram
to his lodgings.
About 800 white pinnies with the Red
Cross on their bibs fluttered in the Domain
on Saturday afternoon. This was the
'’ * • .f 1 ™ rehearsal for their huge display
on April 2b, when the country lass will add
hei strength and smile to the procession.
Midday brought bad news from the Front,
and hefty damsels boldly asserted their right
--in spite of pettis—to arm in their coun-
;^v^ defe , nce - Au Amazon corps of, say,
10,000 gallant bibs from each State wouid
be worth considering. Mrs. Chris Bennett,
director, mothered the Junos.
A Sydney girl, Boadicea Monk (she’s
been Mrs. Stanley Mort for quite a long
time), is still driving wounded soldiers as
required in England. A temporary break in
war work is indicated, however, by the
cabled news that Baby Mort arrived on
Easter Day. When in Sydney before the
days of the Hun uproar, the Monk sisters
Boadicea and Hypatia (the latter is now
Mrs. M. Adams, of Wahroonga), were
amongst the first local girls to drive a car.
Aow? Well, we have reason to be grateful
to the woman at the petrol wheel, for she
takes her corners carefully and doesn’t reck-
lessly dash up and down the earth on the
wrong side of the road. So far as I know
the woman motor-driver hasn’t even knocked
down a policeman or a hoarding to date.

The “Vagabonds” naively stated that “all
the male members of the cast are ineligible ”
lhis was not intended to warn off any spin-
steis m their audience, but was explained bv
the statement: “The others are on active ser-
vice or—will light (alas!) no more.” Last
week the Y'ags. held revel in St. James’s
Hall. The Blue Gum Girls’ V.W.A. fund to
build a soldiers’ cottage gets some of the
profits.
The American-Australian League of Help
(president, Mrs. Brittain) is making a very
helpful effort for our Red Cross Day. In
passing it wishes you to note that “no money
made by the League is sent out of Australia.
It is all devoted to local patriotic funds as
the calls come.” Home-made sweets are a
specialty. Drop in and see the ’Murkan
ladies preparing cookies for April 2(5. Their
lair is 5 Wynyard-street.
On April 26 Mrs. Alfred Lee and her
helpers get busy in their Mint in Macquarie-
place, where they coined about £5OOO on
France’s Day.
Noel Stretch, killed in France lately 7, was
the second son of J. F. Stretch, who has
bished in the Anglican interest at Newcastle
for the past 16 years, and was before that
an assistant-gaiters at Brisbane. Young
Stretch began in the ranks with the A.1.F.,
but was selected with others in Egypt for
training for a commission with British
troops, and had worn stars for two y r ears.
He won the Military Cross twice over in
France, receiving a “bar” the second time.
A brother lately returned from the Front
disabled, and another brother is in Aus-
tralian khaki somewhere in Europe.
When our lady bushrangers were less
skilled in skinning the eyelids of the com-
munity, functions on the Day fell over each
other to the detriment of those that were
underneath. With nearly four years’ ex-
perience they know better. Long before
Red Cross Day dawns every excuse for rob-
bing the healthy to help the wounded will
have been exploited. Conspicuous among
these preliminaries is a cafe chantant on
April 18 at the Australia, with Miss Eadith
\\ allcer and Mrs. Maearthur Onslow in
command of the gang. Before the war you
would never have suspected some of these
gentle ladies of being born highwaymen.
From Brisbane:—
Major A. S. Nobbs, of the Camel Corps, was mar-
ried at Abbassia on January 5 to Miss Blocksidge, a
Brisbane girl. Captains Philpott and Cullwick
tied the knot; a Miss Ashton of Heliopolis was
bridesmaid. Convalescents from the 14th A.G.H.
threw a barrage of congratulations as the party left
the church. After a spread at Shopheard’s in Cairo,
the ’moon was spent at Luxor and Assuan.
Died last week, Callaghan Walsh, merchant, of
Cairns and Port Douglas. I-Ie had been in business
in the North for the last 40 years, and had mayored
at Cooktown and Cairns, was chairman of the Cairns
Harbor Board and chairman of directors of the Moss-
man Central Sugar Mill. Solicitor J. A. Walsh, of
Brisbane, is a son.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

Melbourne, April 15, 1918.
My Deak Yarkie, —
Collingwood snared the Governor-General
lor tlie opening* of a Military Pageant at
the Oval on Saturday, and kept him cooling
lus heels for three-quarters of an hour owing
to the late arrival of a procession in which
every second performer had elected himself
chief marshal. Frank Tudor came along
in the gubernatorial motor-car, and Martin
Hannah arrived wearing a huge bunch of
vegetables in his buttonhole. The G-G
was presented to John Wren and other dis-
tinguished citizens, and, graciously accept-
ing- a fill of 'baccy from an ordinary bloque,
ignored the notice, “Smoking strictly pro-
hibited,” and puffed his pipe until the time
arrived for the Mayor to say his piece. His
\\ orship’s elocution was seriously impaired
by a brass band which deposited itself in
front of the grandstand and proceeded to
P la .V the right tunc at the wrong moment,
pome agitated officials succeeded in silencing
it after a time, but his Caledonian Excel-
lency had only half finished his few remarks
when a Ilielan Pipe Band arrived and made
noises that sounded like a deliberate affront
to the Throne and Person. More officials
had to scamper from the grandstand to shoo
the kilted noise from the vicinity, and his
Ex. resumed, making an observation, doubt-
less intended to be tactful, about “one ass at
a time, please.” When the Collingwood
P.L.C. has had time to think the remark
over it will probably pass a motion favoring
the abolition of imported Governors.
. While in Bendigo last Friday Lady Helen
visited the Art Gallery to view the picture
with which Marion .Tones gained the
Travelling Scholarship of the National Gal-
lery. The vice-regal party was shadowed
by the Mayor and Mayoress, and her Ex.
afterwards progressed to the' young artist’s
studio. Miss Jones is working for an exhi-
bition which she intends to hold in Mel-
bourne later in the year.
Melbourne has now only one medical vet-
eran of the name of Ryan who took part in
the European wars of the nineteenth century
—Doctor Charlie, who was with the Turks
at Plevna. His namesake, Dr. .Tames Pat-
rick, who served ns a surgeon with the Army
of the Loire in the Franco-Hun War,
died suddenly last week at Cliveden Man-
sions—erstwhile the dwelling-place of his
old pal John Madden—at the age of 78. He
had just seen to the removal of his sick wife
to a private hospital. One of the best-known
Collins-street surgeons, he obtained most of
his degrees in Ireland, and in his youth roved
as far afield as Panama, Callao and Iquique.
After the 1870-71 trouble he returned to
England, and reached Australia in ’73.
founding a practice at Richmond, and
subsequently specialising in eye and
ear work in the city. For many
years _ he. was one of the Agricultural
Society’s judges, and Melbourne will miss
the _ slim figure on the dark chestnut so
familiar to those who knew their city. Ryan
was sometime president of the Kooyong Polo
Club, and the Melbourne Club regarded him
as one of its show members.
The returned soldiers, sick or otherwise,
are losing one of their most enthusiastic
girl friends for a while. Frank Allen, of
Hutton’s, is off to America on a business
trip, and is taking daughter “Pop” with
him, so “Pop” and her motor-car will bring
no more batches of khaki boys from Port
Melbourne pier for a while. It is to be
hoped that the car will be as active as ever
in the good cause under other management.
The blonde young Miss Hutton has never
missed a disembarkation since the boys came
trickling back.
Squire Andrew Chirnside of Berwick (he
specialises in the wearing of country-gentle-
man gaiters and the breeding of stock) has
given 500 acres of his Berwick estate for
settlements for returned soldiers. The land
is first-class, near the railway and within
40 miles of Melbourne. Andrew chose
the place in which to settle because the
original home of the Chirnsides in Britain
is at Berwick.
Mrs. Charlie Fairbairn has given a won-
derful string of pearls with three diamonds
in the clasp to be raffled. The gamble will
be managed at the Quamby Club Patriotic
Party on May 2. The fashion-platers will
live for nothing else on that day. The show
is to help Lady Stanley’s appeal for the
Red Cross. There are to be 28 tables at
£1 each, and the usual selling abd raffling
of produce, cakes and sweets. Mrs. Douglas
Menzies has given a picture by Ida
Rentoul, and Mrs. W. J. T. Clarke a blouse
of surpassing loveliness. Roma Clarke is
the picturesque assistant-secretary to Nora
Cunningham. Jack Baillieu is to be l’affles
man.
Marion Montgomery, the hardy horse-
woman who organised the Caulfield carnival
for the Purple Cross, had to stay away at
the last moment. She is shut up at home
with a nurse preparing for some exploratory
operation.
The widow of Chief Justice Madden is
leaving the big fiat at Cliveden, where the
surroundings are too reminiscent of the
past. She and daughter Lesley are taking a
fiat at Studley, South Yarra, one of those
built on the old Como estate. Daughter
Beatrice and her husband, Jack Baillieu,
were previous dwellers on the premises.
Among those who had the B.A. degree con-
ferred on them at Melbourne University last
Saturday was Amergin Oisin O’Dowd, fourth
son of poet Bernard. Amergin is probably
the youngest B.A. in Victoria. ll e entered
the I ni. as a student of 10, and completed his
Arts course before reaching 1!). lie is now
doing his third year Law, and if he lives up
to his record during the next two years he
will qualify for his LL.B. before lie is 21.
Bernard has yet another sou, a giant of 13.
Mildred Gillespie is back at war work in
England. This girl moved over to France
in the early stages of the war when there
was little comfort for nurses. She often had
t > sleep on straw or cold stones, and endured
so much hardship that she was invalided to
England. She has been resting and visiting
her old friend “Tryphena” Doughty, for-
merly of The Bulletin, at her London
house.
The peerage and baronetage and hereditary
landed gentry of Camberwell feted through
the last of the week with stalls, raffles, sales
of home-made cakes and home-grown ehooks
on the wing. The best adders amongst them
are now biting the ends of their pencils, tot-
ting un the final takings. Also the best
young men in their most immaculate flannels
have been springing through a patriotic
tennis tournament connected with the show.
Champion Alf Dunlop lives at Kew, and
several of his best trainees were heavily
engaged.
Colonel Churchas, well known as an archi-
tect and builder, has died in France. He
got away with the 19.16 Anzac guns, leaving
his young wife with her small step-daughter
and infant son to shelter with her parents,
Mr. and Mrs. Westbrook.
Pursy Chirnside, jun., who is a captain in
the Bth Hussars, you know, has been in-
valided to deah old London suffering from
wounds and shell-shock. Pursy, sen., has a
little job in the Army Service Corps.
Flight-lieutenant Flere, reported as missing-
in France, is a prisoner in Germany. This
famous schoolboy athlete fan old Melb.
Grammar boy) is son of T. 11. Flere, of Mel-
bourne’s Ancient Mariners. Another that
was lost and is found is the Bertie Austins’
flying son, who had his wings clipped by
the Turks and was posted missing for nearly
three weeks. He had previously aeroplane!
with the Anzacs in Palestine for over 12
months.
Architect Dick Klingender and his wife,
who have been living on war news during the
two years the only child Keith has been in
the Anzac gunpit, breathe again since hearing
that their soldier has been sent to hospital
with a wounded leg. The boy left Melbourne
Grammar to get into khaki in 1916.
Miss Adelaide’s gossip :
War has struck heavily at the Tennyscns. The
youngest boy, who was in the Navy, was killed
early in the war, and his eldest brother, Lionel
(Rifle Brigade), was wounded for the third time
towards the end of last year. New comes news
that the only other son, also with the Rifle Brigade,
has keen killed in action. Her late Ex.’s own
death was directly the result of overtaxing her
strength working for the comfort of the men of the
navy, and in recognition of this she was buried with
naval honors.
Major W. H. Gosse, of the R.F.A., was killed
in action early in the month. He fought with
one of our contingents during the Louth African
scuffle and decided to stick to the army when he
was given a commission in the R.F.A. Subse-
quently he retired and made his home in N. S.
Wales. When the present struggle began he at
once w r ent back to England and joined his old
regiment. During his three years and more of
service he won the M.C., and was major in com-
mand of a battery when the end came. His father,
W r . 0. Qosse, was at one time Deputy-Surveyor-
General in S. Aus.
His Ex. Governor Galway has offered his services
to the Imperial Government, “unconditionally, for
war work, to be utilised in the best possible man-
ner.’’ It is by no means the first time that the
offer has been made, and there is probably no man
more bitterly disappointed at being denied active
service. It’s an awful fate to be kept loafing as
an Australian Cocked Hat during this welter —
it's almost as bad as the system which endures it.
The Moulden-Hackett wedding occurred on Wednes-
day at St. Peter’s College Chapel, and the deed
was followed by a reception at North Adelaide,
where the newly-weds have bought the late Mrs. W.
Milne's house on Brougham-place.
There is at least one man in this State, Schulz by
name, who has a firm belief in the proverb concerning
clouds with silver lining. He was charged with having
changed his address without notifying the military
authorities. He was fined £lO and, as his total
assets were only about £7, things looked blue for
him till a bank manager, recognising his name as
that of a man he’d been looking for, reached him
out a draft for £3OO awaiting him at the bank.
The Ornithological Association has made Captain
White its president for the third time in succession.
He is our standard reference on all matters concerning
local bird-life, and is a son of the White who was a
valued coadjutor of Gould.
Lieut.-Colonel Butler, who has been invalided,
arrived at the end of the week. The W. B. Wilkin-
sons’ elder son, also invalided, was a passenger by
the same transport.
We are in the midst of a Red Cross fortnight, with
the school children as the chief buccaneers. Fifteen
thousand of them were let loose on Wednesday, sell-
ing buttons and canvassing from house to house for
subscriptions. As the organisation of the Public
Schools’ Patriotic Fund is something to marvel at,
the result of the two days’ effort should be con-
siderable.
There was a mild riot at the Bowman’s Arcade
recruiting stand t’other day when a civilian in the
crowd kept his hat on during the wailing of the
National Anthem. Someone knocked it off for him
and was promptly reproved by a third man. The
eternal feminine then took a hand, attacked the
unlucky reprover with fists and umbrellas, and chased
him up King William and Currie streets and back
through a side street and Bowman’s Arcade to King
William-street, where the police took the panting citi-
zen under their protection. Meanwhile the original
disturber of the peace had melted away like the Arab.
Audrey Chirnside, daughter and heiress of
the George Chirnsides, of Werribee Park, is
wearing the solitaire diamond of Major
Harold Pape, of the 13th British Tank
Corps. Audrey, now in England motor-
driving as a V.A.D., is the only child of her
sporting father. She is keen on coursing
and all outdoor sports, and when here kept
marvellous kennels. Mrs. George, a lovely,
languid, frocky lady, was hard put to it to
mould her into conventional debutante form.
Audrey, a fine-looking, breezy, blue-eyed lass,
loved her tweeds better than frills and fur-
belows. Mother was an Australian beauty,
whose frocking for any social occasion took
the form of a ceremonial. Two hours was
the minimum dressing time; but the result
always warranted the trouble taken with
her flower-like person. She has been in
London almost continuously since the war.
Audrey divided her time between helping
her and helping dad in Egypt.
May the orange-tree that shed its blossoms
over the wedding of Private Boy Vial and
Mollie Crozier bear bushels of golden fruit!
While he was putting in three years’ active
service in the Land of the Midnight Hun, she
and his mother did their bit —they worked
his farm at Cowley’s Creek for him. When
he came home the young people had their
wedding.
Another transport romance. Estelle
Venner Keogh, of St. Ivilda, joined the Im-
perial nursing service two years ago because
the Australian authorities considered her too
young for the job, although she had shown
her proficiency at the Glenroy Base
Hospital. After a spell at Abbeville
Military Hospital, France, she took ser-
vice with the big hospital barges which
carry the wounded down the Somme.
Later she was with a British surgical team
on emergency work at the casualty clearing-
stations, and last New Year the King pinned
the Royal Ited Cross of the First Class to her
tunic. She won it for her conduct during a
bombardment near Ypres, which killed a
Canadian nurse and caused the loss of another
girl’s eye. Estelle stayed to see the last
wounded man off the premises. On the
transport coming home she met Lieut. G. 11.
Wilkins, M.C., a Tasmanian of 30, and now
she has gone to his Island to marry him.
Dame Hackett has dropped her courtesy
title (gained when the late John Winthrop
Hackett of Westralia was knighted) with
her widowhood, and is now Mrs. IMoulden.
She married Alderman Mo'ulden in Adelaide,
and the two are honeymooning here at
Menzies’.
Robert Hannah and Rosie Pascoe are
preparing for a wedding. Rosie, a very
pretty Brighton girl, is a daughter of
Pascoe, of the National Mutual Life office.
Robert dabbles in Imperial Oil. The wed-
ding will be at Yeovil, Brighton, with a
cottage honeymoon somewhere in the hills.
The next-best-man at the marriage will be
solicitor Roy Mackenzie, of Bendigo, and
the chief bridesmaid Ethel Pascoe. These
two have already decided on a wedding of
their own about a year hence.
Marjorie Rouse, the bright young Sydney
girl -who used to liven up Melbourne occa-
sionally at race times, has just been married
in America to a Mr. Lucey, of Erie, Penn-
sylvania. Mother has seen the wedding
through and is bound for home and Sydney.
Beryl McAdam, daughter of Dr. McAdam,
married Dalziel Kelly last week and wore
full bridal costume for the ceremony. Her
gown was white satin and silver, roses and
lace, with the conventional train, veil and
wreath. The wedding was a quiet affair,
but the relations of both the stars are so
numerous that there was a good “house/’
They were married in the chapel of the Eng-
lish Grammar School. Beryl had the joy
of having her soldier brother home on fur-
lough to see the event. The squattocrat
Weatherleys were there, also the mining
Bowes Kellys. Jean Weatherley and Nancy
Isaacs wore sky-blue chiffon as bridesmaids,
and Cedric Battle was best man. Mrs.
Russell Grimwade wore deep blue.
Political recruiting-sergeant Donald Mac-
kinnon is now a grandfather. The stork de-
posited a baby girl at son Donald’s doorstep
in Ireland the other day.
Melbourne Shakspeare Society is having
another birthday on the 19th ; or, rather, it
is the Bard’s birthday, and the Society is
celebrating it for him. The shivoo will take
the form of a dramatic and musical enter-
tainment at the Masonic Hall, ticket-holders
being advised to book their seats at Glen’s.
Talking begins at 8. And they do talk on
Shake’s natal day.
The Lyceum Club, our great stronghold of
blue-stockingism, lately listened to a paper
by Mary E. Wilkinson, the authoress of
several repertory plays including “Whither,”
“The Lie,” etc. The document dealt with
three Australian poets, Dorothea Maekellar.
Bernard O’Dowd and Hugh McCrae. It was
read by sweet-voiced Repcrtorian actress
Mary Curtain, and urged more recognition
for our local bards. Prof. Boyce Gibson,
scholastic Miss Gillman-Jones and president
Enid Derham, commended the criticism
warmly, while Dr. and Mrs. J. Booth. Floss
Greig, the Oswald Syme lady and sister Lucy
Rowe murmured “Hear, hear,” in suitable
places.
The opening night of “The Madras
House” attracted to the Playhouse the usual
collection of Highbrows and Awful Dressers,
the women mostly looking as if their clothes
had caught on them as they ran downstairs
to the tram. (In one of Kipling’s stories,
by the way, one lady describes another as
looking as if she had stood in the middle of
the room, while her husband threw her
clothes at her with a hay-fork, which may
be relevant, and again it may not.) The
H. 11. Champions were in the stalls and
near at hand Archibald Too Strong peered
through his spectacles as though searching
for disguised Bolsheviks. Solicitor-General
Bob Garran, looking like a very thoughtful
giraffe in unblemished evening dress, had a
party in one of the balcony boxes; and Mrs.
Lionel Lindsay, on a holiday visit from
Sydney, was with friends in another. Others
in the balcony were Emily Dyason-Scott
and her _ professor husband, and the city
newsboys’ “cobber” Edith Onians. A par-
ade of mannequins in the third act might
have given the show the interest of a revue
if the ladies had had fewer clothes and more
confidence.
Rumor is flying round that Italian bari-
tone Balboni is much stirred in his mind
over a call to the colors. Balboni is a six-
footer who always appreciates to the full
his own magnificent appearance on stage or
platform. He makes a good color scheme
himself with his blue-black hair, coal-black
eyes and yellow-tan complexion shown up
against the white spread of his shirtfront
and his immaculate black concert duds.

ABORIGINAHTIES
“Jimmie Pannikin”: In reply to “Niin’s”
query, I want to say that the Education Depart-
ments of Australia do most earnestly encourage the
idea of bird-troughs and miniature sanctuaries. The
trouble is the average Australian’s apathy; but for
this characteristic casualness and apparent want of
power to sustain energy and enthusiasm, in the
direction of saving and comforting native bird-life,
every school in the land would have its bird-
troughs for drink and for food and its ready-made
bird bungalows. The enterprising Americans are
miles ahead of us in this respect.
* * * *
“Jimmie Pannikin”: Every school should
have its gathering of feathered friends tamed by
the regular supply of food, drink and kindness. I
have seen bush schools that were visited daily at
12.30 sharp by birds in search of sure and certain
food, non-intoxicating drink and safe, interesting
human company. If the school were a little late
in dismissirg, some of the birds, probably the hens,
became quite irritable. Occasionally the head-
master would delay dismissal to show the pupils
how much the birds disliked unpunctuality. Even the
frli,\, sensitive satin-bird becomes tame enough to
join the bush bairnies at lunch.
* * *
'K. J. Till.vai'd" : As the “man who re-
cently wrote about dragon-flies” referred to bv
Kola (B. 21/3/’18), I liave a bone to pick with
bun. He says that I “overlooked one of their prin-
cipal failings ; they are hogs on bees,” and then pro-
ceeds to describe how “the fly gets busy, and soon
the bees’ innards are sucked out.” and “a fly will
seriously reduce a hive in a very few days.” Now,
if “Kola” had even glanced casuallv at my book,
which apparently he hasn’t, he would see that'dragon-
flies cannot suck. His description very aptly fits the
giant robber-flies, which have been this season such
a serious menace to our bees. These flies have slender
bodies, large eyes, and are very fierce—much fiercer
than dragon-flies are. Their mouth is prolonged
into a horny beak, with which they impale their
victims, sucking the blood from their bodies, and
then casting them aside. Their appetite is insatiable,
and I should say that they consume 20 times as
much food in a day as does a dragon-fly twice their
size. These flies belong to the family Asilidce (not
allied to the family Atinidce, in which we only class
people who gratuitously give the wrong names to
insects and other things), and their nearest relatives
aie the March-flies, or Tabanidne, those vicious blood-
suckers of the bush that we all know. They are the
largest and fiercest representatives of the order Dip-
tcra, which includes such pests as the mosquito and
blowfly, not to mention hosts of others. The dragon-
flies are aristocrats compared with these Huns of the
insect world, and are much more concerned, during
their short existence in the winged state, with the
gentle arts of flying and sex-dalliance. Many attempts
have been made to estimate the actual food "of dragon-
flies, chiefly by Professor Poulton (of Oxford) and
myself, and 1 can safely state that neither we nor
a 113011 c else has ever recorded that dragon-flies attack
bees, although I do not say that such an occurrence
is impossible. If “Kola” would be so good as to
catch one of these tyrants of Oippsland (by the way,
what is Mr. Wise doing to allow this sort of thing
to go on in his kingdom?), and will send it to me
with some of its victims or their “bones,” so that 1
can examine the nature of the injury inflicted, 1
shall be very pleased to give him the scientific name
of Ins enemy, and any other information that I can
furnish about it. Hornsby (N.S.W.) will get me
if the G.P.O. doesn’t put the letter in the Rootv Hill
bag.
* * *
“Pharmo”: For scrub-itch. “Mac” (B.
21/3/’IS) might try the following treatment. Bathe
the irritated parts in a solution of bicarbonate of
sooa (the ordinary baking soda), about one teaspoon-
ful to a pint of water. Dry gently and apply
boracic ointment liberally. Then make for the near-
est chemist and get him to dispense a magnesia and
sarsaparilla mixture.
* * *
“Ornis”: “Wart” (B. 21/3/’18) is in
eiior in stating that the pear-slug was introduced
into the Speck about 15 years ago. It arrived via
Melbourne o\er 30 years back. As the pest does
not affect apples, growers of that fruit do not
worry much about it, but cherry cultivators have to
fight it for all they are worth. It is capable of
killing a large tree in three years. The pest is
of the sawfly family, the female being equipped
with a beautiful and very effective weapon with
which she makes minute slits in the tissue of the
leaves and slightly lifts the edges and then lays
one egg in each tiny pocket. Nothing is visible
until the eggs have hatched, and then minute white
spots show up. Later the slugs develop and fall
to the ground, where they turn into pupze or chrysa-
lis, just below the surface. Those unpleasant-look-
ing, squirmy grubs one sees in clusters on many
gum-trees are first cousins.

‘Mini G.”: Apart from its pre-arranged
job, the Murrumbidgee irrigation scheme is a national
benefactor, as its great stretches of unirrigable land
form a sanctuary for native bixds, animals and
insects. In the Spring millions of love-birds or
shell-parrots nest and rear their broods in the tall
gums at the foot of the ranges, while innumerable
quail sport and fatten in the long grass' on the red-
soil plains. The total absence of the poison-cart
gives the kookaburra, magpie, ibili and many other
old friends a chance to die of old age, and in the
sunny weather the air is filled with the gladsome
hum of the wild bees. As no sheep are running in
the country, young kurrajongs, wattles, all kinds of
acacias and gumtree seedlings are springing up.
The country itself, owing to the fact that it is
not overstocked, is responding with a perfect mat
of natural grasses and herbs. A ride through this
Arcadia in the cool Autumn shadows would make
anyone a lover of Australia for ever.

"Jim Carew”: In Noumea in ’B7 I pur-
chased an old Bible printed in French, and bearing
the date 1823. On returning to N.S.AV. (same
1 eai) I packed the book in a wooden case with
ethers and stored the lot in a bain. Until recently
I didn’t disturb it. On opening the case 1 found
every book, except this Bible, ruined by white ants.
Are white ants religious?
* * *
T-W.”: The pilot-fish ( Ntiucrates due-
tor) turns up at times on the N.S.AV. South Coast in
close attendance on a blue-pointer or tiger shark or
other brand of sea-wolf. I caught five of these little
fishes personally conducting a blue-pointer about 7ft.
long round and round a deep hole at the mouth of
an estuary. Three pilots swam a few inches above
the shark’s nose and two right in front of it, all five
keeping in their positions with wonddrful precision.
Of course the shark doesn’t bother about such small
fry, but when he picks his teeth after a mea] the
pilots score, and this, fishologists assert, accounts
for the strange partnership. The pilots wear bluish-
grey coats barred with brown. They are edible, but
I don t think they often visit the frying-pan.

“W.A.C.”: When toiling in the canefields
in Queensland I had trouble in keeping the luncheon
meat from getting fly-blown until an old hand
advised me to hang the menu 12ft. from the ground.
Cursing at blowfly's offspring thereafter ceased.

"Bouragi”: “Malaria” (B. 14/3/18)
asks how to keen clear of the dreaded fever from
which he takes his pen-name. There is only one way:
dodge the infected anopheles mosquito. That the
“malarial mists” of which he writes cause fever is
a long-exploded superstition. As they generally rise
in the marshy habitat of the anopheles, they get the
blame attaching to the insignificant insect.
“Malaria’s ’ long immunity W'as due merely to his
luck in not having been introduced to an infected
anopheles. Now that many cases of malaria among
our troops are being landed in Australia I would not
be surprised to hear that malaria, always present in
this country, though in very slight degree, becomes
increasingly common. AYe have the right breed of
mosquitoes, and only need a start with the infection.

lin trampin down from Dubbo in the rain an’ in
the heat ;
I’ve wandered through Australia, far an’ near;
An’ 1 know the country backwards, for I learnt it
on my feet,
But the learnin’s grow in’ harder every year.
Tor my swag as I get older
Feels heavier to my shoulder,
An’ there’s times when life is hardlv worth the
strain ;
Sweet an’ bitter 1 have tasted,
An’ 1 feel my life’s been wasted
As I m trampin’ down from Dubbo in the rain.
I’ve humped a heavy bluey through from Charlcville
to Hay,
f lorn Tullamore to Cobar, out an* back;
l 1 roin Dalby to Deniliquin I know the weary way,
An’ I know what men may suffer on the track.
Ah! the billy-tea an’ damper
Seem so jolly to the camper
At hen he s got his week-end friends to entertain ;
But with nought else in the larder—
Well, it comes a trifle harder,
As you’re trampin’ down from Dubbo in the rain!
Now the squatters an’ the cookies always shake their
heads an’ frown,
Where years ago they’d offer me a job ;
But since my thatch has whitened they mostly turn
me down,
An’ nowadays it’s hard to earn a bob.
The folks who meet me, wonder
Why I’m down an’ out an’ under,
An’ they talk to me in sympathetic strain.
I suppose they think I’m dilly,
With my bluey an’ my billy,’
To be trampin’ down from Dubbo in the rain.
Yes, they smile to see me ploddin’ on along the
Bathurst road,
The long, long road that runs to back o’ Bourke ;
They agree “the weather’s beastly to be humpin’
such a load,”
They’re “surprised I’d rather tramp than go to
work!”
But it’s little use to grumble
When the storm begins to rumble,
lor it’s on’y fools an’ farmers who complain;
They may growl about the weather,
I take good an’ bad together,
And I’m trampin’ down from Dubbo in the rain.
Now I’m drawin’ near to Lithgow, an’ I'll cross the
mountain range,
Where the bracken hides the long forgotten graves
Of the pioneers who made the road. Lord! Ain’t
there been a change ?
There’s tourist parties, now, to see the caves!
Toffs from Mosman an’ St. Kilda,
Who will joke about Matilda,
An’ they’ll ask me ain’t it nicer in the train l
They’ve got motor-cars an’ money,
So they think it blinkin’ funny
That I’m trampin’ down from Dubbo in the rain.
But I’ll go the way appointed, an’ I’ll climb the
mountain track,
An’ I’ll find the air is cleaner on the range.
It’s too late to turn aside now, an’ linger, or look
back,
It s much too late for me to make a change.
An’ it’s little I am caring
For the bit of swag I’m bearing—
I’ve a heavier load to carry in my brain;
An’ I wouldn’t mind it, only—
Well, I’m getting tired an’ lonely
As I’m trampin’ down from Dubbo in the rain.
N.s.w.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.

“Horatio”: The Government dairy expert
was lecturing an audience of cow-cockies at a small
Taranaki (M.L.) township. Said he: M I want you
people to realise that there are cows and cows, just
as there are eggs and eggs. Some cows pay, some
cows don t. ’ This was too much for the local grocer,
who spoke up from a back bench: “By cripes, mister,
you re right! An some of the cows who don't pav
are in this 'ere ’all to-night!”
“Paul Cupid”: Ever see a pitched battle
between a tarantula and a centipede? It’s fine. 1
thought at first the fellow of many legs must win
easily ; but tlie tarantula not only held its own, but
seemed to have the other very groggy. However,
when the mill had reached a most interesting stage
a wasp poised overhead for an instant, then darted
down, pulled the tarantula out of a clinch and flew
oif with it.
* * * *
•Tun : Here are two queer places where
I have found bees’ nests: (1) In a rabbit burrow,
near Bulagero Siding, Cootamundra ; (2) on a netting
fence between Harefield and Wallacetown (N.S.W.),
until heavy rains disturbed the busy insects, leaving
a nice piece of comb and honey. By the way, to
make bees alight when swarming, take a looking-
glass and reflect the rays of light on the insects in
the air.

BROWN : “You've been ill, I believe. I heard a report about your appendix."
SMITH ( horrified) : “Good heavens, did you? And they told me it was quite a quiet little
operation.”
“Wnndi”: A team of bullocks was sold
the other day for £5OO. When I was off.siding for
Greenhide a few years ago, the talk of the country
was the sale of a working-bullock for £9 —a price
to startle the cableman then. The purchaser was
an ox-conductor who worked only bovine celebrities ;
if he heard of a great bullock anywhere he would
go after it, no matter how far he had to travel;
and any beast he bought had to prove its worth
in a scratch pull against a dinkum of his own.
“Nine quid’’ was his record price for a champion,
and he knew the bullocks' “Who's Who” from A
to Z. Times have changed, and soon the bullock
will be too important to swear at.
* * * *
“Vigilans”: I have camped ou practically
all rivers in North Australia where crocodiles
abound, but have never heard a vocal note from
them. The croc, is a sloth in action aiid as noise-
less as an angels’ concert, and, moreover, he is the
most cowardly wretch in existence for his weight and
size. I suppose in no portion of Australia are they
so prolific as in the vicinity of Derby, Wyndham and
Darwin, yet the inhabitants of
those hamlets are never disturbed
at night. From the jetty at
Derby or Wyndham at low water
huge crocs, can be seen basking in
the sun or half-hidden in the
mud, but never a sound comes
from them. In fact, for the size
of the creature a crocodile is the
most silent, uncanny mover in
creation. But you cannot outdo
him for cunning. While fishing
off the high banks of the Northern
rivers I have been watched for
hours by huge monsters, the only
portion visible being the snout and
the eyes, which protrude from the
sockets on such occasions. Under
ordinary circumstances the croc, is
a sluggish, slimy, hideous brute,
but for its size and ungainliness
nothing is quicker in movement in
the water, and no inhabitant can
travel with such rapidity and
make less stir in the water. It
can enter and emerge without
causing a - ripple. The brutes do
little hunting on land ; the tail is
used to sweep any adventurous being into the water.
Although they are so numerous in the North you
rarely hear of anyone being attacked, and no one
fears them.
* * * *
“Eddyson” : Clowe was a man who might
have escaped attention his whole life through had it
not been for his quaint delusion concerning the affec-
tion he inspired in dumb animals. When I was
in Saucer Plat it was a bull, a large, methodical
animal, apparently as capable of regard for anyone
as so much punk. The bull’s name was Ovid, and
Clowe owned him chiefly as a conversational staple,
I imagine. At any rate Clowe talked about
nothing else. The affection of Ovid for Clowe
was, it seemed, past all human comprehension.
Ovid had even in a fit of jealousy attacked “Tereser,”
to whom Clowe was paying attentions, and when
Tereser commanded Clowe to choose between her
and the brute, he had no hesitation in deciding that
no woman could ever love him as Ovid did. I re-
turned to Saucer Flat 12 months later, and Clowe
was then being adored by an exceptional litter of
pigs orphaned by lightning. I spoke about Ovid.
“He’s gorn,” said Clowe. “You see, me ’n’ Wil-
liamson’’—Tereser had married Williamson—“had a
row over 15 shillin’s, ’n’ it led to a fight up
in the paddock. While we was at it, up comes
Ovid, ’n’ that bull no sooner sees Williamson is
lickin’ me than he gets to him ’n’ gores him all
up. That bein’, I had to shoot the bull.” I ex-
pressed my sense of Clowe's ingratitude: “The poor
beast sought to serve you, and you shot him.” “Oh,
that’s all very well,” said Clowe, “but 1 had Wil-
liamson pinned at the time, ’n’ the blessed bull
had to go through me to get at him. Three holes
I had. I shot Ovid for not havin’ better sense.”
“D.F.”: When E. J. Eyre was at Fowler’s
Bay (S.A.) in 1840 getting ready for the first over-
land trip to W.A., he saw on the beach the shell of
a very big turtle, which must, he says, have weighed
between 3001 b. and 4001 b. It had been caught by the
crew of an American whaler which put in some
months at Fowler's Bay. Is there any other record
of turtle being found on the south coast of Aus-
tralia ?
“Jim Carew”: In the western parts of
N. S. Wales I have often seen men denuded of all
their hair. A man will go to, bed apparently nothi-g
wrong with him and wake up in the morning to fi id
that every hair on his body has fallen out during the
night. I have never known this disease to attack
a woman. Once the hair falls out in this fashion
nothing yet invented will make a new crop grow.
* * * «
“Breeza”; If any bushwhacker will hand
out a few hints on how to poison, trap or otherwise
lay out crows he will receive the applause of many
labbit men of the back country, where, in some dis-
tricts, a shilling each is offered for the scavenger’s
head. It’s an easy matter to catch one or two, but it
takes a smart hand to get the next 150 necessary
to give a hunter union wages for a day. A cooky
named Merry man was the last to pass me reliable in-
formation about crows. His advice was: “Find a
dead sheep, paint the carcase well with phosphorus
and collect the crows next day.” Soon-after a jum-
buek chucked it in the vicinity of Merryman’s home-
stead and I hastened to paint the carcase and await
results. They were as follows: Seven prime bacon-
ers, three suckers, 10 “slips,” three dogs, Pansy
(she was the boss’s wife’s cat), and half a dozen
magpies. No dead crows, but plenty of live ones
busy picking the eyes out of the casualties.

“Te Whare”: Let me recommend kangaroo
hams. Take 31b. coarse salt, six tablespoonfuls
treacle, lOoz. brown sugar, oz. saltpetre, and loz.
carbonate of soda ; mix with enough water to make
a strong brine. This quantity will cure 10 to 12
hams. Let them soak for five or six days, then hang
up to dry, in a smoky kitchen for preference.

“Jim Carew”: I was recently a spectator
of the fiercest fight between two Australian natives
that it has ever been my fortune to witness. It was
ago between two male white cockatoos. I was hidden
in a cornfield with a double-barrel, hoping for a shot.
The mob was perched in a dead tree about 150 yards
away. By the unusual note of the screeching I knew
that something out of the way was on, anil as they
had forgotten to post sentries in their excitement, I
managed to crawl within 40 yards of them. I was
just in time. The screeching suddenly ceased anil
two of them fluttered to the ground. With a few
preliminary caws they were at it hammer and tongs,
after the best game-rooster style. The scrap lasted
about five minutes, and then one threw in the towel
and both struggled back to the tree. During the
fight the spectators were absolutely quiet, but when
peace was signed the row was nearly deafening. My
gun put an end to the argument; I fired both barrels
and collected 11.

“Rouragi”: 1 see that a snake has come
to an untimely end by breaking into Armidale Gaol.
I wonder if he was as interesting to the inmates as
another I knew of. This chap was a big carpet, and
the gaol he broke into was the one for native prisoners
at Tamata, one-time capital of the Northern Division,
Papua. The gaol was a long building of logs, and
tlie prisoners, when they turned in for the night,
were chained, each by the ankle, to a long trace
chain running down the middle of the floor. Thus
there were two rows of them, sleeping feet to feet.
Most of the prisoners were Orokolo carriers from the
Gulf of Papua, and, like all Western boys as opposed
to Northerners, terribly afraid of snakes. One morn-
ing about 2 o’clock the R.M., whose house was some
200yds. from the gaol, was wakened by a noise as
if all the fiends in the Pit had been plunged into
ice-water. While he was trying to make out whether
it was a general native rising or just a local mutiny,
a horror-stricken police sentry arrived with
the news that the Devil had broken into the
gaol and was eating the prisoners. To make
things as cheerful as possible, the R.M. was
rotten with fever, and the darkness was so
thick that the rain could barely fall through it.
It took a long time to get things explained, but
Vakemba, an ex-policeman who was doing two years
CONSOLATION'.
THE VICTIM: “For Gawsake give us a hand,
Digger."
DIGGER: “Aw, he patient! Theres a bloke
four feet under ’ere ain't sayin' nothin’."
without tlie option for murdering and eating a
prisoner whom he was escorting to gaol, finally
helped to unravel the mystery. While the Western
and Northern prisoners were fast asleep a snake had
entered through the log wall and begun to pro-
menade down the line of Western men. These, panic-
stricken at the horror that crawled by night, franti-
cally endeavored to take shelter among the North-
erners, who, while not fearing the serpent particu-
larly, objected to the trespass. Meanwhile the snake,
more scared than all the others put together, gal-
loped impartially over the shrieking mass, desperatelv
trying to find an exit. When the R.M. finally threw
the door open the reptile slipped between that nerve-
wracked individual’s legs like a streak of agitated
perdition, leaving behind him a reputation in that
part of the world which will last as long as one of
those gaol blackbirds .is living.
“Jack McLaren”: A five hours’ run
through a sunlit sea that sparkled and frisked
brought us to the Torres Strait island of Badu. The
course from Thursday is mainly through a labyrinth
of reefs, sandbanks and islands fringed with grey-
white beaches and straight, sinister-looking man-
gio\es. The quick darkness of the tropic night came
down while we were yet at sea, and above the
pulsing of the engine and the swish of the bow-wave
came the softly-spoken directions of the native who
called himself “cheap officer’’ as he ferreted a
passage with disaster hidden on either side and
ahead. Alleged civilisation has deprived these natives
of many of their virtues, but exact local knowledge
and wonderful vision are still theirs. From their
youth they have hunted pearlshell and bfiche-de-mer
among the valleys and mountains of the sea-floor, and
they know every reef, sandbank and irregularity of
the bottom. The felinity of their sight is almost
uncanny; on the darkest of nights they can see to
a distance quite impossible to whites. At Badu, as
on most of the inhabited islands of the Strait, an
attempt is being made to help these races, who were
happy enough in a general sort of way before civilisa-
tion interfered. The State’s efforts are mainly edu-
cational and protective. Teachers, some of them
women, school Black Brudder’s nippers, adjudicate
in petty civil questions, and keep a check on immo-
rality. To these teachers much of the work is heart-
breaking, and it is all underpaid. In addition to
these guides, native councillors are chosen from the
supposedly wisest and best-behaved of the villagers,
and their duties are to keep order, sit in adjudication
with the teachers, and, on state occasions, wear a
guernsey with “Councillor” in large letters upon it.
A certain kind of success has attended the Church’s
efforts, mainly because the nigger, steeped in super-
stition, takes rather kindly to the missioner’s
alleged ability to protect him from evil spirits and
other bad influences, and because “Church members”
consider themselves of more importance and a nearer
approach to the envied white man than the common
herd. Then there is, also, an inherent love of cere-

mony and display, which is a powerful attraction.
Altogether, the policy adopted in these islands has
justified itself in one respect—the population is
rapidly increasing. What to do with them wheD
their increasing birthrate makes the islands too small
to hold them is going to be another of Australia’s
problems.

“Pitta” : It’s the “inter-house trade” that’s
making (paper) fortunes for the wholesaler
at the present time, and incidentally send-
ing the cost of living sky-high for the con-
sumer. Anything from tea to tin-tacks sells
now. A man I know heard Blank and Co.
had a shipment of a certain class of wrap-
ping paper just in. (Paper is right out of
his line, but it’s impossible to go wrong
these times.) He sent a broker round for
particulars and took the lot. He held it
two months. The same broker then called
s eking the same class of paper and was
quoted 75 per cent, advance on cost. When
the sale was made the contract note dis-
closed that the original seller was now the
buyer! The Lord knows how many times
this paper was sold and re-sold before it
Covered pork chops.
“F.C.”: I know one delegate who didn’t
attend the recent A.N.A. Conference at Ben-
digo (Vic.), though he took a few days off
for the purpose. He. missed helping to carry
many b°autiful propositions—those in favor
of the referendum and recall, extension of
the beet-sugar industry, unification, propor-
tional representation, preference to Austra-
lians, encouragement of the flax-growing
industry and the tote had had his support
at many annual conferences —but his wife
told him he’d be better employed at home
perfecting his nearly-finished machine for
making clothes-pegs and wooden spoons. He
grieved over the loss of so many rhetorical
displays, but was honest enough to admit
that the wife was right.
“Croson” : More absurdities of price-fixing !
A while back the Australian Glass Works,
in Melbourne, paid £4 a ton for broken glass,
and were glad to get it. Then somebody who
has a job operating the meddlesome War
Precautions Act fixed the price at 30s. It
looked nice and pretty, like the price-fixing
of bunny-skins, but it worked out wrong.
The glass collectors said 30-bob was no good
to them, and turned to something else; the
Glass Works had to go on half-time, and the
chemists and others who want bottles had to
go without or pay about five times as much
as they should. The legal price is still 30
bob, but it might as well be 30 pence for all
the glass it brings in.
* * * *
Another way of doing it: —
Josiah Bett owned 500 acres of so-called arable
land at I‘ole Hill. Certainly it had potentialities,
but when Bott took hold its virtues were hidden
under gum-trees, spear-grass and bracken. In order
to get a productive area Bott had to adopt a mort-
gage, and for years he nursed the incubus. But he
was an industrious devil, with a red ant’s pertinacity,
and lie kept moving. He had a decent house up, and
an orchard and poultry, and his vegetable patch was
the best in the district. Then he hit out in another
direction. He welcomed paying guests. The neigh-
bors grinned at the idea of Bott's home as a luxuri-
ous retreat for the enervated rich, but to everybody's
surprise Jos. secured one apparently affluent patron.
Henry Arthur Bodewell motored out from the city
every Sunday morning, and motored back to the
city every Monday afternoon. The interval he spent
at the home of Josiah Bott at Pole Hill with Miss
Ariadne Bellview, chorister and toe-dancer, with a
reputed capacity for very small parts in the musical
plays with which the Red Spot Operatic Co. was delight-
ing the metropolis. When not walking with Miss Bell-
view in the fern glades, and assisting that business-
like young lady to gather the buttercups and daisies
for which she had no use, Bodewell slung himself
loosely' over the furniture, and smoked cigars, and
Josiah Bott told him about the mortgage. Appar-
ently Henry had no real, human interest in the mort-
gage ; he was alway's fretful when the subject was
brought up. In fact, there were distinct hints that
Henry detested Bott’s place, and Bott, and Bott's
mortgage, and loathed Pole Hill, and was far from
satisfied with the treatment Miss Ariadne Bellview
was meting out to him. He condoled with himself
all the way back to town, without any appreciable
effect upon Miss Bellview’s genteel composure. “It’s
a nice change from the city,” she said. “I like it.
1 love cows-—they do me good. Besides, there is
nobody to talk and make scandal. It's there or
nowhere, Henry.” And Henry, in whose otherwise
turgid bosom hope sprang eternal, and who had a
firm, almost terrified, conviction that Miss Ariadne
Bellview was necessary to his happiness, if not to his
very existence, went there Sunday after Sunday, and
heard again and again the moving history of Bott's
mortgage, and paid father more than first-class city-
hotel rates for thirty shillings’-worth of bush
accommodation, and was a miserable, discontented
young gentleman of abundant means and small men-
tality. But one Sunday he cut loose, and said he
would bally well stand it all no longer, dash his
eyes if he would. What was he dragged to the
rotten, out-of-the-way hole for, anyhow, and what the
place of everlasting torture did he care for Bott’s
mortgage! Hang Bott's mortgage! He was going
back to town for a Manhattan cocktail, and every-
body' else could blessed well do as she jolly' well
liked. “Oh, all right, Henry,” said Miss Ariadne.
“Toddle along. I’m staying.” “Wh-a-a-t?” squealed
y'oung Mr. Bodewcll. “Stayin’— here?'’ Ariadne
nodded coolly. “Yes,” she said. “You see, I only
stuck to the theatre to help Jos. lift the mortgage.”
“You—you!” gasped Henry, feebly. "You stay with
this —this mud-turtle?” “Ye-s-s,” said Jos., “I could
do with home now. You see, we haven’t really
had any married life, Bell ’n’ me bein’ busy' liftin'
the damn mortgage. But I'm real grateful to you
for liringin’ her. out to see me every week!” Then
Bella Bott, alias Ariadne Bellview, settled comfort-
ably to her cows.
* * * #
“E.S.S.”: Contributions of walking-sticks
for wounded soldiers lire asked for in Mel-
bourne, on the ground that “suitable woods
for making sticks are unprocurable.” They’re
not. Let me direct attention to gidgee,
mulga, black myrtle and tulip. There are
several others, but I mention these particu-
larly because they are amongst the best for
the purpose that can be got anywhere in the
whole wide world. Ring gidgee is the
hardest wood on this globe. It has a beauti-
fully dark, wavy grain, marked with cross
waves, like tortoise-shell, but almost black.
It is so plentiful that it is used outback for
fence posts and fuel—a sinful waste. Queens-
land walnut, one of the many excellent Aus-
tralian woods used for rifle-stocks at the
Lithgow Small Arms Dormitory, is also
suitable for walking-sticks of the' best class.
It is a dark-fed wood, very hard, and severe
on tools.
* * * *
“Ornis”: Even the oldest inhabitant can-
not remember a season when blackberries
were as plentiful as they have been this
year in the Speck. On the north-west
coast it has been not uncommon for a
family to make between £4O and £5O. The
price paid by the jam cos. in Hobart was
only three farthings a pound delivered at
the factory, and that will give same idea
of the size of the collection. In addition,
hundreds of casks were shipped to Mel-
bourne. Many cocky farmers are seriously
considering blackberries as a side-line. Just
as well, for in many places the trailing pest
is threatening to push everything else off
the land. Once it takes hold of a bit of
ground that suits, all the cussing and burn-
ing in the world won’t make it quit.
* * * *
“Murrabit”: Despite the meagre protec-
tion given to the manufacture of Australian
porcelain ware, the small works established
at Sunshine (Vic.), mentioned in The
Bulletin some months ago, are making
headway, and a co. is now taking them over
to extend them. Excellent cups and saucers
and inkwells and such-like have been turned
out, and the next move will be to make all
requirements of this nature in connection
with electrical installations.

“The Floorwalker”: They steal in all
shapes, and their audacity would take your
breath away. One ladylike person, captured
with a lace dress valued at seven guineas
stowed away, when taken into the office
begged that the management would overlook
it just this once. “It’s a lovely dress,” she
sobbed, “and, you see, one must make a good
appearance at this game!”

“Osric”: “Timberite’s” reference to the
92ft. ironbark stick (B. 17/1/T8) used as
a derrick to hoist the mill-funnel into posi-
tion at Mount Rous is dinkum. After the
funnel was induced to stand upright on its
pedestal, held in position by a dozen out-
size bolts and nuts and four strong cable-
stays, the twig was dragged into a paddock
and left there for 12 years, when my dad
bought it and cut it into blocks. Some of
those blocks form the foundation of the room
I am now sitting in. The funnel referred to
weighed every ounce of 20 tons and was 6ft.
in diameter. It was brought on to the
premises in sections. An iron ladder inside
the tube stretched from base to topmost rim,
and it was up this that the painters were
obliged to climb, crawl gingerly over the lip,
make themselves comfortable in a sling, and
then slap and daub 120 ft. earthward. This
was a performance given only once in a
decade with a view to astonishing the
natives. For two months before the act
came off the performers took a course of
Nerve Nuts. I remember the dethronement
of this stack, and of the sister brick one, that
took place when the mill went bung. The
iron chimney was an easy job. The big nuts
at the base were unscrewed, the cable-stays
cut away, and a little zephyr did the rest.
She fell with a mighty wallop. The other
succeeded in covering half an acre of ground
with bricks and mortar.

“Tim Burr”: “Sub” asks (B. 7/3/T8)
about butchers’ skewers. Well, they are
being made in Melbourne. And the demand
is so great the manufacturer can’t make
enough to go round. Like the axe- and
shovel-handles, they cost about half of what
the imported article does at present.

In May will come under the auctioneer’s
hammer the south-west corner of Bourke
and Elizabeth streets, Melbourne. It forms
part of one of the half-acres obtained by
Robert Hoddle at the first land sale, in
1837. Hoddle, who was the salesman, took
the frontage between Bourke and Little Col-
lins streets as his commission.
FEEDIN' PIGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out here in the paddock callin':
“Pig, pig—pig, pig, pig!”
See ’em scamper, scramblin’, failin’,
All sorts —little, big ;
Black pig, white pig, pig that's spotted,
Lean, ’n’ fit to kill,
And I do my task allotted,
Dishin’ up the swill
In a greasy, slattern rig.
“Pig, pig—pig, pig, pig!”
Standi n’ where they hog and waller—
Not above them, much —-
I could lift me hands ’n’ holler,
For me hate is such ;
’N’ I’m human, ’n’ I’m lonely,
Where the hogwash flows.
What a pig a pig is only
Such as I am knows.
It's me grave their long snouts dig.
“Pig, pig—pig, pig, pig!”
Once when I was less than twenty
(“Pig, pig—pig, pig, pig!”)
1 had ’opes ’n’ dreams in plenty—•
Didn't give a fig
For the men here. Now I'm thirty
There’s no man o’ mine.
Drabble-tailed I am ’n’ dirty,
Handmaid to the swine
Where they waller, gulp, ’n’ swig.
“Pig, pig—pig, pig, pig!”
Circe turned men, says the teacher,
Into pigs ; but who
Cares to think what kind of creature
Pigs have turned me to ?
Pigs in dozens squeal ’n’ nuzzle
All along my way ;
What the wretches crunch ’n’ guzzle
Taints me night ’n’ day'.
In my heart they root ’n’ dig.
“Pig, pig—pig, pig, pig!”
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.
The Inky Way.
The last mail brought the first number
of Aussie, “the Australian soldiers’ maga-
zine, printed in the field by the A.I.F. Print-
ing Section.” He introduced himself much
as The Bulletin introduced itself to Aus-
tralia 3S years ago :
That good trench incident, that interesting story,
that smart gag, that bright epigram—send them
along. Don’t keep a good Aussie joke to yourself.
Let's all in the laugh. Don't worry about literary
style. Just jot it down and let’s have it.
So, as you might expect, Bulletin names
abound—including John Dailey’s: he writes
the “short story” for the first number; P. L.
Harris (“Philo”), the editor, writes “Leave-
making in Paris” ; and there is a Wild Cat
prospectus on the back cover.
* * * #
“M-yola”: It may surprise Campbell
Jones, of Sydney Sun, to know that a lonely
bird in the middle of the Indian Ocean was
named after him. Early in the war, when
Jones was London correspondent for the
Sun and other evening dailies, his last press
cable each day, lodged in London at about
3 a.m., always ended “Good-night—Jones.”
The Australian newspaper men were not the
only ones to sigh relievedly when the “good-
night” message came; it was watched for
and welcomed by the operators all along the
line. At Cocos Island, where visitors are
rare, a single frigate-bird used to fly regu-
larly across the cable station just about the
time when Jones’s final cable was passing
through. The bird was known to all as
“Good-Night-Jones.” Its end was sudden.
One of the staff, under the influence of a
scarcity of fresh meat, shot and ate “Good-
Night-Jones.” But, like the albatross in
Coleridge’s Rime of the Ancient Mariner, it
was evidently a bird of omen; for shortly
after it was slaughtered the landing party
of Emden Huns hoisted the German flag
over Cocos, wrecked the cable instruments
and decamped with a schooner, provisions
and clothes.
* * * *■
“Gullabong” : Major A. E. Wearne, M.C.,
leaves Sydney this month to take up his old
post as Reuter’s correspondent at Peking,
which he relinquished 2| years ago to lead
an Australian platoon in the Big Biff.
Sixty-nine members of the clan are fighting,
or have fought, in the war, and, in addition
to the Major, a nephew has w y on the Mili-
tary Cross. A brother is on Sydney Even-
ing News.
