“First Floor” is reflective:—
The other Saturday morning two artists received
their second lesson in the Importance of Being
Dolled-up. David Low and Hal Gye arrived at a
quite-respectable Healesville (Vie.) pub in the utterly
disrespectable condition of pedestrians who had
tramped about 15 miles bearing swags and billies
and clothes of uncertain age. In this disgraceful
disguise they demanded breakfast—breakfast at an
establishment where girls in glad rairhent and best
boys in superior white flannel pants are familiar
guests. The two horrible intruders survived the sus-
picions of the staff and the open contempt of the
beautiful boarders, and got their breakfast without
being required to pay in advance. Then Low, in a
crude attempt to assert the dignity of Art, lounged
to the bar and ordered a cigar. Only a very young
man could have expected to atone for his cheap and
grimy appearance by associating it with a costly
imported smoke. The barman flung down a box of
forbidding Susannas and said, “That’s your sort!”
No doubt the smoke was good enough for the likes
of ’im, but David, it seems, refused to realise what
sort he really was. He demanded the finest cigar on
the premises and proffered a £l-note to pay for it.
Had it been a fiver he would doubtless have been
arrested on well-merited suspicion. As it was, the
two artists retired hastily, under the impression that
the barman wanted to scrutinise the note through a
magnifying-glass. This was their second lesson in the
evils of neglecting pomps and vanities. The first was
taught in a Melbourne restaurant on a Sunday when
Hector Lamond was with them. They were a travel-
stained trio, with disreputable outfits, Hector’s swag
being calculated to scandalise a flock of sheep. They
got their refreshment served at a Dago shop, but not
until the waitress had brought the manager to take
their order. The lady wouldn’t risk the responsi-
bility of executing it without permission. Speaking
of these painful occasions, the hurt that Low and
Gye mostly complain of was the indignant reception
given to their glad eye when they turned it towards
youth and beauty. It begins to strike them that the
glance of genius makes no impression without reason-
able assistance from the tailor.
NEWSPAPER GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, isn’t it wearisome, Newspaper Girl
(I know that at heart you’re a sport),
To chronicle brassard, and slip-on, and curl,
The piffle and drivel of fashion’s gay whirl —
The sex’s sole small-talk, in short?
And what is the use of it, Newspaper Girl,
Though no reputation it wrecks?
I’ve heard you, in private, your knowledge unfurl,
And if you could print ’em, they would cause a
skirl—
The things that you know of the sex!
You flatter the dowager, flapper and knut;
You prattle most innocently ;
But, ’struth, you could make at all chatter “T :
tut!”
If a pen indiscreet ran away with you—but
You keep all such gossip for me.
Lord knows it is pleasant to hang up my hat
And join you at supper at ten
By the little gas-stove in your snug little flat,
For a yarn with the fid off—an untrammelled chat
On the subject of women and men.
The facts may be libels, the libels be facts,
But they’re piquant as mustard-and-cress;
And the veil that you lift from sassiety’s acts
Discloses a vista not published in tracts
Nor told in the pulpit, I guess!
Oh, priceless Pandora of things as they are,
I hang on your lightest remark,
For there’s many a case in un unpublished par.,
Or a saleable tip for a trial at bar,
For —I’m a solicitor’s clerk !

POLITICAL
POINTS
“The Wanderer” : You have heard of the
Dorrigo scrub, on the N. S. Wales North
Coast, with the finest timber in Australia.
Well, North Dorrigo is to have a new school;
and the specifications provide that the ceil-
ings shall be of American Oregon. Right
alongside the school site is a timber-belt
bristling with tallow-wood; and for weather-
board and ceilings tallow-wood can’t be
beaten this side of tlie line or the other. It
is no use merely saying that Australia can’t
make progress while such things are done.
The responsible officer should be informed
that if he ever does it. again he will be
sacked.
NOT WIT AT SHE WAS LOOKING FOR.
“I say, Teddy, what do you think of my
new hat?”
“Well—em— to tell you the truth ”
“Nuff said. If you're going to talk that
way, I don't want to hear you.”
Carmichael, M.L.A., formerly N. S.
Wales minister for Education and now an
infantry captain on furlough, has been
appointed chairman of his State’s Recruit-
ing Committee and has given himself six
weeks to raise another “Carmichael Thou-
sand.” He appeals to eligibles this way:
“Come in with your mates; you will go to
England together, train together, fight to
gether.” Birdwood, it is added, has approved
of the plan. It is an excellent one; yet if
it is excellent, the recently-declared policy of
the Defence Department is the very opposite,
because it provides that men shall enlist for
general service—they are not to know till
they leave Australia whether they are to be
infantry or artillery, or they may be miners
or be passed into the Camel Corps; and if
two mates join together, one may be a
camelero in Palestine and the other an in-
fantryman in France. The fact, of course,
was that, at the time when the new order
was made, infantrymen were wanted and
practically nothing else; but in trying to get
infantrymen more or less surreptitiously
they got nobody—and by this time there
must unfortunately be room in every branch
of the service. The stupid system should be
altered instantly.
TRANSPORTS.
PATRIOTIC GENT: “Young man! Don't you
think all you young fellows ought to be
travelling somewhere else instead, of
packing into these trams to go to the
races?”
SPORT: “Well, it Is a bit crowded. But it's
worse in the race-train.”
Dismal Dick Butler, Treasurer of S.
Aus., had a horrible fright last week. The
first returns from his district of Barossa
were so unfavorable that the Premier ex-
pressed regret at the loss of a colleague, and
the daily papers printed his political obitu-
ary. But later returns put him in third,
just a trifle ahead of the nearest Labor man.
The old identities who went out were
mostly early Labor men who became
“National” or Coalition last year. John
Verran, ex-Premier, was one, after 17 years
of bull-headed political work. McGillivray
had sat 25 years for Port Adelaide; by an
odd chance lie was put out by the son of
Verran, a newcomer who now represents
the seaport with a son of Tom Price,
another Labor Premier of long ago.
Orders issued by the Hun in the parts of
Italy under his control:—
All men and women and all children over 15 are
to work in the fields from 4 a.m. to 8 p.m. Lazy
workmen may receive 20 lashes daily, and after har-
vest will serve six months’ imprisonment. Every
third day they will receive only bread and water.
Women whose work does not give satisfaction will do
six months’ imprisonment after harvest. Children
whose work is unsatisfactory will be beaten.
If the war goes the wrong way we may yet
see the Trades Hall being flogged in all the
capitals of Australia except Canberra, which
is hidden behind the grass and may escape
discovery.
The argument about Ireland is getting
boiled down. The Government now offers to
swap Home Rule for Comb Rule.
Labor in Australia hasn’t anything with
even a remote resemblance to a war policy.
For instance, at the recent conference in
Melbourne a motion was passed spurning
“the audacious lie that the Australian people
insist upon retaining the German possessions
in the Pacific.” Next day, while the presi-
dent of the conference (Chris Bennett) was
toning that down, Higgs, M.H.R., was mak-
ing a holy show of Bennett in another place.
The president carefully explained that “the
suggestion that we desire to return these
colonies to Germany is entirely unjustified
by the wording of the resolution,” the inten-
tion being that the captured German colonies
should be “placed provisionally under inter-
national control.” Higgs, however, snorting
about Hughes’s “on till victory” resolution in
the Reps., spoke thus: “We should declare
now that the moment peace was declared we
would be willing to hand back her colonies
to Germany.” Until Labor adjusts these
little differences with itself, how can it ex-
pect to present a united rear to the enemy?
Robespierre’s position towards the end
was this. He was sure to be killed if he
stayed in Paris, yet he was
safer in Paris than anywhere
else. Prime Minister Lenin
(or Ulianov) of Russia, the
German secret agent who was
once a bogus Hun, alias
Cederblum, is also in a difficult
position. Patriotic Russians
must want to tear him to pieces
for that treaty by which the
disarmed country gave Ger-
many all it asked for the sake
of peace and didn’t get peace
after all. And probably the
Hun view is that he wouldn't
be much use outside Russia,
so he had better stick to his
job there till he is torn to
pieces.
There is method in the mad-
ness of building hopeless rail-
ways with high-priced loans
through empty parishes in the
Dim Remote. It is the only
industry which is practically
free from strikes, for there is
no difficulty about granting all
sorts of wages and conditions
out of loans. When it is a
case of building boots it is easy
to prove at short notice that,
owing to mismanagement, 10s.
worth of boots (retail price)
are costing 155.; but the rail-
way. even if it costs a Roths-
child’s fortune per mile, can’t
be convicted of being an in-
curable white elephant till the
trains begin to run, and that
may be years hence, and even
then nobody except the rare
student of statistics knows
which line pays and which
doesn’t. Also, it removes from
the city the robust class of men
who, 20 years ago, used to
demonstrate outside Parliament
House and threaten hon. mem-
bers with physical violence unless work was
forthcoming, which was a cruel thing to do,
for almost all hon. members were Free-
traders, and didn’t know local industry from
a crow.
The doctrine of despair and helplessness,
as preached by the S. M, Herald (23/3/T8) :
Had the British authorities not proved both able
and willing to buy and pay for wheat and other
produce of ours, of which they were unable to take
delivery, trade in the Commonwealth to-day would
have been at an absolute standstill.
Instead of Australia being one of the countries
least affected by the war, it would have been one of
the hardest hit.
The Prime Minister labors under no illusions on
this score. Six months ago, with characteristic
emphasis, he said, “Unless the British Government
can be prevailed upon to take our produce, we are
down and out.”
Young women. . . .who are now making arrange-
ments to buy fur .coats for the winter might easily
have found themselves on the bread line.
And all that lugubriousness is based on the
assumption that this country Can’t Possibly
Build Ships. If Australia hadn’t been so
sodden with the Herald’s importing theories
it could to-day be sending its produce to
Europe in its own vessels, and getting the
prices that are making Canada so prosper-
ous that the eyes of hundreds of young Aus-
tralian soldier-farmers are turned that way.
The Repatriation Department has at last
got to work (in moderation), and while the
returned soldier is looking for a job through
a special labor bureau an allowance to keep
the wolf from the door will be made to him.
This labor bureau, so far as N. S. Wales
is concerned, is in Sydney; and the man
who is looking for a job must apply for his
allowance there every day. As a scheme for
keeping Billjim hanging around the already-
bloated city it seems all that can be desired.
Meantime the State Government has arrived
at the sensible conclusion that production of
wheat is of great importance; and it
guarantees 4s. a bushel. C.0.D., for the
next two seasons. Billjim might get work
helping the farmer grow wheat; but unless
the cocky takes up a scrub lease in Hyde
Park his chance of getting Billjim seems
remote.

Tlie Huns are making the peaceful folk of
Northern Italy gather in their own harvests
for their tyrants to eat, working 1G hours a
day for bare rations, floggings, long terms
of gaol, and the joy of earning Hun divi-
dends. Meanwhile Australia learns that its
interned loafers, including the fatted crowd
at Berrima which has flags, music and
Kaiser’s-birthday banquets and processions,
has already cost nearly £BOO,OOO of good loan
money. And the end is still far off. ‘Tam-
pering Huns, £2,000,000,” will figure among
the “permanent and reproductive works” for
many a long day after all the accounts are in.
The ineffable Pearce again! It was
stated in the Reps. (10/4/T8) that the
Defence Department had let a contract for
making next-of-kin medals to a firm which
had been fined for trading with the enemy.
The impeachment was admitted, with the
qualification—or aggravation—that “the Min-
ister was not aware of the fact when the
contract was let.” A fact is the last thing
one would expect him to be aware of. It
occurred to a Melbourne newspaper to ask
him whether the Defence Department pos-
sessed a list of the firms which had been
prosecuted for trading with the enemy. “We
have not a list,” replied the portfolio-holding
fumbler, “but wg certainly should have it.”
And he added that he would get it “imme-
diately.” Immediately is Pearce’s long day.
Meanwhile it seems that the contract holds
good.
OUR NEED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“More men? More man ! It’s there we fail.”
—Biglow Papers.
I think the heavy time at last has come
To make a sorrowing song,
With no brave music of a marching drum
To hail the clay gone wrong.
More men, they cry ; more men, and yet more men ;
And all the weary while
Some pompous pedant with official pen
Breaks armies file on file.
As has been well explained by those who know,
Our force of men and steel
Outnumbers that of our terrific foe
Who makes our legions reel.
More wealth is ours, more food, a better cause—
Shall courage still be vain
Because a hard-pressed nation never draws
On its reserve of brain?
Let someone lift upon tlie winds a cry
To drown the battle din:
“Our special need is not for men to die,
But for a man to win!”
The means are ours. Get busy with the comb,
Not in the shirker crowd,
But where gold lace walks ponderously at home
And wears his honors proud.
Weed out the fools! Scrap all the shams and
shows!
The bitter time is here
When, yielding ground before our vaunting foes,
We needs must taste of fear.
These have betrayed us. They betray us still.
No peace is theirs to give.
An eager nation wastes its striving will
To conquer and to live.
Still we can conquer if the path is cleared
For soldier feet to tread.
Shall muddle as a standard still be reared
Above our gallant dead ?
For those who died we owe it to our land
To sweep with truth’s wide broom,
The dust of death that chokes us brain and hand
And smothers us in doom.
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

A GOOD COMPLEXION.
A girl’s complexion is something more than
a matter to concern her vanity. It is an
indication of the state of her health. Pallor
in a growing girl means a thinning of the
blood. Eruptions mean impurities in the
blood. Parents should be watchful of their
daughters’ complexions, and should see to it
that these danger signs are corrected, not
covered up.
When a girl in her ’teens becomes pale
and sallow, especially if, at the same time,
she shows an inclination to tire easily, a
listlessness and inattention to her work or
studies, she needs Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
a tonic which directly and specifically cor-
rects the condition from which she is suffer-
ing. A chemical analysis of the hlood of
such a girl would show it to be deficient in
just the elements that Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills can supply, but the physical signs are
plain.
These pills combine with food and air to
actually increase the blood supply. An in-
creased appetite and capacity for energetic
action are noticeable in a few days after
their use is begun. There is no safer tonic
for growing girls, and they are obtainable
from any dealer who sells medicine.
Any reader of this paper may have a useful
booklet on “Building Up the Blood” by
writing to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co-
Box 594 G.P.0., Sydney.— Advt.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
There were about 40,000 people at City
Tattersall’s meeting on Saturday—the last of the
“big" racing days of the Autumn in Sydney. For
the next six months the pony and suburban proprie-
tary clubs will carry on business at the old address
without a holiday, and because the public will be
rushing to a couple of obscure courses every Satur-
day and adjourning to “outer circle” fixtures during
the week instead of going to Kandwiek, the morning
papers will have nothing further to say about™ the
iniquity of the “burly eligible” (and incidentally
of the ineligible citizen and the ancient and his
wife and the returned soldier) going to a race
meeting. This will be because the frequent races
will be at remote, unspectacular places instead
of one of the finest courses in the world to which
the traffic is easily detected.
The totalisator turn-over during the day was
£37,303, which it need only be remarked was but
a fraction of the money in the game, and substan-
tially less than is handled by machinery at second-
rate meetings in Maoriland. Yet limited though
the sum be, it gives, in conjunction with details of
what happened at the tote during the A.J.C. meet-
ing the previous week, very solid proof that the
public wants the machine and more inducement to
use it. A promise was made the other day by Chief
Secretary George Fuller to revise, in the interests of
country clubs, the ridiculous system of totalisator
betting they are compelled by law and regulation
to have on the premises. This page recommends
George to find out if there is even the ghost of an
excuse for not revising the system at Randwick by
introducing there a single-dividend, straight-out-on-
the-winner machine, to the public and Ike. This is
the contraption every , advocate of the machine asked
for. It is what they are still asking for. Also it
is what the Government mulishly withholds without
explanation of the why and wherefore.
* * * *
Winner-of-the-Doncaster Dame Acre,
weighed at 9st., was reckoned a sure thing by the
great army of punters for the Denman Stakes on
Saturday—and once more that valiant band saw
their reserves swept away and the Denman Stakes
annexed by an outsider. Of course on recent run-
ning the mare looked almost unbeatable. But the
start was all against her. She turned half round,
gotj tangled up in the scramble and emerged last.
That settled her. She stayed there. Meanwhile La
Steppe was out in front alone, three lengths ahead
of The Welkin gelding Greenstead. La Steppe, who
seems to have misplaced her dash, punctured half-
way up the straight, and Greenstead, running her
down, went on and. hit the line a length and a
half ahead of Warspite, who came from a long way
back. Greenstead was a winner at Randwick and
Rosehill in December. He was a 14 to 1 chance on
Saturday.
The 1100-guinea colt Son of the Desert
did his best up-to-date on Saturday, finishing sixth
in a field of 25 youngsters at Randwick. Hitherto
the expensive animal has been addicted to watching
the others from astern. But his elder half-sister,
Dessert, kept up the family reputation during the
afternoon. After running unplaced in the Denman
she was saddled up for another spin in the Welter
Mile, and triumphantly snuffed out the favorite.
Baltic Sea, winner of last year’s rich Breeders’
Plate. It seems to be taking the Baltic Sea a long
time to get over that first effort of his. Perhaps
the name he staggers under has something to do
with it. Dessert was a very high-priced prad, too,
and so far has not earned half what was paid for
her. But she has always run well up.
Any hopes Paddy Nolan may have had of
inducing The Spire to put up another time record
over hurdles at Randwick stand adjourned. The
nag is docked for repairs. The Spire had been
having a long holiday until lately, and had probably
forgotten that there are no guard rails to the jumps
at Randwick. Anyway he had not gone far on
Saturday when he took a leap right on the edge of
a hurdle and landed on the other side with a leg
astride the top of the course fence. He slid several
feet with the rail tearing a gaping wound in the
misplaced skin and flesh. Strangely enough, he
kept his feet. lie was pulled up and sewn up later.
Mehi King, winner of this year’s City
Tattersall’s Cup at Randwick, had been a horribly
unprofitable horse to a lot of people. Since running
second in Wolaroi’s V.R.C. Derby he had been
started in many important races and let his owner
and stable down practically every time. More
recently he had been started in unimportant events,
• but continued the letting down process with unim-
paired efficiency. Latterly he was sold in disgust
into the Colugo stable and appears to have approved
the change of residence. Anyway he was running on
well at the end of the A.j.C. Tocal Handicap on
Easter Monday with 9st. 61b. aboard behind Lord
Acre and Elsdon. Tatt.’s Cup had another furlong
to go, and with Bst. 41b. Mehi King looked hard to
beat, lie was backed for a hatful, went out at 4’s
and got away with the bacon. Rebus, the Sydney
Cup winner, was doing well up to the distance with
9st., but from there the King ran clean over him.
Westcourt, the Melbourne Cup winner, who was a
favorite for Sydney Cup, ran like a tired commercial
traveller. He had been galloped off his legs the
week before.
* * * *
Mooltan, a great racehorse of 10 years
ago—lie was twice winner of the Metropolitan,
second to Apologue in the Melbourne Cup and
winner of the A.J.C. Spring Stakes and Craven
Plate —was sold in Sydney the other day for £lOO.
Which is a fair discount on the £3OOO or so paid
for him at the height of his fame and the £2OOO
given to get him back by his original owners for stud
purposes. But he wasn’t a champion at the harem.
* * * *
Ron Cameron, who rode The Parisian and
appropriated the Melbourne Cup of 1911, had ridden
five winners at the Alburv meeting last week and
went out to steer another. But on the way the
animal rushed against a post, and when they lifted
Cameron off they took him to hospital. One of his
legs was badly smashed in the collision.
» * * *
“Centurion!’: The fine horsemastership of
the Australian units in Sinai and Palestine should
give one more knock to the cherished British military
Action that the Australian is a bad horsemaster. This
fiction rose out of the S’African war, and got its
start from one or two brass-hats who were called on
to account, before a Royal Commission, for the shock-
ing losses in horseflesh which marked that campaign.
As the Australians and Maorilanders, who did most
of the mounted work, lost most of the horses, they
got the blame, the fact that the Yeomanry, after
one or two disastrous experiences, were allotted gar-
rison and routine work, being ignored. The way
Australian horses were treated there was wicked. As
an examnle, take the case of the sth Q. 1.8. This
regiment, with its horses, was entrained the day of
landing after a month on the water. It was taken
a six-days’ journey in the train to Pietersburg and
marched out a day later, coming into action at Oli-
phant’s River two days after that. The country
was rotten with “horse-sickness,” and a month later
the regiment reached Pretoria 400 neddies short.
Just after that the day of the flying columns began.
A mounted column went out for 10 days with a
couple of H.A. guns and two or three pom-poms.
When it found the enemy—usually after a long and
rapid night march —it ran him down till horses and
men were exhausted. I have known such a running
fight to last for 24 hours on end, and finish with all
hands foundered miles away from the little light
transport that had been taken, and 100 or 200 miles
away from the nearest railway-line. Under these
conditions, with half-rations and often not that for
man and beast, and with blazing heat in summer and
bitter cold in winter, it is not surprising that the
Australians lost horses enough to shock yeomanry
and cavalry regiments whose mounted work consisted
of 10-mile patrols from comfortable townships on
the railway lines. Three years’ experience in the
S’African trouble and two and a half with mounted
troops in this war leaves me more than ever con-
vinced that the Australian’s care for his neddy is not
excelled anywhere, and under unfavorable conditions
cannot be equalled.
* » * *
“Mookingah” : There is always variety in
the sports programme in the North-West (N.S.W.),
but for the Novelty Stakes I enter a wrestling match,
catch-as-catch-can, between man and foal at Mil-
parinka on Easter Monday. Fred Blore, the only
nomination, is aged 70, and he brought his own
yearling with him. When the foal wandered on to
the track, the boss ordered it off. The foal treated
him with contempt, whereupon he collared the animal
low and brought it to the ground. The foal claimed
a foul, and the judge disallowed the throw. After
another round the judge considerd the foal might
get hurt, and declared the contest a draw.
Arthur Mailey, who gathered most wickets
in Sydney premiership this season (80, averaging
19.31) did the same thing and put up a premier-
ship record (102, averaging 14.04) in ’l5-T6.
“Bosey’s” wrong ’uns were unmercifully biffed in
many matches; but he got wickets, and that was
what he was after. In nine out of 13 matches more
than 100 runs were smacked off his trundling—
seven for 188 by University being the hardest
smack. Jack Scott’s 60 wickets at 11.48 apiece
is the best average among the heavy wicket
gatherers; and if Scott could obliterate the no-ball
habit he would be the most dangerous bowler at
large in Sydney.
Petersham’s outright win by 10 wickets
and eight runs over Sydney on Saturday landed the
grade premiership, and the shutters went up for the
season. The bulk of the glory that is Petersham’s
came from the efforts of Jack Scott (11 for 87)
and E. W. Adams (nine for 56), and the batting
of O. S. Smith (41 and 36 not out). As it was
Petersham’s first win the club when last heard from
had thrown its collective hat aloft, and the garment
wasn’t expected down for a week. When the round
began Waverley looked a winner; but Gordon shat-
tered its hopes by 36 runs. Waverley won the
premiership in 1901-2,
Tommy Warne, skipper of the Carlton
team, winners of the Vic. district premiership this
year, is only 48. As he started to play 31 years
ago and has been doing it ever since he seems to be
contracting the cricket habit.

narian J. Taylor, who
push-biked from Sydney
to Melbourne in 12 days
(B. 4/4,/’18), is stirring
others to emulation. In
two days, though the
actual travelling time
was 15 hours 50 min-
utes, A. Ileywood and
G. J. Lynch, walking,
and Luke Lees, propel-
ling himself by hand on
an invalid tricycle, jour-
neyed from Ballarat to
Melbourne. Lees, whose
left leg is withered and
whose right is ampu-
tated at the thigh, reck-
ons he could have cov-
ered the distance in 13
hours, but he did not
leave the walkers. A
trip of 100 miles in
three days, from Ben-
digo to Melbourne, is
contemplated.
CHARLIE
MACLEOD,
Veteran Cricketer.
In Brisbane cricket this season the
Goodna team has obliged all visiting teams with a
good afternoon’s leather-hunting. It began its last
match against University like this: Maddock, retired,
110; Farquhar, retired, 103; Burns, not out, 193 ;
Stanley, not out, 72 ; sundries, 6 ; total, 394. Inci-
dentally, Maddock succeeded in making his 1000 runs
for the season. Goodna is the Bananaland lunatic
asylum staff.
* * * *
Dave Gregory, who was another year older
on April 15, captained the first Australian XI which
toured England 40 years since. Of the dozen who
tripped under Jack Conway's managership, Tom
Garrett, G. H. Bailev, Alec and Charlie Bannerman,
Jaek Blackham, F. R. Spofforth and Dave Gregory
are still not out.
H. Y. Braddon, M.L.C. —in business life
man'ager of Dalgety’s—is again N. S. Wales Rugby
Union president. Braddon was a star in the Maori-
land XV which came to Australia in 1884 ; and he
was in later days in the first flight of oarsmen and
tennis players.
* * * »
Harry Murray Bayliss, who has had a
funeral after 30 years’ practice as a solicitor at
Wagga, was a prominent ’Varsity footballer in his
time. For three seasons he captained the Uni.
team, and led the N. S. Wales 15 against the Eng-
lish crowd which toured Australia and M.L. in 1899,
and against Maoriland and Bananaland. Some classed
him as the best full-back the world has seen. Ho
was son of Bayliss, P.M., of Wagga, who was wounded
in an encounter with the notorious bushranger
Morgan.
Dead in Bananaland, James Greddan,
holder of the Australian record for a standing high
jump with weights (sft. 7in.) and standing broad
jump with weights (13ft. sin.), as well as other Aus-
tralian records at leaping. James stood for Parlia-
mentary honors on the side of the Labor party for
Murilla a few years ago, but didn’t spring high
enough. Tiring of being a loco, driver, he started
pulling a beer pump on the Downs, and drove the
implement to the finish.
* * *
Bowlers seem to keep as far ahead of old
Father Time as heavyweight lifters and parsons.
Anyway, the roll-up of N. S. Wales bowlers who have
passed the 75-year mark produced a battalion 32
strong the other day, all of them eager to start roll-
ing ’em up. The eldest, decorated with snowy whisk-
ers, pleaded guilty to 90, and a couple of others
admitted 84. The skip of the rink that scored the
biggest majority was a mere youth of 76 who had
only been practising for 20 years or so.
N. S. Wales Public Schools’ sculling cham-
pionships on the Parramatta River last week differed
from preceding occurrences of the kind only in that
Young Australia, made more noise than before. Still
their frantic optimism was a fine tonic for the aged
spectators. In the “eights” the Grammar School bovs
squeezed home by half a length from St. Joseph's,
with C. of E. Grammar and St. Ignatius a few feet
away. The hard luck was with the Anglicans. They
had to re-seat their crew almost at the last moment
on account of the stroke being carried off to have his
appendix extracted. The disordered organ took up a
most unsportsmanlike attitude, and simply refused to
postpone itself till after the race.
An argument which lias been raging in
Sydney schools all summer was temporarily settled
on Saturday when Noel Hathaway (Cleveland-street)
and L. Bannister (North Sydney High) collided in
the senior cadet 100yds. championship. Hathaway
won in lOtsec., and took the 28th Battalion 100yds.
senior-cadet championship in 10 4-5 sec. When
these two fine young sprinters clashed on a pre-
vious occasion Bannister won in 10 4-ssec.
Hathaway won the 100yds. N.S.W. schools cham-
pionship this year; last year he won the 15
years’ 100yds. championship and put up a schools’
440yds. record (59sec.). Both lads represent top
form in Sydney schools’ athletics.
* # » «
Does anyone ever play all-fours now? In
Sydney in 1794 this ancient game and cribbage were
Tatt’s, the tote and the books rolled into two. All-
fours and cribbage were played for six, eight or 10
dollars a game. Those fer whom that was too slow-
used to toss for dollars —Sydney’s first two-up players.
* * * *
The feminine who deserves first mention in
any social column is the Black Orpington hen belong-
ing to C. E. Graham. The poultry-breeders of the
State were filled with deep emotion this week when
the cackling champion performed the wonderful old
conjuring trick once more and produced her 325th
egg for 365 days, thus beating the world’s record for
any breed. But she need not expect any Order of the
British Empire while Hughgs of Warwick is here to
crow about keels he is going to lay.
* * * *
The learned Mr. Sicksenate, counsel for
the defence in a ease concerning (he manipulation
of the ace of diamonds and a couple of spades,
heard in a Sydney court the other day, started
giving a demonstration of how to do it. But Judge
Backhouse was not having anything to do with
yarns about games of skill. “Oh, we know all
about that, Mr. Sicksenate,’’ he observed. “Of
course the man who risks money in this game with
a spieler has no possible chance of winning. It is
like the thimble and pea. The pea is not there—
nor is the ace. It is always abstracted.”
Backhouse thinks he knows a good deal more than
he really does. In the three-card game the ace is
never taken. It is always there. The chance to win
is, of course, mighty remote, because the odds always
look several thousand to one on the card which
happens to be the wrong one. A sucker will tumble
in every time. Most men who know the game will
fail to locate the winner more often than not,
because even to them there will always be baffling
uncertainty about whether there had been a double
or a single pass of the black over the red. But the
real expert who “gees” for the player and wins
bogus wagers will never make a bloomer—nor, in
most eases, omit to bring the proceedings to an
abrupt end with a punch on the nose if some deluded
ass tries to pick up the right card by mistake.
* * * *
“11.J.R.”: Noticed on my Easter visit
that sporting papers and tipsters are becoming as
numerous in Sydney as the liard-lleads who stand
outside the gates of the pony courses. Even a
couple of crackajacks from Melbourne have dug
themselves into the congestion. As a youth I regu-
larly followed a deluder who tipped for a well-known
weekly under a wonderful alias. Years afterwards
I became friendly with the real person, and he told
a joke against himself. “I had tipped 17 events
for Ascot, and didn't even get a placed horse,” he ex-
plained quite without shame. “Coming home in the
tram two dead-broke punters who had been follow-
ing my fancies were left alone in the car with me.
Said the first, ‘I wonder who the Mazes this So-and-
so is?’ The other: ‘Whoever he is he ought to have
his tips pushed down his mouth crossways.’ I felt
that if they had known my identity they would have
eaten me. Next day there was another meeting. I
had the tips in again. It was the easiest part of
the job.”
PLAYING THE GAME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“It is a dreadful reflection on the idle rich men
of Melbourne, who do nothing but play golf all day.
I know some who say they are too old to go to the
Front, and that they would like to do patriotic
work, but the matter ends there.” —Piece spoken at
the monthly meeting of the L.M.P. League, Mel-
bourne.
The air was mild and balmy, with faint incenses
afloat,
Among the tea-tree, zephyr-borne—no sad, forebod-
ing note
In Nature’s song, no sombre tones in Autumn’s golden
glow;
Oh, no!
A blaze of heavenly blue above and gracious green
below;
Over the cliff-top, cradle-croon, the lullaby of the
sea,
And down the breeze a peewit’s burst of merry
melody.
Right in the midst of all these things a large, round
patriot stood,
And there addressed his ball as such a portly patriot
should.
“Ahem!” he said, “I strike this blow for dear old
England.” [Whack!]
“A smack
For England and the Empire!” llow they thumped
him on the back
And cheered him to the ei ho as the ball sped to its
goal,
Rolling at last to rest a bare six inches from the
hole!
“Bravo!” they cried who watched him there. “By
Jov;, you are the one!”
And Full-and-plenty arched his chest, seeing the
deed he’d done —
The calm, strong act of one who would avert the
Empire's fall. .. .
That’s all.
Who? What? Why, yes, of course they do! They
heed the nation’s call,
Each of ’em doing the best he can, doing his little
bit
By—hem!—well, playin’ the game, y’ know, and —
hum, ah! —keepin’ fit.
VICTORIA.
O. L.


A Skilful Boxer Fears No Foel
A skilful Boxer is a man to be envied and admired.
He has himself under complete control. He has been taught to be
patient and tolerant. He is not easily “put out,” and usually is the last
to seek a quarrel.
When he is forced, however, to defend himself, he does it with a quiet
calm that breathes self-confidence in his every action. He is not flurried
in the least He has a set plan, and adapts himself to circumstances. He
reserves his strength until the other fellow is getting “groggy.” He is
always doing the right thing at the right time.
Would you like to be a skilful boxer —to be able to give aggressive
individuals a couple of stone and a beating? Then, why not learn
Scientific Boxing from me in your own home?
I Teach Boxing by Post.
I want to tell you all about my methods. How
simple and interesting they are. What rapid pro-
gress you’ll make, and what healthful exercise you
will secure from my instruction.

In Samuel Butler’s Erehwon, you may not
remember, there ivere “straighteners” who
were called in by the rich to flagellate them
for their sins. Maybe this co. serves as a
“straightener” for John Grice, Bowes Kelly
and the other very affluent and successful
men avlio continue to sit on the Board. It
is certainly the most distressful co. that these
people hang on to. It has never paid a
dividend on ordinary capital, though it is
uoav 21 years old, and it isn't getting visibly
nearer one. Some years ago it funded
£12.000 then owing to 4 p.c. cumulative pref.
capital, undertaking to pay off £OOO a year;
but instead of shifting, the dead horse has
had a foal, and now the “Funded dividend
certificates and interest” item stands at
£17,065. Also the co. doesn’t own all its
line; it leases part from the Emu Bay and
Mt. Bisclioff Co., and £11,275 is owing for
“Deferred rent and interest.” Also there is
some deferred debenture debt. These three
items amount to £40,400. There is £lOO,OOO
of pref. capital; and, though only 4 p.c. is
cumulative, the shareholders are entitled, to
another 4 p.c. before the ordinaries
(£105.000) get a bite. Last year Avas par-
ticularly disastrous: in the early months a
bridge was washed away, and not only did
that mean a renewal, but it made traffic
costly and lost some. So the figures work out
like this :
1913. 1916. 1917.
More or less liquid assets £15,572 £21,334 £1 1 ,716
Liabilities (apart from de-
bentures).. .. 21,448 36,014 44,1 ;>8
Excess liabilities .. £5,876 £14,680 £26,442
If the entire cost of the bridge is charged to
repairs, then the year’s net revenue wasn t
sufficient to pay the rent of the leased line;
and there were still left the debentures and
the pref. shares to pay. But there are some
people Avho still think that there is a hope.
They mostly rely upon what Mt. Lyell will
do with the Rosebery group of mines; and
immigration, of course, after the war. This
paper isn’t going to say anything further to
disturb their dreams.

This co., with a big pastoral connection,
carried on business in Victoria and Queens-
land until 1916. Then it issued new shares
in N. S. Wales and established itself there
also, taking in John Mair, for many years
secretary of the Pastoralists’ Union, as local
manager. The new shareholders got into a
very profitable business and paid a very low
price for the privilege. Look at the solid
progress made in 10 years:—
1908. 1918.
Public securities .. ..£36,155 £57,637
Other debentures .. .. 2,985
Loans on mortgage . . . . 22,506 9,589
Real estate .. .. 9,059 60,709
Fixed deposits and sundries .. 10,250 8,895
Total assets.. £80,955 £136,830
Less Liabilities .. .. 36 7,449
Net assets ..£80,919 £129,381,.
Meantime the capital has been increased
from £73,822 to £lOO,OOO, and £4013 net was
received as premiums on the neiv shares;
total neiv capital introduced, £30,191. Thus
the co. has nearly £5OOO more assets apart
from office property than it had 10 years
ago; and the additional office property cost
well over £50.000, of which, say, £30,000 has
been provided by new capital. From that
the half-year’s dmdend has to be deducted,
leaving for the shares 255. of assets apiece.
In effect, therefore, the new shareholders
paid nothing for goodwill. The value of the
estates in hand isn’t disclosed; but it is
fairly obvious that the co. is now doing a
much larger business than it did 10 years
ago. Then commission earnings were £9012
and rent and interest were £3526; total
£12,538. Last year total earnings (after
providing for contingencies) were £28.592.
Allow £5750 for rent and interest earnings,
and there remains £22,842 for commission,
indicating an increase during the 10-year
period of 150 p.c. And there is no visible
reason why progress on much the same lib-
eral scale should not be continued. For,
apart from the general favor into which
trustee cos. are steadily coming, an office
Avhose organisation extends over Victoria.
N. S. Wales and Queensland must make a
special appeal to men with scattered inter-
ests ; and, though everybody is going to have
his pockets heavily assaulted presently,
squatters and stockowners will probably come
out. on the Avhole, .as well as anybody. They
have made a good start, at any rate.

H. P. STURGI&, FRED. G. PARBUIiY, W. B. PERCEVAL, Directors.
A. C. WILLIS, Manager. W. A. LAING, Accountant.
London, Bth January, 1918.
AUDITORS’ CERTIFICATE AND REPORT.
In accordance with the provisions of Section 113 of the Companies (Consolidation) Act 1908, we
report to the shareholders as follows: —
We have examined the above balance-sheet with the books at head office, and have had access
to the branch accounts transmitted to the head office from Australia and New Zealand. We have verified
the correctness of the cash, money at call and short notice, investments and the bills of exchange held in
London, and, having obtained all the information and explanations we have required, we are of opinion
that such balance-sheet is properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the state of
the company’s affairs, according to the best of our information and the explanations given to us, and
as shown by the books of the company and the said branch accounts.
WHINNEY, SMITH and WHINNY, Chartered Accountants, Auditors.
London, Bth January, 1918.

No notice (except of a hostile character') will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped,
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

With Lewis gun and rifle fire,
Our boys smashed the Bodies into pulp;

They knew that they were well beat;

The few score inhabitants on Pitcairn
Island may be right on the edge of nowhere;
hut they can give Australia a lead when it
comes to shipbuilding. In 1916, seeing dan-
ger ahead, they decided that they had better
build a schooner so that they might keep in
touch with the rest of the world. But they
did not send to U.S.A. or make speeches.
They wanted a shin: they didn’t want to
show up the disloyalty and general wicked-
ness of the labor unions. So they went
ahead with the materials they had, even
forging nails out of odd bits of old iron, and
in 12 months they had the schooner away
on the 1200-mile run to Tahiti.
He who walks in narrow unlighted ways
on dark nights is bound to hump into things.
Sometimes the other party to the bump is a
criminal; sometimes a cow; generally an un-
suspected public debt. Thus Australia con-
trives to owe nearly £11,000,000 on wheat
overdraft. J. Bull paid for a lot of wheat
which he hasn’t received ; the farmer doesn’t
get much, and some of that little is in
arrear ;• and there is the overdraft in addi-
tion. So there are three ghosts sitting on
the wheat bag. If there were four ghosts the
bag would probably be fined for overcrowd-
inr*

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Mature. For service, comfort aud good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
ELDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free, 1/3. Free sample posted.

Orchard, Minister for Recruiting, is the
amateur actor of the Federal Government.
The other 13 or so are professionals.
“S.S.” : The cable announces the intention
of Woodrow Wilson to take over all the
U.S. railways. There is, however, an impor-
tant consideration which, I am convinced,
the President has not weighed. I have seen
four long picture films within a week, each
of which depended largely for its success
upon a liberal use of at least one great
American railway system. So far as I can
understand it, the whole service was at the
beck and call of the picture producer. This
is quite a common thing, and how is
Wilson going to . provide for the pic-
ture demand if he uses the railways
of America for his paltry war? The
war has its followers, no doubt, but if a
beautiful Francis Bushman film is ever held
up for the need of a few railway smashes,
Wilson will find out where the heart of the
country really is.

starred a couple of
tiny children, said
to be five. The

“First Old Man” : Worthy of record that
the first complete theatrical combination to
travel to Westralia by the East-West line will
be J. and N. Tait’s “Turn to the Right” com-
pany, which plays Perth and Ivalgoorlie when
it has got through its Melbourne season. The
“bad sailors” of the crowd will rejoice,
though the low comedian w T ill have no chance
to crack a wheeze regarding “the Great Aus-
tralian Bite.”
“75”: Back in Melbourne, looking none
the worse for three years’ active service,
Lieutenant J. C. “Blandolt” Morris, who,
before more exciting work was offering,
appeared with credit in various Williamson
productions (notably “Joseph and His
Brethren”) and with Harry Corson Clarke
during his Melbourne season. He is about
to be given his discharge, and intends getting
into grease-paint again.
One of the strangest plays on record is
“Out of Hell,” just produced in London. It
is in four acts, and yet has only four char-
acters —and two players ! Twin sisters, one
married in Germany, have sons exactly
alike. The young German goes to England
in his captive cousin’s uniform, and at first
deceives his aunt, who presently detects him
and denounces him as the spy that he is.
Then the Englishman escapes and calls on
his German aunt, who is killed in helping
him to get free. Finally he is hack in
England, but his mother’s mind has given
way and she refuses to acknowledge him—
till close on curtain-fall. The general verdict
is that it is a fine, thrilling war sketch, but
unduly padded out to make a full evening’s
bill. Also the one man and one woman who
double the parts, however clever they may
be, cannot avoid a certain monotony.
“Thula”: The lady with a powerful voice,
who alarmed the audiences at a series of
concerts in Sydney Town Hall lately, put
up a performance reminiscent of the punish-
ment inflicted by Albani on an audience out
west. The veteran arrived on tin* scene a
few hours before the concert was to begin,
weary and jaded
with travel, but
keen as a razor to
get in touch with
the local piano-
tuner. He was run

into the presence.
Placing a tuning-

she ordered him
to .tune the piano

That meant lower-

about a tone and
a half. The tuner
knew the job
couldn’t be done in
the limited time,
but being a soci-
able citizen felt
there was nothing
to be gained by
worrying the Even-
ing Star with de-
tails. So he oblig-
ingly made the
pitch of both in-
struments identical
by doctoring the
fork! When the
test was applied a
few moments be-
fore the concert
began, Albani was
satisfied. She be-

at Melbourne King’s.

parson.
confidently and lightly. Then- ! Not once
did she come within cooee of the right note.
But her remarks about tuning-fork jugglers
omitted nothing.
“Amoi Ivito”: Harry Ingham, who
whipped up the orchestra to chase the flying
feet of vaudevillains under Harry Rickards’s
management for many years, has gone to take
the beat of the angels. Of late years he had
been keeping count of the flickers on the
screen at Hoyt's, and at latest was in one of
the suburban picture shows. He was a genial
soul teeming with happy reminiscences.
Rickards was watching with Ingham a
thought-reading act in which one partner was
in the gallery picking out articles that would
be named by' the other who was on the stage.
Suddenly the boss growled: “Do you mean to
tell me that he can read what is in your
mind?” “Oh, yes, governor, that’s what the
game is.” “Then if he can read what is in
my mind, he knows I think I am paying a
damn sight too much for that turn !”
London papers spell the name of Marie
Lohr, the new manageress, with two dots
over the o, in the German way. Daddy Lohr
never spelt it like that. Daddy’s father was
an English clergyman.
“Invictus” : Re the popularity of children’s
plays in London (B. 21/3/’18). I recently
got a letter from an H.Q. man telling of
his difficulty in securing seats for “Peter
Pan.” “I applied on December 14 for five
seats,” he says, “and managed to get three
in front and two behind for December 31.
Five reserved seats together were not obtain-
able till January 15, a whole month ahead.”

“Harrison O.”: It is dangerous for a
dramatist to allege that one of his characters
is a genius and then try to prove it by pro-
ducing samples of his work. The* audience is
invited to believe that the hero of “The
Cinderella Man” is a poet of purest ray
serene, but at an early stage some of his
mushy verses are read (they are about a
bloke building a suburban residence out of
his heart), and the judicious are unable to
accept the invitation. In the second act the
poet is shown in the throes of composition,
lie seizes his pen and for some time behaves
as if flea-bitten in 10 places. Then he
declaims the result —four lines that any
purple-pill rhymester could have invented in
his sleep. ' Subsequently the hero writes a
grand-opera libretto which makes his for-
tune, and the dramatist misguidedly sup-
plies a few extracts. The blunder is re-
peated in regard to another character sup-
posed to be a famous composer. He is made
to warble one of his own songs, and it is
the sort of ditty that many of us compose
every morning while soaping ourselves. It
should be a rule with all dramatists to allow
the allegedly great artistic masterpieces per-
petrated by their puppets to be taken as
read.
“C. Ockney”: After all, capable “legiti-
mate” mummers seem easier to procure in
London these days than f.a.q. music-hall
entertainers. Anyhow, many of the West
End Vaudeville “palaces” are giving up their
turns and putting on a full-length theatrical
show instead. Even the old-established
Leicester Square house, the Empire, has
given up variety and gone in for musical
comedy. Its latest attraction is an American
sample, “The Lilac Domiwo.”
Doris Keane, the Yankee actress who made
a London hit in “Romance” three years ago
and has been playing the role ever since, has
just committed matrimony with Basil
Sydney, who became her leading man last
July.
“J.F.11.”: Bernard Shaw’s latest war
sketch, produced at London Coliseum, is
called “Annajanska, the Wild Grand
Duchess.” It is the poorest stuff imagin-
able, a disjointed, incoherent skit upon the
revolution in Russia or Beotia, as he pre-
fers to spell it. It is a pity to see Lillah
McCarthy wasting her sweetness on such
stage-desert air. She is supposed to be an
escaped Russian Duchess, captured and
brought before one of the revolutionary
Generals by a couple of villainous-looking
Guards. The usual Shaw topsy-turvy
dialogue ensues, and finally Lillah discharges
a revolver, throws off her feminine garments
and reveals underneath them the white uni-
form of the Russian Imperial Guards. The
curtain comes down on this situation, leav-
ing the audience in a state of hopeless
bewilderment as to the aim or meaning of
the thing. It falls as flat as the beef of
yesterday.
"Stargazer” : Applause has lost its signifi-
cance, more particularly first-night applause.
There was a time when one could judge from
the plaudits at the conclusion of a “first pro-
duction here” what place the play and the
playing held in public esteem, but nowanights
rampant enthusiasm is more likely to follow
on the heels of a performance that has been
an unmitigated failure than to reward the
efforts of actors and author in a very satis-
factory entertainment. Managements discov-
ered the advertising advantages of public
enthusiasm, and, assuming that fictitious
demonstrations would serve the same purpose,
made arrangements for turning on applause
when wanted, with the result that persistent
recalls of principals follow upon a produc-
tion that has bored the audience stiff, and
has no hope of becoming a draw. The
idea that you can delude people who have
had a wearisome time into the belief that they
are, oh, so happy, and have had a delightful
night, oy making a joyous tumult all about
them, is characteristic of the managerial
mind that no longer pretends to know what
the public wants, but aims to bamboozle it
into wanting what it gets.

“First Usher” : Guy Bates Post, whom the
Taits will exhibit in "The Masquerader”
shortly, owes much of his significance in
Not) Yark to Richard Walton Tully, a Cali-
fornian producer who gave him a big chance
in “Omar the Tentmaker,” adapted from the
poem of “Omar Khayyam.” Mainly owing
to Tully’s indifference to artistic propriety
in the arrangement of his effects, this reckless
perversion of Mr. Khayyam’s philosophy ran
three years, and Guy Bates Post enjoyed a
deserved share of its income and celebrity.
Later on, Tully wreaked some more of his
stage mechanics on an undiscerning world in
“The Bird of Paradise,” and again booked
the mightily useful Post for the job of the
beachcomber. Th result was an embarrass-
ing diversion of greenbacks into Tully’s
pockets and a growth of popular curiosity
that hasn’t been sated in six seasons. By
this time Post was looking like a mascot, so
the old reliable was commanded to Jek.vll-
H.vde the characters of Loder and Chilcote
in a sensational resurrection of “John Chil-
cote, M.P.” This piece, “The Masquerader,”
caused another bulge in the bank account,
and has made the Taits’ financial soul soar
with enthusiasm. If the show doesn’t prove
a success here, the Taits’ confidence in their
own judgment will be severely shaken.

comes into a million and a quarter dollars.

From London:—
Ada Crossle.v has settled down to music-hall work.
She has lately been singing- at the Palladium, to the
great joy of the box-office. Rene Maxwell is getting
plenty of engagements and has been offered one by
Beecham to appear in London in grand opera, re-
cently she made a hit as deputy for Rosina Buck-
man at Brighton. Ivy Shilling is touring with
“Topsy Turvy” and is a favorite everywhere. Butt,
Grossmith and Laurillard want her for future pro-
ductions, and she has received good offers from the
U.S.A. Marie Lohr has struck a winner with
Somerset Maugham’s “Love in a Cottage,” her first
production under her own management. Enid Ben-
nett and Annette Kellerman are here on the films.
Both are billed as “American” actresses. America,
having “come in,” is in the boom just now.
THE ARTIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ho took his pen with brave intent
To write a story wondrous true.
The best his genius could invent,
And strove at it the whole night through.
His heart resolved that it should shine,
With wit wrought into every line.
When done he judged it “rather tine."
“It’s bright enough ; the theme is new ;
Vet not the thing I meant to do.”
lie felt again his being glow
With inspiration for a song.
The splendid rhythm seemed to flow
His vibrant, dancing pen along,
lie wrote it with his heart’s blood red,
And then he conned it o'er and said:
“ ’Twill sell—my children will be fed.
But far is it as God’s own view
From that dear thing 1 meant to do.”
So on in years of patient toil
He strove all times with honest zest
To cull from his soul’s fruitful soil
The rainbow flower, his Spirit’s best.
And still his best, elusive, sly,
Mocked at him with a laughing eye,
And lured him yet again to try.
And ever with an impulse new
He sought the thing he meant to do.
Then, pen in hand, the artist died ;
Half writ the book he meant to write.
Through starless spaces high and wide
He sought and found the Realm of Light.
There still he starts his perfect song,
The gem-like thing a half-span long.
To hear him all the angels throng.
He heeds them not, but seeks anew
The precious thing he means to do.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For this
purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair-
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair, but to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Removing, Her Skin.
The quickest and surest way to make a
good complexion out of a bad one is to
actually remove the thin veil of faded or dis-
colored outer skin from the face. This can
he done easily, safely and privately by any
woman. The process is one of gentle absorp-
tion.
Get some pure mercolised wax from your
chemist and apply it at night like cold cream,
washing it off in the morning. In a few
days tiie mercolide in the wax will have
absorbed the disfiguring cuticle, revealing
the fresh young skin underneath; you will
have a beautiful, clear, natural complexion.
The process is pleasant and harmless, making
the face look brilliantly attractive and youth-
ful. It also effectually removes such blem-
ishes as freckles, tun, moth patches, liver
spots, pimples, etc. Every woman should
keep a little mercolised wax on hand, as this
simple, old-fashioned remedy is the best com-
plexioued beautifler and preserver known.
Blackheads Instantly Go.
A very simple, harmless and pleasant pro-
cess is now used to remove blackheads and
correct greasiness and large pores in the
skin. You have only to drop a tablet of
stymol, obtained from the chemist, into a
glass of hot water and bathe the face with
the liquid after the effervescence has sub-
sided. The blackheads will then come right
off on the towel. The enlarged pores immedi-
ately contract to normal and the greasiness
disappears, leaving the skin smooth, soft and
cool and free from blemish. But to make sure
that this desirable result is permanent it is
advisable to repeat the treatment several
times at intervals of say about four or five
days.
Grey Hair Unnecessary.
One need not resort to the very question-
able expedient of hair dye in order not to
have grey hair. The grey hair can easily be
changed back to a natural color in a few
days’ time merely by the application of a
simple, old-fashioned and perfectly harmless
home-made lotion. Procure from your chem-
ist two ounces of tammalite concentrate and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this to the hair a few times with a small
sponge, and you will soon have the pleasure
of seeing your grey hair gradually darkening
to the desired shade. The lotion is pleasant,
not sticky or greasy, and does not injure the
hair in any way.
Naturally Wavy Hair.
Good stallax not only makes the best possi-
ble shampoo, but has the peculiar property of
leaving the hair with a pronounced natural
“wave,” an effect presumably desired by many
ladies. A teaspoonful of the stallax granules,
dissolved in a cup of hot water, is ample for
a luxurious head wash, and leaves the hair
with a peculiar lustre and flufflness obtainable
from nothing else as far as known. It is per-
fectly harmless, and can be obtained from
most chemists, although since it has been but
little used for this purpose it comes only in
jib. sealed original packages, enough to make
up 25 or 30 individual shampoos. [2

The peril of the soaring wages-board
award : Get a log and lose a billet.
“Haka”: The Maoriland Scot dies hard.
Three years ago the cookies down Balclutha
way proposed that the worn-out nags of the
district should be boiled and exported
for sale to the Belgians. So stirred was
McSnavle by the plight of Belgium that he
offered to sacrifice Mag and Dick at cost
price. On that occasion Massey replied
that he would see the McFeersons in the
heatworks before he would sanction the
State-export brand being affixed to potted
ponies and frozen fillies. Now the local
Sandies up Wairoa way are at it again: on
this occasion they ask that the toothsome
grub be exported for sale to Britishers.- One
gentleman pointed out that they had a sur-
plus of 30.000 nags and the grass would be
available for fattening stock if these were
canned. Also (good old wheeze!) it would
“improve the breed of horses.” This philan-
thropist’s name was Vile. So is the
price we are making Britain and our Allies
pay for greasy wool —up to 24£ck, the
highest ever obtained in Maoriland.
“A Female of the Species” from Kansas: —
I agree with “Bad American” (B. 22/11/T7)
that "Good Australian” (B. 25/10/'T7) doesn’t
know what he’s talking about when he states
that “rents of houses, flats and apartments in
’Frisco are twice and thrice as high as in Mel-
bourne.” F.ven during Fair-time in San Francisco I
paid only £5 a month for an apartment on Nob
Hill in a first-class house with hotel service. It
was furnished and ready for instant habitation, and
if you can show me this for 255. a week in Mel-
bourne or Sydney I’ll eat my new Spring hat. I
can’t understand why Mftnly and our other resorts
have not a dozen or more of these compact, .clean
and serviceable buildings, instead of weatherboard
hot-houses with the dried-up gardens and dingy
hangings and obsolete kitchens.
“7833” : The military are very particular
about the rationing of troops. When Pte.
Billjim marches out from one camp to
another, or from the training-camp to have
his first smack at Fritz, he carries his rations
and a certificate to show he has had them
issued to him. If he goes to hospital rations
and certificate accompany him. Each week-
end a return is submitted to H.Q. showing
the number of men marching in or out, with
or without rations. I found this on a
return a few days ago:—
Name. Marched Out To. Were Rations taken ?
Pte. Smith. (Deceased.) Yes.
Maybe he’ll need them ; they say it’s a long
march.
“James Bildad”: O Bulletin, I pray you
to print this in a conspicuous part of
your valued paper. I would like it to
go next to advertising matter, but that is
not essential. It is a true prophecy. The
ship on which Hughes and Cook leave for
England on their great mission will dis-
appear. A German raider will get it, but
that will not be known till long afterwards,
and not even then. The message which they
throw overboard in a bottle requesting that
the whole British fleet be sent to their rescue
will miscarry, and the attempt will lead to
them being confined in the coal bunkers —not
both in one bunker lest they should dominate
one another, but each in a bunker by himself.
Gradually it will soak into the public com-
prehension that the ship is late; then that it
is later; finally that it is too late altogether.
In a remote and flat part of Prussia the
pair will be interned, and warned that all
appeals of any kind are useless and will
only lead to a further reduction of their
sau'er-kraut. Meanwhile their salaries will
run on until some indignant member asks
if this is an honorarium or a Marathon
race. Attempts to shift them by vote of
censure will be ruled out of order on the
ground that there is no precedent, and the
Governor-General will refuse to accept the
Acting Prime Minister’s resignation on the
ground that the disappearance of two mem-
bers of the Cabinet is quite irregular. The
British authorities will hunt up all known
precedents, and in despair will abolish the Aus-
tralian Constitution, thereby making an end
of the Parliament and the Ministry, and
the accumulation of Hughes’s and Cook’s
salaries. The Governor-General, after being
an autocrat for 24 hours, during which time
he will give a great ball and charge it to
revenue, will grant a new Constitution just
like the old one, and after an election, at
which unparalleled excitement and a great
issue of paper money will prevail, everything
will start again.
“T.F.M.” adds a footnote: —
Port Albert was settled, as surmised by “I Say”
(B. 28/3/T8), prior to Mallaeoota Inlet; but the
Singapore adventurers he refers to were not its true
Pilgrim Fathers. To three sailor brothers named
Martin belong that distinction. Perhaps the many
swamps around Port Albert reminded them of the fens
of their native Lincolnshire; anyhow, they squatted
there with their families in 1840, and probably
the reason why historical savants make no mention
of them is that they were not big' land sharks,
but were content ’ with just as many acres as they
could put to profitable use. They were an enter-
prising trio. While one was on a visit to Laun-
ceston he bought an elephant that some speculative
sailormen had imported from Ceylon, and in due
course the brute was landed at Port Albei't. After
making himself useful for some years as a farm
laborer Jumbo absconded, his bones being discov-
ered in the ti-tree long afterwards. Port Albert
was not named —as is the general belief —in compli-
ment to Queen Victoria’s husband, but after the first
white child born at the settlement, Albert Buntine.
This child's mother had established a claim
to be considered a sturdy old pioneer when she
died in 1896. The stories still told in Gippsland
about her would fill a book. She could ride after
stock, kill and dress a bullock, use a pick and
shovel, swing Douglas, split posts and rails, erect
fencing, strip bark, and drive a bullock-team; she
was, in fact, the first bulloeky to take a drayload
of stores through the ranges to Walhalla. Another
notability among the pioneers of South Gippsland
was the chief of the Macdonells of Glengarry, who
landed at Port Albert in 1842, with a contingent
of his clan, with intent to become a cow-cocky
on a big scale; but the enterprise turned out a
failure, and the chieftain left Australia. He was
the son and heir of the man who figures as Fergus
Mclvor in Scott’s “Waverley.”

You know what children are!
They will slip off to school in the rain without hat,
coat or goloshes and run round the school ground
without hat or coat when the cold winds are blowing.
When you know they have done these things nip the
probable cold in the bud by giving them Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy.
Very often the first intimation you have that
they sat all day in school with damp clothes and
wet feet is when one complains of sore throat
and another has the snuffles.
But Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy has never
failed you yet, and even if it is too late to nip the
cold in the bud it will soon be a thing of the
past if you give them this wonderful medicine
in frequent doses.
Colds are the special bane of childhood and a
succession of colds, or even neglect of a common
cold, will often lay the foundation for chronic
bronchial affections of later life.
Don’t wait till your child is sick but keep
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy on hand ready
for instant use when needed.

“The following list of previous occupa-
tions of some of the staff in the pay offices
illustrates the strange incongruity of their
present work: jockey, pearl-buyer, letter-
carrier, nail-maker, theatrical artist, wTin-
dow-dresser, horse-dealer, butcher, linesman,
blacksmith.” — Extract from Defence Com-
mission's report.
“Hadji”: Curious thing that the Angels of
Mons didn’t put in an appearance last month
at the Somme. They must have known that
they were needed even worse than they were
at Mons. Possibly they were just sulking:
you can’t expect even an angel to work over-
time if you cast doubts upon his reality.
The hangman did not have to wait more
than a month after Foundation Day for a
job. Australia’s first execution took place in
February, 17S8. The subject was James
Barrell, found guilty of robbing the public
store in company with three others whom
Phillip pardoned.
Returned wounded, to resume his job in
the electrical branch of Melbourne City
Council, Sapper F. A. C. Fynmore. He is
a branch of a fine old tree. Ilis dad, a
captain, fought in the Maori War. His
grandad was a middy at Trafalgar, where
also his great-grandad fought as a captain
of marines. The middy entered the navy
in 1808, transferred to the marines five years
later, and died a lieut.-colonel in 1887, aged
94, the last Trafalgar veteran.
Proposed by a member of the British Com-
mons that everybody with a certain amount
of wealth or more than that amount should
be deemed to be dead so that the death duties
could be collected immediately. The Vene-
tian republic, at a time of dreadful stress, did
almost as well as that. It put a tax on dead
bodies, and burial wasn’t allowed till the
duty was paid. If the family couldn’t or
wouldn’t pay the neighbors did —especially in
hot weather.
“Machete’s” star of other days:—
Many an old-timer will be sorry to hear of the
demise of “Lady” Barrett in Brisbane. She was the
daughter-of a well-known couple who kept a roadside
inn on the Ipswich-Brisbane road in the gay old
coaching days. That was in the ’5O-'6O period of
last century, and her ladyship was something of a
belle with the Dundreary-whiskered squatters and
others who rode up and down the highway to the
Downs, or flocked to Ipswich races. It was in those
days that she first wore the mid-Victorian riding habit
and the immortal top-hat. She had long threatened a
volume of reminiscences; but for many a year she
had been “persuaded” not to publish it.
Captain W. J. Varley, who got the Military
Cross lately, left as a trooper in Australia’s
first contingent in 1914, and struck trouble
on his first day at Gallipoli. He got fixed
up in time to do six months on the Peninsula,
winning a mention in despatches and a
D.C.M. Invalided to England, he was
offered a commission in British field artillery,
and collected two more wound-stripes and a
second mention in despatches. A younger
brother, who left Australia as a private a
year later, is now acting-major, M.C. Father
is postal inspector at Armidale (N.S.W.).
“Eight Bells” : The late Governor Bras-
sey’s appetite for beer was not fostered only
by the push-bike (B. 7/3/’18). One of his
favorite thirst-raisers was rowing. In the
early hours of the morning he could be seen
pulling a little, fat dinghy on the Yarra
close to the vice-regal roost, with a large
demijohn planted firmly between the knees.
He probably disliked rowing as much as he
did cycling; anyway the oars were swung in
at frequent intervals and —up went the
demijohn! The old chap had infinite
capacity for the brown stuff, and the cellar-
man at Government House would back s’Lud-
ship to outdrink the best pint-lifting sun-
downer that ever had his bottom teeth
under beer.
The record of a crew that has pulled its
weight:—
In the schoolroom at Tortoise Head Island, AVest-
ernport (Vic.), hangs a photo, of a crew of lads
from the training-ship John Murray, who, on April
16, 1916, defeated a crew of trainees of H.M.A.S.
Pioneer. The lads are (or were) Percy Duffy,
enlisted and wounded; Frank Roach, who now wears
stars on his shoulder-straps; Maurice S. Purcell,
killed in France, aged 20; Lieut. Austin Ilarde,
D. 5.0., who, after going to sea, enlisted as a pri-
vate ; young Callender, who donned khaki at 16, and
lost a leg and a finger at the Front; and George
Mott, who is somewhere ~t sea.
“The Old Soldier”: Only 103 years have
passed, yet what a change since Waterloo!
When we read of a battle-line over 50 miles
long, it seems hardly credible that Welling-
ton’s army, on which the history of Europe
hung, stretched only two miles and a quarter.
Ludendorff, so one cable said, occupies a
remote building with rugs hung over the
doors to keep out the sound of distant battle.
Wellington and Napoleon had their armies
within view, if not actually within reach
of their voices. Through the whole
action the Duke remained close to the
Guards, ordering them to lie down and stand
up as seemed necessary. He went into one of
their squares when the French Cuirassiers
charged, and when the charge failed he
ordered the breaking up of the squares, and
told the men to lie down again.
The Hun is no respecter of Generals who
have outlived their Frightfulness. Von
Mackensen, who led the drive against Russia
and also directed the furious rushes into
Serbia and Roumania, is now a comparative
outcast in charge of a few divisions in the
Roumanian shambles. On the other hand, his
lieutenant, Von Gallwitz, who helped him
in Serbia, is a “head” on the Western Front,
enjoying the same status as Albrecht of
AVurtemberg, Rupprecht of Bavaria and
Clown Prince Willie. The three royalties
are the only heads who have maintained
their commands since the beginning. A-’on
Ivluck, who saved his side at the Marne;
A r on Ilausen, who nearly precipitated dis-
aster in the same extensive conflict; the
patriarchial fraud Von Haeseler and several
more generals have all retired into carpet
slippers. But the royal geniuses still, hang
on. Whatever they do they will never lose
their jobs. The legend of their infallibility
persists through the simple process of invent-
ing their victories and blaming their defeats
on to somebody else.
