Nile Water.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Who drinks Nile water must return.” —Egyptian
proverb .
MnRICIvLE of brooks in the mountain
places,
Silver laughter in lips of fern ;
Deep green pools, where the fairies faces
Glimmer up from the quiet burn ;
But through the thrumming of pixy fingers
The call of the far-off waters lingers,
Murmuring ever “You must return.”
Oh clear blue eyes that reflect the laughter,
Oh, yellow head to my breast compelled,
Do you know how the past comes crying after
Till strange brown links with the gold will
weld V
Till the restless ache for the long sand-
shimmer
Makes foregrounds fade to a background
dimmer
That other secrets held?
The plains roll down to the merry river,
The merry river will court the sea ;
And the small green leaves in the reflex
quiver;
Fecund and fair is the homeland lea,
With daisy heads in the clovers showing
But Over There comes the samiel blowing
Out of the bosom of mystery.
With the rose of your palms you seem to
hold me
To the white oasis between your breasts,
And your innocent prides of love enfold me
To make a wall round the sheltered nests.
Hyssop and date on horizons dim
And a sand-blown landscape that has no rim
To baffle a spirit’s quests.
Amber beads in the hennaed fingers,
Flash of gold in the grey bazaar,
Faint blue haze on a mosque that lingers,
The tomb of a Sheik set high and far
To watch for the rest of their widowed lives
The pranks of his young, forgetful wives
And fret at the grim death bar!
Lamplight over your smooth white pillow ;
Starlight out on the pale lagoon ;
A cedar boat by the bending willow
Still warm from the kisses of afternoon ;
I remember the long brown waters creeping
To a goat-hair tent.... where there was no
sleeping.
I remember a desert moon.
I remember a dawn in the night’s heart hid
That broke like a pearl from a velvet case;
I remember the shape of a pyramid
Rose at day, in that lonely place ;
I remember.... a tent-flap thrust apart
And a sudden knife in a leaping heart
And my breath still on her face!
Then I look at you, as you lie there dream-
ing—
God! of me, and the thing I mean....
And I hear the wind through the black hills
screaming,
And I feel the sting where its lash has
been,
And I see the Bedouin fall, and lie
With his dark face masked, till the storm
sweeps by,
And I find.... the years bettveen!
The brown Nile waters —a serpent twining
In and out of the fateful land ;
A hot red sun in a clear sky shining,
An amber bead in a woman’s hand ;
And I know, if we be a life apart,
The knife is still in her hidden heart,
Though it rust beneath the sand.
Yellow hair on the pillow lying;
Lids on the blue eyes folded down ;
A soft blue bow all the gold uptying;
A blue rosette on the snow-white gown—
Gold and blue... .and all for my loving.
But a shadow over the sands is moving
And the long Nile tides are brown.
Trickle of brooks in the mountain places;
Silver laughter from lips of fern;
Deep green pools where the fairies’ faces
Glimmer up from the quiet burn.
But through the thrumming of pixy fingers
The call of the far-off waters lingers
Murmuring ever “You will return !”
QUEENSLAND.
M. FOREST.
Point Piper.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was in the forenoon of a fine January
day in 1820 when the praiseworthy
Captain Piper, who held his Majesty’s
commission as Naval Officer at Port Jack-
son, received word from the signal-station
up in Fort Phillip that a ship had been
sighted from the South Head. Whereupon,
as was the function of his office, he ordered
out his boat and its crew and proceeded
down the harbor to meet her.
The Naval Officer, long since extinct, was
a curious bird who was not wholly a custom
house, a marine board, a water-police sys-
tem, a harbor trust nor a port admiral, but
a little of each and of all together. He
was a kind of superior “warden of the port,’’
and had a harbor-master and a pilot service
under him.
John Piper—lately of the notorious New
South Wales Corps, and one of the some-
what exceptional decent officers of that
scallywag battalion—by his occupation of
the office exemplified the fact that, to be Naval
Officer of Port Jackson, it was not necessary
to be. or even to have been, an officer of the
Royal Navy or the mercantile marine, or any
sort of a sailor whatsoever. What he had
to do when a ship came to Sydney—under
the Port Regulations drawn up by Mr.
Judge-Advocate Wyldo to the order of Gov-
ernor Macquarie—makes it quite evident
that he was expected to earn the pay that
attached to the position.
He had to board every incoming ship—■
they were not numerous in ’20 —and serve
a copv of the regulations upon the com-
mander. The latter was required to furnish
him with every particular as to the ship, her
human complement and her cargo. Then
he took over the mails and sent them ashore.
After that, if all was in order, he permitted
the Admission Flag to be hoisted, and got
himself busy with a report, “on the pre-
scribed form,” to the Governor. You may
gather some notion of his other duties from
the fact that he is expressly mentioned in
17 out of the 51 paragraphs that make up
these quaint and excellent regulations,
whilst at least another third of the total
have to do, in some manner or other, with
liis office. _ ;
So John Piper had no illusion that he
was picnicking when he shoved out from
Sydney Cove that sunny morning a century
ago.
Down between the Sow and Pigs and
South Reef, in the Eastern Channel, Cap-
tain Piper espied the pilot’s boat coming up
behind him out of Watson’s Bay, and bade
his men lie upon their oars until it caught
him up. The pilot was Daniel Fallon, who
had formerly been coxswain of the Naval
Officer’s whaler—a West Country sea-dog of
great merit, entire knowledge of the weather
past, present and future, and a teller of un-
reliable tales regarding the fish which he
caught in the many spare moments that the
not over-worked pilot-service afforded him.
“Good morning to you, Daniel,” said
Piper, with a friendly smile —“who is she !
Can you make out her number from up
above?” He pointed to the flagstaff, where
the “ship in sight” signal still stretched sou’-
west in the nor’-easter, which was already
blowing freshly.
“Not yet, sir—she’m no closer-to than off
Bot’ny Bay. Wi’ this breeze —a-growin’
every half an hour —I’ll gi’ un to nigh fower
o’clock afore she beats up t’ th’ hentrance.
I be a-goin’ t’ h’ist sail, when we’m roun’ th’
corner yarnder, an’ us’ll run down to her
in no time. But ’twill be well into after-
noon afore we’re ’twixt th’ Heads.”
“Oh, very well, Daniel. I’ll wait for
you. We’ve food and drink aboard. We
might do a little fishing. How are they
biting?”
“Oh vamous, y’r Honor. Whv, yestere en,
back yarnder,” he pointed towards \\ ool-
lahra. Point, “I caught”
“Yes. yes, Daniel —I know. The biggest
fi*h that ever was, eh? Well, get along with
you. We’ll run back to the point and await
you. You might anchor off it —’twill save
these lads’ arms —and we’ll attend to the
ship’s business there.”
The pilot boat shot out towards the end
of the curling surf on South Reef. Captain
Piper gave orders to step the mast, and
before long he and his crew had landed for
their mid-day meal upon the narrow isth-
mus where, two generations afterwards,
good Lawrence Hargrave found his traces
of the old-time Spanish ships.
* * * *
In this first month of the year Lachlan
Macquarie was a troubled _ man. Ten years
of autocratic government is alone sufficient
to try the nerves of anyone, but such a
government as Macquarie had to carry on
must have come, in time, to be a mortal
weariness of flesh and spirit.
He had many cares, many enemies —open
and secret —and harassments without end.
But of all his troubles at this time it is
safe to assume that John Thomas Bigge,
Commissioner of Enquiry into the affairs or
New South Wales and its dependencies, was
the very worst and most obnoxious to him.
Mr. Bigge does not come into this story to
any further extent than as being the chief
disturber of the peace of mind of Mrs.
Macquarie’s husband, and the unwitting
cause of that dear lady’s constant insistence,
after her fond fashion, upon taking all the
means at her disposal for counteracting the
wearing effect upon her lord’s health of the
multitudinous worries that beset him. Of
these means, the chief was her gentle insist-
ence upon tearing Macquarie away from his
desk for an occasional jaunt into the country
about Sydney.
To-day, after an early midday meal, his
Excellency and Mrs. Macquarie had ridden
out unattended, in a direction that led them
along the southern shores of the harbor,
skirting the bays, towards South Head.
About the time when Captain Piper, weary-
ing of the ill success that attended his
angling, had landed from his boat, they were
riding through the bush along the rough hill-
top of the wooded point from the extremity
of which he was scrambling up, intent upon
a little exploring expedition of his own.
Down below him his men were still peace-
fully fishing from the white boat.
It wrns a beautiful afternoon —one of those
blue days when the splendid lake looks at its
best. If you go out along Wolseley-road on
a summer afternoon and turn your gaze
northward towards Manly you will behold,
with some difference, the lovely prospect
upon which the Governor and his lady feasted
their eyes as they drew rein upon the brow
of the hill and overlooked the harbor.
The long blue carpet of sunlit waters
stretches away to the white sands of the
Harbor Beach at Manly, with its crest of
Nqrfolk Island pines. To the right runs the
grey rampart of rock that shuts out the long
surges of the Tasman Sea —broken half-way
up bv the slanting entrance and the bold
bluff of Inner North Head. The green cape
of Bradley’s, the wooded crest of George’s
Heights and the sheer extremity of. Middle
Head, crowned by its fort, lie in succession
upon the left. There may be, if the tide is
out. a splash of white foam that flecks the
middle blue, where the Sow and Pigs pierce
the surface. The houses and high pine-trees
in Watson’s Bay, the tall white flats near
Yaucluse and the red roofs of Mosman,
sprinkling the dark forest above Taylor’s
Bay, with, far away in purple haze, the
pleasant village of Manly—that is the pic-
ture to-day.
But it was all the wild emptiness that had
entranced Arthur Phillip when he sailed in
in the Supply thirty-two years before that
the man and woman on the hill looked upon
that afternoon. The sort of scenery you
may see in Broken Bay—never again in
Port Jackson. It was, perhaps, not so lovely
as it is now, but was very lovely indeed.
“My dear Lachlan,” she said, smiling at
him, as they sat upon their horses, “how ex-
quisite—how very beautiful!”
“ ’Tis so, my love—’tis so indeed. Come
—let us dismount and rest awdiile.”
He got off his horse and helped his wife
to alight, holding the skirt of her long habit

of the period clear of her feet and lifting her
from the saddle. The Governor took the
reins of both horses, and Mrs. Macquarie,
gathering her skirts, sat down upon the
ground, altogether innocent of the fact that
she had chosen for a seat the inconspicuous
nest of a colony of the fierce green and
black three-quarter-inch ants which the
small boy of Australia had already named
“soldiers.”
Macquarie’s brow cleared as he looked out
through the trees across the blue waters, and
the frown which for many months had made
harsh the aspect of his rugged face gave
place to. its more natural expression of
rugged dignity. lie drew his tall figure up
and expanded his chest. She smiled up at
him, pleased with the change, and waited
for the oration which she knew would ensue.
“A paradise, my dear Elizabeth —an
entrancing view! Some day, my love, other
eyes than ours will behold the realisation of
our labor, the fruits of our anxieties for the
advancement of this settlement. I foresee,”
he began to prophesy, “a noble city spreading
about these shores. Villas and pleasure gar-
dens, great fleets of shipping—even, may-
hap, steamships after Mr. Fulton’s design
will plough these placid waters, extending
the advantages of commerce and the bless-
ings of civilisation. I look forward to the
time”
“Oh, Lachlan,” she screamed suddenly.
‘Oh, my gracious! Oh, heavens!”
She scrambled to her feet, and danced in
agony, clutching at her dress, and frantically
rubbing at her thighs with both hands.
“Oh, oh, oh!” She emitted a series of
little shrieks. “Lachlan —something is biting
me in a hundred places. Oh, the torture!”
His Excellency, astonished and alarmed,
dropped the bridles and rushed to his wife’s
assistance. She danced in a sort of hysteri-
cal frenzy. He looked down at the ground,
and saw the angry ants swarming about the
flattened crater of their citadel.
“Quick, my love,” he cried, “run down to
those trees. Tear off your clothing. ’Tis
the only way. There is no one here to see
.vou. Quicldy! I will mind the horses, and
keep a look-out. You have been sitting on
an ants’ nest —soldier ants. Run, run—and
shake them from your garments. Quickly!”
Whimpering and in tears, the poor lady
ran down the slope, holding her habit high,
and displaying a pair of white-stockinged
legs. She disappeared in the trees, a little
way down the slope.
It was an oval opening in the white-gum
that she stumbled into, carpeted with green
grass, and shut in by a wall of scrub. Fran-
tically she tore off her clothing, in which the
ants swarmed, and in hardly a minute stood
stripped to her white skin, on which, over
bosoms, thighs and body, the marks left by
the bites of the angry soldiers showed red
and angry. Hastily she shook each separate
garment to free it from her persecutors,
and
Captain John Piper parted the bushes and
walked into the sylvan dressing-room !
Utterly astounded, he stood and stared.
Mrs. Macquarie shrieked, and snatched up a
white garment to hide her naked person.
She was dumb and he was dumb, and they
stared at one another whilst a man might
count five.
“My God!” he muttered, and leapt into
the scrub. A clatter of stones, as he ran
down the hillside blindly, was the sound of
his flight. Mrs. Macquarie sank upon the
heap of clothing.
A fortnight later, in his best uniform, Cap-
tain John Piper presented himself at the
door of Government House, whither he had
been summoned by a little letter in Mrs.
Macquarie’s delicate handwriting.
“Dear Captain Piper,” it went, “I would
be obliged to you exceedingly if you could
find it convenient to call here this after-
noon. His Excellency and I desire to mark
our Great Appreciation of your Silence and
Discretion. —Y’rs. v. sincerely— Elizabeth
Macqitakie.”
The gallant Naval Officer was a little
nervous while he awaited her coming. He
was glad to find that the drawing-room was
unusually darkened, for he felt very capable
of blushing. And so did Mrs. Macquarie,
and that was why the curtains were very
closely drawn.
Silently she entered the room, with a roll
of paper in her hand. Captain Piper stood
up and bowed, and was glad again that the
light was dim.
“Captain Piper,” she faltered. “I—that is
to say Mr. Macquarie and I —would be grati-
fied if you would accept of this paper. I
—we- —cannot thank you too much for your
—for your reserve. Captain Piper—l wish
to say,” she was whispering now, “I want
to say that I consider you—er —to be, Cap-
tain Piper—that you are a Gentleman!”
She thrust the document into his hand,
muttered something that he could not catch,
and fled from the room.
He carried the roll to the window and
drew a curtain back. It was a deed of grant
signed “L. Macquarie,” and conveyed to Cap-
tain John Piper and his heirs 190 acres of
land near Sydney, which included the point
where he and the ants had found Mrs.
Macquarie. On the map accompanying it
the projection was marked “Point Piper.”
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
Apostolic Favors.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THOUGH nobody but himself knew it,
the Apostle had a grievance; nay, a
wound that festered in his soul.
Three nights before that bitter day, while
out prospecting with the black sons of the
Crescent, he had been snared by one of our
patrols —snared like a rabbit. But it was
not that. Lugged off to headquarters, he had
been invited to answer questions that showed
a very nice regard indeed for the Turkish
health. But that was not it. He had been made
then, hateful enough, to busy himself with
the unclean affairs of Christians. But it
was not that either. It was this: Massey,
while passing the enclosure where the shabby,
drop-chin prisoners were wired up, seeing
this fellow with the prophetic beard, and
not knowing that he spoke English as well
as his own father’s son, had made a most
impolite remark. Massey had then forgotten
all about it.
All that day the Apostle had hidden his
anger, nursed it, fed it with much thinking.
While the other prisoners, else unoccupied, had
put their whole mind into getting some cover
up, he had thought of this only. Stretched
with them, later, in a dog’s doze, his thoughts
had not changed. That evening, as they
trod round the wire like sulky animals in a
pound, not one whit cooler because the sun
had dropped, he had still nursed that affront,
still meditated revenge. And though time,
they say, is the best medicine for such a
fever, it had not cured his. It was thus,
though nobody but himself knew it, that the
Apostle had a grievance.
Our men at this time were not precisely
town bucks; and somebody one day sug-
gested that a barber would not rust out wait-
ing for work. After some thought, they
tackled those Turks; and the Apostle, under-
standing perfectly, said, with a smile, that
he was the Heaven-appointed man. He was
installed at once.
He soon became one of the most popular
figures in camp. The Jacks, especially,
thought it good sport to see him working—
this Habakkuk with the great beard (kept
intact now against a certain dark oath), and
the pious jowl that seemed to be ever at the
edge of laughter. With a razor, keen-edged,
yet heavy as a brush-hook, he would go
briskly round the enemy’s gullet; and men
came from his hand as women come from
beauty specialists.
As there was a scheme on foot to help
desertions from the enemy, the' Apostle,
though not informed thereof, was next billed
for a part in it. What the scheme was, any
soldier may guess; but it included, among
other things, an escape by this now thriving
Turk. This, however, in spite of all the
ingenious arrangements to help it off, he
stubbornly refused; whether because his
fare at the cook’s quarters was too sump-
tuous, or because his tobacco cost nothing, or
because he was now pushing a stately trade
as a middleman in contraband rations—in
eggs, tinned fish and chocolate, smuggled in,
at enormous profit, from Imbros and God
knows where —whether because of any of
these things or all of them, none
c6uld tell. And well indeed might these
reasons have sufficed to hold a smaller man;
but the Apostle, you will remember, though
nobody but himself knew it, had a grievance.
One day Massey turned up. Being em-
ployed at another part of the line, he had
not been this way lately.
The Apostle, just then in the midst of a
triumphant advance through the entangle-
ments on Todd’s chin, gave no sign; but if
his client had not been so absorbed in some-
thing else—to wit, in thinkinsr of his new
boots, secured only after a brilliant piece of
strategy—he might have noticed that the
artist in hair sho<ik visibly' at the jester’s
approach. IN either did his eyes fill with the
caressing fires of affection; they went hard.
But any momentary hitch in his internal
mechanism was soon put right; and the
Apostle, bland enough, and with his heart
well up his sleeve, waited for what the gods
should appoint.
Massey pulled up at the entrance to the
saloon, a shallo v recess covered with a square
of canvas, thrown over a rough tee in front,
and pinned to the rearward cliff with a
couide of old bayonets.
“Strike me!” he said, “there’s a good
length of sewing-silk hanging from this black
ram. Why don’t some of you cows shear the
blighter? In this state he’s not fit to handle
a Christian.”
“Not so,” replied Hawkins, next in the
queue. “An Apostle without whiskers? As
soon talk of a milker without teats. He’s
all right.”
Massey grinned.
“A thought strikes me,” he said. “When
this brush combs out to another finger or two,
you mean to twist it up into a rope, and hang
the old tup in his own fibre. That’s it!
Well, I’ll help.”
All through this the knight of the razor,
applying himself to his job, still gave no
sign. Then, looking at the hard-mouthed
jester, he said slowly:
“Allah knows all; and all is in the hand of
Allah.”
“All is in the hand of Allah,” drawled a
long Queenslander, parroting the Turk. “All,
mates, is in the hand of Allah. Even the
cleaning up of your dirty lugs—Allah has a
hand in it.”
The crowd grinned; and then somebody,
turning to the newcomer, said :
“You’d stand a bit of a clean-up yourself,
matey. If you’re pushed for time, take the
chair next.”
Todd got up; and Massey sat down and
threw his head back on to the rest. The
Apostle lathered the hard brown face, took
up the razor and tried the edge. Yes, it
was keen enough. Then, holding the sol-
dier’s head firmly with his left hand, and
marking the right spot on the long neck,
open for the operation, he gave thanks to his
god, and lifted the sharp tool.
“Allah”
But the Apostle, quick as he was, was not
quick enough. Taking a heavy right from
the Queenslander, he saw numerous fine and
very sudden stars, and dropped in a heap.
Massey, with his neck leaking like a stuck
pig’s, jumped from the box.
“What the blue fire is this!” he said. And
feeling at his neck, he brought his hand away
dripping with blood.
“This,” said the Queenslander, “is two
hospital jobs—and the last of a good coat.
I watched the cow. He had a queer look;
and no man ever started a square shave with
the blade at that angle.”
Then, looking at his mate’s neck, he said:
“Yes. Bill, I think I stopped the blighter iu
time.”
“Well,” said Massey, tying his neck up in
a strip of somebody’s shirt, “that’s the last
time I’ll lend my tender neck-piece to Abdul.
But what an old sport!”
Massey got a new tunic: and the Apostle,
when the boat next called for prisoners, was
wafted, as some feeling and comprehending
poet puts it, to lands afar.

SOMETHING
BETTER!
If Engineers and Steam-plant Owners would only
realise the importance of EFFICIENTLY insulating
their Boilers, Steam Pipes, etc., more attention might
be paid to this particular matter. Great economies
can be effected in this direction, in some cases the coal
consumption being reduced as much as 50%. But the
insulating material must be of the very best.
Carbonate of Magnesia is to-day recognised as the best
non-conductor of lieat. Newalts Coverings are com-
posed of 55% of this material, and 15% Asbestos—the
Asbestos being used for binding purposes only—and in
addition possess innumerable advantages over all other
forms of lagging. Newall’s Coverings are used, practi-
cally exclusively, by the British Admiralty, N..S.W.
and Victorian Governments, and have been used
throughout the Ultimo. White Bay, Melbourne. Perth
and Canberra Power Stations.

'isfep 2-Stand Shearing Plant
The Outfit that “Swept the Poll” in the 1917 Season.
F. D. McMASXER, Esq.., Dalkeith, Cassilis, writes, under date 24th January:
“I have pleasure in stating- that I have given both of the 2 Stand Portable
Crutching Plants I purchased from you an exhaustive trial, and with the
utmost satisfaction. They are handy to move about, economical to run, and
the Fairbanks Morse Engine is simplicity itself. Crutching at the different
sheep-yards is, in my opinion, a far better method than doing the work at
the woolshed, as the saving for the lambing ewes in driving alone is very
considerable.”

DESTRUCTORS.
The Advantages of the Destructor Method of dealing with Trade Waste, Hotel, Factory and
Household Garbage and Municipal Rubbish have long been appreciated.
We cater for this class of requirement with our “GUTHRIDG” Type—
No. 1 ... Capacity 3cwt. per 8 hours ... £lO 10s.
No. 3 ... Capacity Bcwt. per 8 hours ... £l7 10s.
No. 4 ... Capacity 20cwt. per 8 hours ... £32 10s.
Hundreds of these have been sold to Schools, Hospitals, Factories and the State and Federal Governments.
They are solidly constructed of Cast Iron and lined with fire brick, and destroy even damp garbage without smoke or smell.
The System has now been extended to the Home by the introduction of the “Household” Destructor, Price £2 2s.
This is on sale at Messrs. Nock & Kirby’s, George Street, and Alex. Smith & Sons, Pitt Street, Sydney.
